# Complete Guide to Tableau for Data Scientists

> If your work requires any sort of graphical visualization of data, chances are you’ve run into Tableau. If you’ve been using Tableau but want to learn how to really harness its full power for data science, join expert Matt Francis in this course as he shows you how to take your skills to the next level. Matt starts with one of the most important features in Tableau: the difference between the gree

### Introduction

#### [The power of Tableau for data scientists](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/the-power-of-tableau-for-data-scientists?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/the-power-of-tableau-for-data-scientists?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Matt] Welcome to the Complete Guide to [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md). My name's Matt Francis, a two-time Tableau Visionary and a Tableau Social Ambassador. I'm going to show you everything you need to know about Tableau. We're going to start with connecting to data and then combining that data to make robust data sources. Once we've got that, we're going to learn how to analyze and visualize our data. That's so that we can explain what's happening behind those numbers. Ultimately we need to share those insights and those answers so we can see, understand, and act on data. I'm super excited to have you along on this journey, so grab your Tableau, grab some data, and welcome to the Complete Guide to Tableau.

#### [What you should know](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-you-should-know?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Here's what you need to know to get the very best out of this course. Now, if you're a beginner, we're going to assume that you have zero knowledge of [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md). We're going to start by taking a tour of the user interface, so you'll know exactly what I'm talking about when I tell you to click in certain places. We'll talk about connecting to data and actually bringing your data into Tableau before we even start building any visualizations. If in intermediate, you're going to learn things that aren't in other courses. We're going to spend a lot of time talking about blue and green fields and the difference between discrete and continuous measures. This is something that you very rarely see anybody else teaching, but this is going to give you an insight into Tableau to understand exactly what goes on behind the hood. And if you're self-taught, well, you are going to learn the right way to do things. One of the things about Tableau is it rewards experimentation. You might not have actually been on a course before, but you've been able to build something by dragging and dropping in kind of getting the results, but maybe you don't understand why and how that happened. This course is going to get rid of all of that for you, and you'll understand exactly what to do and when to do it. So what do you need to have? Well, you need a copy of Tableau, and there's two options you can go for. You can have Tableau Desktop or Tableau Desktop Public Edition, which is also just referred to as Tableau Public. Now, what are the difference between these two options? Well, Tableau Desktop is the fully paid for license. However, there is a 14-day free demo that has all of the options open to you.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=93)** Tableau Desktop can connect to dozens of different data sources, including web connections, [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md) such as Oracle, PSQL, [MySQL](../../Skills/Software%20Development/MySQL.md), and it can publish to public.[tableau.com](https://tableau.com). But you can also publish to a local Tableau server, maybe within your organization. Now, Tableau Public, on the other hand, is totally free. The only limitation is you can only connect to files or something like [Google](../../Glossary/Service/Google.md) Sheets. The local files need to be something like an [Excel](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Microsoft%20Excel.md), a CSV, a PDF file, something that exists on your local machine. You can't connect to remote databases. In the latest version of Tableau Public, you can save locally to your local machine, or you have to publish to public.[tableau.com](https://tableau.com). But when you do that, you can make those files private so you are able to practice publish to the web to see how it looks without having to make it available to absolutely everybody. To get to Tableau Desktop, go to [tableau.com](https://tableau.com) and look for the Tableau Desktop Download. You'll have to register as a user, but the 14-day free trial will start as soon as you register it. Alternatively, you can go to public.[tableau.com](https://tableau.com), register for an account there and download Tableau Public. Remember, this is the free version. So, what else are you going to need? Well, we're going to need data. I would always recommend
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=185)** that you save your data files for this course in this directory. When you install Tableau, Tableau creates a folder within your My Document area called My Tableau Repository. Inside that, it's going to create a load of sub-folders. I would always say, save your data sources within the Data Sources folder, and any workbooks you create, save them within the Workbooks folder. These are the default location that Tableau will look for when it tries to open those up for you. So stick with the defaults and it's just a lot easier to do. Okay, so you've got your copy of Tableau, you've got your data where it needs to be, let's get on with the course.

#### [Tableau UI tour](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/tableau-ui-tour?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/tableau-ui-tour?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] This video, we're going to do a quick tour of the user interface, so that later on in the course when I tell you to do something, you'll know exactly where to go. Now, when you first open up [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md), this might be your starting screen. In the middle, there's a big white space at the moment, but this is where all of your previously opened and saved workbooks are going to appear. So that's going to show your recent history. You'll be able to click on one of those, and then that will open up the previous workbook. On the left-hand side is the Data Connection pane. This is what we're going to use to connect to some data. It lists the type of data we can connect to, whether it's a file or whether it's on a server. If you're using Tableau Public, it would also include things like [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md) as well. On the right-hand side, there's an Information pane. This puts updates from Tableau and useful bits of information, things like product updates and that sort of thing. If you've already opened up a existing workbook, your starting view is probably going to look something quite different. We can toggle between this start pane and the interface by clicking on the Tableau icon at the top left of the screen. This is the user interface that we're going to be spending a lot of time in. It's broken down into four different areas. The first one we have is the Data pane on the left-hand side. This lists all of the fields that are present in our data source.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/tableau-ui-tour?u=76281980&t=94)** Our current data source is called Orders, and it comes from the sample Superstore which comes with Tableau Public. The data is organized into dimensions and measures at the bottom. Measures are things that we are going to aggregate together: the sum of sales or the average profit. Dimensions are what we use to break those values down. So if I went to look at the summer sales per category, I'd be using the category field and the sales field to produce that visualization. And in order to create anything, what we do is we drag fields out of our Data pane and onto the Row and Column shelf at the top of the interface. Now, the combination of whether it is a green field, a blue field, a measure, a dimension, and how many we put in there, is going to determine the type of visualization that we create. So for example, a simple bar chart might be something like sales on the rows, and then category on the columns. I now have three bars, one for each category, and the length of the bar is determined by the sum of sales. The more fields that I place on the row and column, the different kind of visualization we're going to produce. So the Row and the Column shelf determines the type of visualization that you get. There are certain rules that Tableau uses, which we'll look at in further videos. Tableau works by plotting the data as a mark in the view.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/tableau-ui-tour?u=76281980&t=189)** The view is this big white area. It's also sometimes referred to as a canvas. This is where Tableau's going to create the visualization. Now, each of these bars is technically called a mark. You can think of it as a mark on a page. The type of chart, remember, is determined by what is on the row and the column. But how the mark look is determined by the Marks card, which sits in between the Data pane and the view. Tableau will automatically try to pick the correct type of mark to show based on what you've put on the row and the column. In this case, Tableau's figured out that for the combination of category and sum of sales, the best data visualization to use is a bar chart. But we have full control over what each one of those bars look like. I could change the colors of the bar. Maybe I don't like blue. I want them all to be orange. I have the power to do that. I could, if I wanted to, actually use the values of one of the fields. If I was to put category onto the color, now it's color-coded for each one of the categories. I could put sales onto color, and now it's color-coded by the value of the sales. I can change the size of the marks using the Size card. I can make them thin. I can make them fat. We can turn on labels by click on the Label card and so on. So the Marks card is ways that we change
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/tableau-ui-tour?u=76281980&t=284)** what the marks look like. Now, inevitably, we're going to have too much data that we're interested in. You've got access to tens of thousands, hundreds of thousands, millions of records. You need a way of filtering that. We do that using the Filter card. We can drag dimensions and measures onto the Filter card to produce a filter to include or exclude values that we want. If I was to put category onto filter, I get a dialog box that allows me to tick on or tick off certain categories. So for example, say I'm not interested in technology, I can turn that off. Hit Apply. And now I have a filter that only shows me the remaining two categories. So those are the main areas we're going to be looking at throughout this entire course. We have the Data pane on the left, which has all of the values in your data source divided into dimensions at the top, measures at the bottom. We filter the data from the Data pane through the Filter shelf. We can have as many measures of dimensions we want on there, and that will then include or exclude data to appear in our view. To build the view, we put measures and dimensions onto our rows and columns. We can move these around to change what the visualization looks like and what type of visualization that we get. The marks themselves are controlled by the Marks card. We can change the color, the size, the shape. We can change the labels, we can change the tooltips.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/tableau-ui-tour?u=76281980&t=378)** The Marks card changes how each market is represented within the entire field. You are going to get a lot of practice moving around this user interface, but it's a good idea to know where to start.

#### [Tableau order of operations](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/tableau-order-of-operations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/tableau-order-of-operations?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In order to understand how and when [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) does something, we need to think about Tableau's order of operations. Now, this diagram shows the order of operations that Tableau does. Starting at the top and moving towards the bottom is the order in which Tableau's going to perform these functions. Now, it's important to understand this because some of the behavior that Tableau does could be influenced by some of the other things that we do. And understanding the order in which these are going to be applied can ensure that we get the correct analysis, the correct visualization, and the correct answers from our data. So the first thing that Tableau does when it analyzes the data to produce any view that you create is look at filtering. Filtering is the removal or inclusion of data that we define. And there's a couple of different sorts of filtering that we can do. And the first filtering that Tableau will do in its order of operations is extract filters. When you create an extract, that is, you take an offline copy of your data, you can apply a filter to that extract. So only the filter data from your main primary data source is included within that filter. Secondly, you can then have data source filters. So these are filters that are contained within the data source itself. So even before you get to your view where you start with your rows and your columns in your data pane, the data is already being filtered out. The third type of filter is called a context filter. This is when you take one of your measures or dimensions
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/tableau-order-of-operations?u=76281980&t=94)** and you say to Tableau, "Apply this one before you apply any of the others in the filter shelf." Next comes a set of four different conditions. We have the condition filter, FIXED LODs, sets, and Top N filters. These are going to be applied after the main data source filters. Next we have dimension filters. So these will be dimensions that you'll drag and drop from your data pane onto your filter shelf, things like the customer state or the order ID or maybe the region. Next, you have things like data blending and EXCLUDE LODs and INCLUDE LODs. Data blending is a method of combining two data sources together within the view rather than the data source. So this happens after the data source has been created, after all the filtering is done, the new blender results of two or more data sources together. Measure filters are when we apply a measure to a filter. Maybe it's the sum of sales or the average profit. But it's important to note that these filters happen after the dimension filters. So if we are relying on a value of the measure to be able to filter on it, that's going to depend on what's been filtered out by the dimension filter. A filtering in whatever method it occurs is removing or including values that you can later use in your view. After that's happened, Tableau's going to create your view, and that will depend
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/tableau-order-of-operations?u=76281980&t=186)** on what you've put on your row or your columns. And that's going to be things like your bar charts, your line charts. After you've done that, you can then do secondary things to that view. For example, you could use the clustering or forecast to make some analysis of your data. You could do table calculations like running sums or percent of differences. You can apply totals, say, to a cross tab. These happen after your visualization has been created because they depend on what's in the view. It then makes sense that table calculation filters have to happen after the table calculation has been created. And then finally, after we've filtered our data, we've created our view, we've applied the cluster and the forecast in the table calculations, we then can literally draw on top of that view things like reference lines or trend lines. Every time you do something within the Tableau user interface, Tableau's going to have to go through this entire list of operations every single time just to check that nothing has changed. That the behavior that happens with the Tableau could be unexpected because you haven't taken into account the order of these operations. Understand this, and you have a much better understanding of why Tableau's behave in the way it is, and that way, you can get it to do what you want.

### 1. Green and Blue Fields, What Do They Mean?

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understand the difference between green and blue fields](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/understand-the-difference-between-green-and-blue-fields?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/understand-the-difference-between-green-and-blue-fields?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] To really understand what [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md)'s doing, it's essential to understand the difference between blue and green. Now, what do we mean by blue and green? Well, let's look at a typical user interface. We can see we have some green fields, we have some blue fields. If we look at our rows and column shelves at the top, we have the sum of sales in green and the subcategory in blue. On the Marks card, again, we've got a category in blue, some of sales in green. And when we look at our data pane on the left hand side, we can see all the icons for our measures and dimensions are colored green and blue. Now, that's not just to look pretty. Those differences are really, really important. And what do they represent? Now, for a lot of people, they make the assumption that it's to do with whether it's dimensions or measures. And if you look at the interface, you can see how Tableau groups, all the dimensions in blue, all the measures in green, but actually that's not what the color coding means. The color coding is actually to do with whether it's discrete or continuous. So what is discrete and continuous data and what does that mean for us? Well, first we need to define what discrete data is and what continuous data is. So discrete data can only have certain values. There's only a finite set of those values that the discrete data can have, and typically you can count those values. So it's kind of data that you can count up. Now in some cases, but not all,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/understand-the-difference-between-green-and-blue-fields?u=76281980&t=93)** discrete data will have a logical sequence. So for example, if you were talking about temperature, you might have cold, mild, warm, hot. There's a certain logical sequence to it that we assign. On the other hand, continuous data has a whole range of values, and in actual fact, there's an infinite set of values within a range. You can get smaller and smaller definitions of a continuous range by more high precision. Continuous values can't be counted, but they can be aggregated together. Now, that's kind of a broad definition of those two. But to understand it, let's think of a data set that everybody's familiar with. Then there's lots of things we could measure about the human body. And if we took a collection of people, either in the workplace or a school, or just a general population, there's lots of things we could measure about the human body and we get lots and lots of different data. If then we were to go through each of those datas and then categorize 'em as either discrete or continuous, how would that occur? What would happen? So let's start off by looking at the number of limbs. So if we went along and counted how many limbs everybody has. Now there's a finite number of limbs that it's possible to have. So we record how many limbs each of our persons have. And that's a good example of what would be discrete data. There's a finite number
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/understand-the-difference-between-green-and-blue-fields?u=76281980&t=185)** and every single person is going to have either this many, this many or this many. What about ages? So again, we could go and ask everybody's age. Now, depending on the degree of accuracy we do with somebody's age, maybe it's how many years or years and months or numbers of days or numbers of hours, there is a range of values that people could have. And it could be a small range or it could be a large range, depending on the population we decided to ask. But the ages would all fit somewhere on a scale between the oldest and the youngest. Or what about heights? Again, you could line everybody up and measure their heights. And again, depending on the precision that we do, everybody's going to be on somewhere of a range between the shortest to the tallest, and everybody else would fit somewhere within there, depending on the precision. Eye color. Now that's an interesting one. If we looked at everybody's eyes and looked at the color of those eyes, what would they be? Well, even though there's a lot of variation, we tend to categorize those as something like blue, green, brown, hazel, gray, gray-blue, and then kind of variations on that. But there's still only a finite number. And when you think about it, there's probably not a huge amount. So typically, we'd think of eye color as being discrete. Hair length, well, much like height, we could go to everybody, can measure how long everybody's hair was. There'd be some people with no hair
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/understand-the-difference-between-green-and-blue-fields?u=76281980&t=278)** to some people with long hair, and everybody else's hair length would fit somewhere within that range. But when you think about it, how do you describe your hair? I mean, you don't describe it as, "My hair is 20 centimeters long." You describe it as shoulder length or medium or long or short, or none if you're bald. So actually hair length could be described as being discrete. Well, actually, what about height and age? Those ones could also fit in there as well, because people, if you were to describe somebody, they don't tend to describe themselves in terms of the numbers of centimeters or meters or feet, inches tall they are. They might say they're a short person, they're a tall person. Same with age. It could be young or middle age or elderly. There are certain data types that fit discrete or continuous really neatly. Then there are other ones that kind of straddle the two. And in that case, it depends on the use case in which we're looking at. As we'll see with Tableau, Tableau treats discrete and continuous data very, very differently. If we apply discrete values to color, we get discrete colors. If we put continuous data on, we have a range of colors. Understanding what Tableau does with these data is going to enable us to build exactly the right visualization that we want.
>
> **[6:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/understand-the-difference-between-green-and-blue-fields?u=76281980&t=371)** So to carry on this, check out some of the other videos where into more detail about how Tableau treats discrete and continuous data with all aspects of the user interface. Remember, some data is very much discrete, some is very much continuous, but there is some data that can switch between the two, and which one you'd use? Well, it depends.

#### [How do green and blue fields affect rows and columns?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-green-and-blue-fields-affect-rows-and-columns?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-green-and-blue-fields-affect-rows-and-columns?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's see how blue and green fields affect rows and columns. So all of the data in our data pane is either blue or green, which is either a discreet or a continuous field. Our continuous fields tend to be our measures. They're the things we can aggregate together, like the sum of sales or the average profit and the dimensions are what we're going to use to break those values down. Then when we add one of these fields to either the row or the column, [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) is going to behave, depending on whether it's discreet or continuous, that behavior is going to drive the type of chart that we get. And understanding how tablet's going to interpret the data field, discreet or continuous, will determine the type of chart that we have. And if we know that, we can basically build any chart that we'd like. So there are two rules that we have to bear in mind when we think about measures, dimensions, discreet, continuous, and the rows and the columns. The first one is that for every continuous field, tableau will create an axis. It'll create an axis, and then it will plot the aggregated value of that field somewhere along that axis. So let's do that in action. So if I take my sales from my data pane and drop it onto the column shelf, Tableau creates an axis and plots the total sum of sales 'cause that's a default aggregate for our measure. And plots the length of that bar
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-green-and-blue-fields-affect-rows-and-columns?u=76281980&t=94)** corresponding to that value. So the total sales that we have in our particular data set is 2.3 million. Now, that is a bar chart. It's not a very exciting bar chart, admittedly, it's got one single bar. But the rule of a continuous measure means we have one axis for each measure on our column. So if we added a second measure, so if I bring out profit and I drop that onto the column, we now have two continuous measures on my column. Some are sales, some are profit, using the default aggregation again, and I have two axes, one for the sales and one for the profit. For every green field that you put on the row or the column, you get an axis. Now, the next step normally with a bar chart is you want to have multiple bars for each value okay? So for that we have to add our dimension. The dimension is going to take that single bar and break it down into multiple bars for each value within that particular dimension. So for example, if I was to drag region, which is a discreet field, and put it onto the rows, we get the blue field on the row. And what this does is create a header. It creates the header for the region, and then displays all of the values underneath. Now in this case, for region I have four regions, central, east, south, and west.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-green-and-blue-fields-affect-rows-and-columns?u=76281980&t=187)** So I have four bars for the summer sales and four bars for the summer profit. So the rule is for every blue field, for every discreet field on a row, we create a header with one row for each of the values in that particular dimension. If I was to add another dimension, Tableau's then going to create a bar for each one of the dimensions that I add. So for example, at the moment I've just got region, which has only got the four. If I added category to the row, put that to the right of region, I now get one bar for each category and then for each region. So in total, I've now got 24 bars, one for the sales, one for the profit, for each category, and each region. As we add more and more dimensions, we're going to break that bar down into smaller and smaller segments. And we don't just have to add extra dimensions to our rows. We could have put them on the columns. And depending on the orientation of rows and columns, you're going to get a different looking chart. So for my example here, if I took category off of the row and put it onto the column, I've still got the same number of marks. I've still got my 24 bars, but now they're arranged slightly differently in this orientation. It makes it easier to compare the category of the product across the different regions. So for example, I can look at furniture
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-green-and-blue-fields-affect-rows-and-columns?u=76281980&t=281)** and I can see that the west is my highest region for furniture, and south is the lowest. Now, in this view, it makes it harder to compare across the categories, because the bars aren't lined up in that way. But all I can do is I can put a category onto the row and then I could move region onto the column. And we've still got exactly the same number of bars, still got our 24 marks, and they're still exactly the same size, but we've changed the orientation. Now it's a lot easier to compare across the region. So for my central region, I can then see which one of my categories is top. So the combination of measures and dimensions has two different effects. For every green field, for every continuous field, we're going to get an axis. And for every discreet field, a blue field, we're going to get a header with one row for each one of the values within that dimension. And then whether we put them on the row or the column, or all the columns or on the rows, whatever combination, it enables us to create the chart that's going to answer our question most effectively.

#### [How do green and blue fields affect filters?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-green-and-blue-fields-affect-filters?u=76281980)

But more often than not, you're not interested in every single record. You're only interested in a subset, maybe a particularly year or a month, or the most recent date, maybe a particular category or particular person. #### [How do green and blue fields affect colors?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-green-and-blue-fields-affect-colours?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-green-and-blue-fields-affect-colours?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Probably the most powerful tool we have in our data fields toolset is color. Color is one of the very first things you notice when you see a data visualization. It could be used to draw the eye to something interesting. It can highlight outliers. Because it's so powerful, we need to understand how [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) uses color in order to best use it. Now, Tableau has two ways of handling color, and that depends on whether we are using a discreet or a continuous field to apply that color. So we're going to look at both of those so you can understand how it works, and to make the best decision on how to use color. Let's start by looking at continuous color. So in this view, I've got summer sales on my column and category and subcategory on my rows, and it creates this bar chart. Now, the length of the bar denotes the size of the sales. Now, if I wanted to emphasize that a little bit more, I could use color. Maybe I want to color the bars based on the value of the sum of sales. So to do that, I'm going to drag sales out of my Data pane and drop it onto Color. Now, sales is a continuous field. It's a green field. When I put that onto Color, Tableau uses a continuous color range. It sets the lowest value to be the lightest color and the highest value to be the darkest color. So in my example, we can see on the right-hand side on the legend, I have 8,500, that's my lowest value,
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-green-and-blue-fields-affect-colours?u=76281980&t=94)** and 335,000 as my highest value of sales, and also the deepest color. So I can see that now all of my bars are colored accordingly. Now, is this a good use of color? Maybe, maybe not. We're kind of doubling encoding because the value of the summer sales is already encoded in the length of the bar. So using color to color it as well is possibly not the best choice of this really powerful tool. So I could have, if I wanted to, used a different measure, maybe profit. So if I drag Profit onto Color instead, it's going to replace the summer sales. And now my bars are colored by the sum of profit. And if it makes a loss, is orange, and if it makes a profit, is blue. So in this example, we can see that tables and bookcases make losses, as does supplies and things like fasteners. There might be a little, small profit there, but nothing major. By adding that color, it enables us to answer different questions from that bar chart. It allows us to identify the highest and the lowest values. Is that the best choice for color? It kind of depends on what it is you're trying to do. Now, the other way we can use color is by you putting a discreet field onto the color. So let's do that with category. If I take my Category out, drag and drop it onto Color, we now color-coded the bars based on their parent category. I've got three categories:
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-green-and-blue-fields-affect-colours?u=76281980&t=188)** furniture, [office](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Microsoft%20Office.md) supplies, technology. So I use three colors. Now, instead of the color encoding the value of the sales, it's now encoding the type of product that it is. So it's easier to kind of visually group those together to show that those four at the top, my furniture, are a different category to the office supplies and technology. It hasn't changed the order at all, but it has made it much clearer that there are three distinct groups of bars here. Now, I could have, if I wanted to, color it by the subcategory instead. If I put Color on Subcategory, now each of my subcategories has their own individual color. Is this a good choice of color in this case? Probably not. There's too many colors to really make any kind of useful information about this data. There's just too many colors. So in this case, there are too many dimensions. There's too many values in here to make good use of that color. Once you start getting above six or so colors, it starts to get a little bit more difficult to see anything clearly. So adding color is actually a distraction. Let's put my Category back onto Color. And now my bars are color-coded by their parent category. And much like we saw with the continuous measure, When I put profit on, I can color this by a value that's not part of my view.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-green-and-blue-fields-affect-colours?u=76281980&t=282)** So for example, if I was to put Region onto Color, my bars are now colored depending on whether it's the Central, East, South, or West region, broken down by the furniture and the subcategory. So now I've got 68 individual bars here, some of which are stacked together depending on the category and subcategory. So I can then see the breakdown of chair sales, for example, broken down by each one of those four regions. Is this a good choice of color? It depends. It depends on what you're trying to understand and what you're trying to do. Now, similarly, we can see the same behavior if we were to look at say a line chart. So in here I've got a continuous date field and continuous sum of sales. If I was to color-code this by say category, if I put Category on the Color, I get three discreet lines because I have three discreet values in that category: furniture, office supplies, and technology. So I can look and see, over time, does one of these categories outperform the other? Is there the same kind of seasonal variation? Or I could color it by a measure. I could put Sales onto Color. So again, the line is darker for the higher sales, lower for the lower sales. Or as we did with the bar, I could put the profit so I could see how the sales improved over time. I can see if the profit also improved alongside.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-green-and-blue-fields-affect-colours?u=76281980&t=378)** Color is an incredibly useful tool, but we have to understand how Tableau's going to apply that color. Do we want to apply it in a discreet way or in a continuous way? What's correct? Well, it depends.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

#### [How do green and blue fields affect dates?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-green-and-blue-fields-affect-dates?u=76281980)

And it's because they exist in this kind of middle ground between discreet and continuous. They're kind of not one or the other. They're either discreet or continuous depending on what type of data you're looking at and what kind of question you want to answer from that data. [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) treats dates in a very different way depending on whether you're looking in them as a continuous date or a discreet date. And it makes some questions a lot easy to answer one way rather than the other. So look at that. First of all, let's look at dates in a continuous way. First we're going to create a timeline. So to do that, I need a measure and a continuous date. So I'm going to start by putting Sales onto my row. This creates a single column, the total value of sales. > **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-green-and-blue-fields-affect-dates?u=76281980&t=95)** which we'll come to in a second, but the part I'm interested in are the ones at the bottom. > **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-green-and-blue-fields-affect-dates?u=76281980&t=280)** That's September for all of the years in my dataset. If I want to look at a particular year, I need to bring out another copy of Order Date. So what I can then see is for a particular year, say 2021, I can look at all of the sales for a given month. Now this makes it easy to answer the question which month had the highest sales. For that, we need to flip the orientation around. So I put month onto the row and then put year onto the column, and now I'm looking at each month across so I can then compare the growth for January across each of those years. Viewing the data in a continuous way or a discreet way makes it easier to answer some questions and harder for others. It's always worth, particularly with dates,

#### [How to clean Excel data with the Data Interpreter](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-clean-excel-data-with-the-data-interpreter?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-clean-excel-data-with-the-data-interpreter?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) always works best with a nice, clean table of data, but unfortunately, that's not always what we get. In this video, we're going to look at how we can use the built-in Data Interpreter to clean up an [Excel](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Microsoft%20Excel.md) file. At the top, we have this header called New Pc Registrations Or Sales, and underneath the header, we have a subheader explaining what the table is showing. Now, this kind of structure for an Excel table is incredibly common because what happens is people put extra text in there
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-clean-excel-data-with-the-data-interpreter?u=76281980&t=96)** to help a human make sense of that data. Now, unfortunately, Tableau doesn't know that because Tableau isn't human. What Tableau's expecting is a nice, clean table of data. Now what we need to do is say to Tableau, "Well, ignore everything else. Just concentrate on that table." That's only the bit that we're interested in. That's where the data actually is. Now, to do that, we'll go back into Tableau, and on the left hand side, under the Sheets, it says the Data Interpreter. So we're going to click that button. What Tableau now does is analyze our sheet, looking for table-type structures that it can discover. Now we've clicked that, we can then see that now, we have all of our headings of actually the correct headings that we expected. We still have one null column in here because although Tableau's identified the [rest](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Representational%20State%20Transfer%20(REST).md) of the table, it wasn't quite sure what to do with this one. So rather than get rid of it, it shows it in there. But we can scroll down and see, actually, that does look like it's empty, so I could go ahead and click on the caret, click on Hide, and just get rid of that column that I'm not interested in. Now I've got my data, we could go ahead and build our visualization. Now, in this case, it looks like it's done a good job, but I might want to check that. Well, Tableau helps us do that. Underneath the Check button, there is a Preview the results link, and when we click on this, Tableau will generate a new Excel file based on the interpretation of its data.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-clean-excel-data-with-the-data-interpreter?u=76281980&t=191)** The first tab is a key to understand the results, and on the next tab, it shows the original table, but color-codes it based on the type of data that it found. So the orange table at the top in my view, is the header, and then all the other columns, it's named as Data. So I can correctly check that Tableau's picked the right set of data to actually pull in. Now, once we've done that, and we start going to our sheets, we can then see that we have all of our year names and our regions and countries. So from this point on, Tableau's just treating this as a table of data because that's what it's done for us. What this means is that now we can utilize Excel sheets or [Google](../../Glossary/Service/Google.md) Sheets where people have tried to be helpful to other humans by putting header and footer text. If there were two or more table structures in that data, Tableau would also be able to identify those for us as well. This unlocks a huge amount of data that otherwise would be quite difficult to get into. Tableau.

#### [How to connect to Google Sheets](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-connect-to-google-sheets?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-connect-to-google-sheets?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] [Google](../../Glossary/Service/Google.md) Sheets are becoming an increasingly popular way of drawing tables of data. It has all the advantages of [Excel](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Microsoft%20Excel.md) in that it's easy to use, but because it's in the cloud, it's also available anywhere where you have a connection. Plus, it's really good for collaborating. Now, [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) can easily connect to a Google Sheet and use it for the basis of a dashboard. The advantage of which is that if the data changes on the Google Sheet, the published dashboard is automatically going to update it so if you don't have to republish the dashboard every time. So let's look at a table in Google Sheets and see how we bring it into Tableau. So I have a table here that's looking at my fantasy football data for 2024, and I want to bring this in so I can look at how my weeks did, what my overall rank was, and so on. So the first thing I have to do is tell Tableau that I want to connect to a Google Sheet. So we'll go back into Tableau and I'm going to click on Connect to Data and I'm going to set the [Google Drive](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Google%20Drive.md) connector. The next thing Tableau's going to do is pop up a browser and it's going to ask me to authenticate my Google account with Tableau. This is required so that Tableau has permissions to access the drive and any sheets that are within it. So I'm going to choose the account that I want to connect and I need to sign in and then authorize Tableau to have access to my Google Drive. Once that's done, we can close that window down and I can see all of the Google Sheets
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-connect-to-google-sheets?u=76281980&t=95)** that are available in both my drive, any that are shared with, me and any shared drives. Now I know my file is called FPL 2024, so I can put that in the search window and it's found the sheet for me. I can double click on the sheet. Then Tableau then opens up that sheet, looks for any sheets within the Google Sheet itself, and it's found two here, and the one I want is the Ugly FC one, so I'm going to drag that into my Data pane. And it successfully has picked up all of the rows and columns that was in the sheet that we saw a moment ago. I can go to Sheet 1 and then I can do some analysis. So I can see that I started off about 500k, got into the top 90k, went back down, so it was a bit of an up and down season all over. Now the really good thing about Google Sheets is if there are changes made to the Google Sheet, this is going to permeate through into this dashboard. So if I publish this dashboard somewhere, that data's going to get pulled through, I don't have to republish it. Now, there is a slight downside to that
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-connect-to-google-sheets?u=76281980&t=188)** that we have to be aware of. The real benefit of Google Sheets is the fact that you can have multiple people working on them or updating them. Now you have to be really careful. It's okay to add rows or remove rows. Tableau expects data to grow or data to shrink, but it doesn't expect column heading names to change. If they do and we've used them in the dashboard, that's going to break our dashboard. So let's see that in action. So one of the columns here is called Team Value, so this is how much all the players in my team added up to. Now I maybe want to track that 'cause I want to see did my trades pay off or not? So if I put Team Value onto my row and I could create another line chart for that one. So change it into a line and I can edit the axis, tell it not to include 0, and then I can see how my team value changed over time. Now say later on, I actually thought, "Well, I'm going to change this Team Value column name here, I want to put a currency in there as well to indicate that this is actually money." So I might come to my header and change it and put pound sign in. Now that's perfectly fine to do within the context of Google Sheet, but the Google Sheet is now being used as the source of some data. So imagine this was a collaborative document and somebody else has changed the column name without you knowing.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-connect-to-google-sheets?u=76281980&t=281)** If then go back into my dashboard, right-click on the data source name, hit Refresh and say Yes, Tableau is then going to go back to my Google Sheet, pull in the latest copy of the data and try and visualize it. And now we've got a problem. Tableau can't find a table called Team Value because it doesn't exist in that Google Sheet anymore 'cause I just changed the name of it. Instead it's found a column called Team Value with the pound sign. Now, because I've used the original name as part of my view, it's now broken my view. If I'd have changed the name of any of the other fields that weren't being used, it wouldn't be a problem. But because we've directly referenced one of those, it's now broken my dashboard. It's easy enough to fix. I can right-click on the Team Value with the red exclamation mark, I can go down to the bottom where it says Replace References, I can click on that and tell it to do a global swap for those two values and that will now fix the dashboard. So Google Sheets a really good way of storing data, but because of the collaborative nature, you have to be really careful that nobody's going to come along and rename the columns or remove columns that you are later relying on in your Tableau dashboard.

#### [How to connect to PDF files and extract tables of data](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-connect-to-pdf-files-and-extract-tables-of-data?u=76281980)

Now, getting data out of a PDF always used to be quite tricky, because the table format within a PDF can have a lot of background formatting in it, and sometimes we'd have to copy and paste it either directly into [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md), which may or may not work, or we'd have to put it into a third-party piece of software and then import that into Tableau, which again, may or may not work. #### [Setting up the default properties of your data source](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/setting-up-the-default-properties-of-your-data-source?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/setting-up-the-default-properties-of-your-data-source?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Once we've successfully connected to our source of data, no matter where it is, we are able to change the default values of any of our fields so that we don't have to do it every single time we use it in our visualization. Let's see an example of that. So if I had say, sales out of my column and region on my rows, I'm now looking at summer sales broken down by region. But on the label and the axis, it's just saying numbers. It's not indicating this is actually a currency. So I could go ahead and right click on summer sales. I can go to format and then I can change the number format from being an automatic number to being a custom currency because these are pretty large numbers. I'm going to change it to zero decimal places. So now my sales axis is labeled as a currency. So that's easy and straightforward to do and I could go and do that with my other values that I have on display. The problem is if I then go to a new sheet, and this time I want to look at the sales by segment, when I pull sales out of my data pane, again, the labeling is still as the numeric value. There is for that is, is that in our data pane in the left, all of our measures don't have any default values. When [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) sees a number in a newly imported data, it just thinks it's a number. It doesn't understand that things might have a currency or be a percentage. We have to set those default values
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/setting-up-the-default-properties-of-your-data-source?u=76281980&t=95)** and that's easy enough to do. I could right click or click on the caret on sales to get the context menu and about halfway down under default properties, I can change the default number format. So I could change it to be a custom currency. I get rid of my two decimal places. And now anytime I use sales, it's automatically going to pick up that formatting. So if I create another sheet, and for example, if we put category on our rows and now when I bring out some sales, it's automatically showing it as a currency. So I could do the same thing for profit 'cause that's also currency and change default properties number format to custom currency. And if I was to pull the profit out, I then get the total amount of profit. Now the discount is actually a percentage. If I pulled a discount out and change that to be the average, maybe I don't know what the average discount is for each one of my three categories, I can see it's giving it as a decimal, which is actually going to be a percentage in this case. So I could go to the discount and I could make two default changes actually, I could change the number format to be a percentage, single decimal place. So now we have nice whole numbers for my percentage and I could also change the aggregation. So maybe I always want to look at
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/setting-up-the-default-properties-of-your-data-source?u=76281980&t=188)** what the average discount is 'cause the sum of discount doesn't really make sense. It's the concept for our data. So if I change that now to the average, anytime I drag discount from the data pane, it's going to take its formatting with it and show the average discount as a percentage. We can also make changes where we could change the names, we could change the grouping to enable us, not to have to make these changes every single time. The other thing I've noticed is that row ID when I imported my data, appears to have been pulled over as a measure. Now the row ID is just an ID number that's been assigned to the row in our data. So it starts at row one, finishes at row 10,000 or however many records we've got in here. Now Tableau just saw a number. So it assumes that that's a measure that we're going to want to aggregate together. But for our data, that doesn't make any sense. So this could be a user ID, a customer ID, product ID, something within your data where the ID denotes the ID rather than a measure that you want to aggregate. So we have to tell Tableau that that's not correct. It's actually a dimension. There's two ways we can do that. We can either left click and drag it into the dimension section and it'll then change become a dimension or we can right click and click on Convert Dimension and it moves the row ID into the dimension
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/setting-up-the-default-properties-of-your-data-source?u=76281980&t=282)** and we change the indicator to be blue, to show that now this is a discreet value. It's important to remember that any change we make within our data isn't reflected back in our row table. Tableau's read only, it doesn't write back to the data. So any changes made here are not going to be changed back in the [Excel](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Microsoft%20Excel.md) sheet that I earlier connected to. However, changes to the Excel sheet are going to get carried forward. So if we were to rename row ID to just be ID, the name of that field is going to change. If that's been used in referenced somewhere in a calculation or in part of the view, maybe in the row of the column or even the color, that's going to break the dashboard. So be really careful about renaming your columns to ensure that your tables don't change.

#### [Solution: Creating a data source](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/solution-creating-a-data-source?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>

### 3. Combining Data

#### [Combining data using relationships](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/combining-data-using-relationships?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/combining-data-using-relationships?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Often when you're building a visualization, you need to bring in additional data in order to answer the questions and do your analysis. Now, another example here, I'm looking at the Summer Sales broken down by category, but what I want to know is which of our regional managers is responsible for those sales? I want to compare each one of their performance, for example. Now, if we look on our Data pane on the left-hand side, that information isn't in there. There's no manager name, and there's no regional manager. None of that data is currently available to us. We've got the region, so I know where the [products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md) were sold, but not the person responsible for it. Luckily, [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) makes it very easy to combine data together using relationships. We simply define that these two tables are related somehow, and Tableau does all the work of combining that data for us. To see that in action, we're going to go and edit our Data pane, and we're going to add our new table. So I'm going to right-click on the order name, click Edit Data Source, and I can see that there are three other tables within my [Excel](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Microsoft%20Excel.md) sheet The orders one I'm already using, that's got the order information. We have a tab here called People. When I click on the View Grid, it shows four regions and the regional manager for each one of those regions. So I know that's the table that I want to bring in. Now to build a relationship, all we do is drag that table into the middle of the view, and a little orange noodle would appear
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/combining-data-using-relationships?u=76281980&t=95)** to say that when I release the mouse button, Tableau's going to relate these two tables together. Now at the bottom of the screen, we can see the result of that relationship. Tableau's identified a column in Orders called Region and the same column in People called Region, and makes the assumption that these two tables are related by that column. That's important to note. All Tableau does is match it on the name. It doesn't know the content. So if you[re trying to relate two tables together that just so happen to have a same column name in each one, Tableau will attempt to do that relationship join between it. Now you can remove that join if it's not the correct one and select the correct ones that you want to do the join on. But it's always good just to check to make sure that Tableau's made that correct assumption. But, in this case, it has 'cause it's quite a simple join. So I can see the four values that Tableau's going to put in together. And when I go back to my sheet, we can see that the Date pane has changed slightly. We now see that the Measures and Dimensions are grouped together. So one set is under Orders, and towards the bottom, there's another set under People. So what Tableau's done is in the background, behind the scenes, it's related those two tables together and says that these two are connected via the Region. And then once that's happened, these are just treated as additional fields.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/combining-data-using-relationships?u=76281980&t=189)** So my People and my Regional Manager are exactly the same as all the other data types. So I can basically just now add them into my views. So I could maybe put Regional Manager onto Color. So now my Regional Managers are color-coded blue, orange, red, and green. So I can then see for each one of my subcategories, how each one of my managers has performed. I might want to put the manager on say the column and split out each individual manager. The point is, is that as of now, that extra information is just part of my data. I don't have to think about doing the joins. I don't have to think about how to combine that data. Tableau's doing it for me. Tableau finds the most efficient way of combining that information to create the ultimate view that we're after.

#### [What are the different ways of joining data?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-are-the-different-ways-of-joining-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-are-the-different-ways-of-joining-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's take a moment to talk about the types of join there are and the different ways of combining data. Now, if we're using relationships within [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md), where Tableau takes care of doing the join for us, or we're manually joining our data, it's important to understand what's actually happening behind the scenes. It's good to know the different types of join, because that way we can anticipate the sort of results we're going to get. And in the manual case, pick the right type of join to make our analysis easier. Now, there are three main types of join. There is the inner join, the left and right join, and the outer join. They all work in a very similar way. The mechanism is quite similar, but the results can be very different. Now, which one you pick is going to depend on what ultimately you want to do with that data. Now, all joins add columns, and this is going to result in a wider table. So when we join tables together, we make a wider final table. So a join add columns from one or more tables to make a wider table. But for that to work, we need to have a common field between the tables that we're trying to join together, that we can say that the column in table A is the same as a column in table B. That way, Tableau can then join those two tables together to add the additional columns to make the wider table. Let's look an example of this. So if we had two tables, one with a list of [products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md)
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-are-the-different-ways-of-joining-data?u=76281980&t=96)** and then one with a list of sales data. So the blue table is a list of our inventory. So we have four products with IDs, 1, 2, 3, 4. We have the name and we have the price. In the right-hand table, we have some information about some sales. So we have the Fruit ID, so the fruit that the customer bought, the customer name, and how many of that product each customer had. Now, I want to combine these two tables together, because I want to know, for example, how much money did each customer spend. Now, that information isn't in either one of those tables. We need to combine the data together. So I can say the price of the product multiplied by the amount of the product gives us the total price. So first of all, let's look at what an inner join would do in this situation. So an inner join is only going to show us transactions that appear in both tables. So we're going to join the table together using the common field, Fruit ID, and we'll match the Fruit ID in the first column with the Fruit ID in the second. And this is the resulting table that we get. Now, this resulting table has only got three rows, whereas both of the starter tables had four rows. Well, why is that? Well, if we look, in our inventory, oranges have got the Fruit ID 4, but in our sales, nobody bought an orange. So that one doesn't exist in both tables, therefore, it's not part of the inner join.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-are-the-different-ways-of-joining-data?u=76281980&t=189)** Similarly, Denise bought five fruits with an ID of 5. Well, that 5 doesn't exist in our inventory. Maybe it's a fruit that we used to sell, we don't sell it anymore, but that data doesn't appear in both the tables and therefore, it's not part of that final inner join. So this join, the inner join is good, for we know that there is data in both of tables that we want. And if the data in one table we're not interested in or data in another table where it doesn't exist in both, it gets excluded from the join. Now the left and right join work in exactly the same way, except all the items in either the left or the right table are going to be returned, plus any matches it can find. So first of all, let's look at the left join. We're going to left join the orange table, our sales data with our product data, and this is the table that we get. Now, this has got four rows, unlike the inner join, because we have four items in our product list, 1, 2, 3, 4. And where we have sales in the corresponding table, we've matched those together, so we can calculate our totals. But for oranges, we didn't sell any oranges. There's no orange sales data. So instead we have a blank line. Now, if we'd have done the right-hand join instead we'd get a similar result. We'd have four rows,
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-are-the-different-ways-of-joining-data?u=76281980&t=283)** because there are four rows in our sales table. And in all cases where we can find the match, we can do the correct join, we can do our total. In the case of Denise who bought that missing fruit item, the product is listed as blank. The left and right joins are very useful if you know that there are some missing items, if you know that we haven't sold the products. But I still want to list all of the customers, or if I wanted to know all of the customers, but not necessarily ones that haven't bought anything, maybe we are looking at this month and we have customers that haven't bought anything this month, but they'll be customers last month. We still want to include them, because they were still former customers, it's just that we don't have any current data for them. So the left and right join is really useful when you know there is empty fields, but that's acceptable to you. The third join type is the outer join. And this is kind of a combination of the left and right join in that all items in both of the tables including matches where possible are going to be created. So if we take our two tables and do an outer join, we have six rows this time. We have all of the sales data, including sales data where we haven't got product data and we have all of the products and we have blanks where we haven't sold any of those products. So the outer join gives us everything from one table, everything from another table, and puts blank values in wherever it can find it. Now, there is no right and wrong way of joining your data.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-are-the-different-ways-of-joining-data?u=76281980&t=378)** There are perfectly valid reasons why you might want to do an inner join versus a left or right or outer join, but the results are going to be very different. With an inner join, you're not going to see null values in your rows, whereas with a left and right, you inevitably will do. And if you have got those in your final data, you're going to have to do some mechanism later on to take that into account. When Tableau builds relationships, it picks the correct join type, depending on the type of view that we're trying to use. But if we create a manual join, we can get some very different results depending on whether we do inner, left, right, or outer. The correct choice, well, that's up to you.

#### [How to join tables in two using a cross database join](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-join-tables-in-two-using-a-cross-database-join?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-join-tables-in-two-using-a-cross-database-join?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the real strengths of [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) is the ability to combine data from two different data sources in two different locations. We're not limited to just joining sheets within the same [Excel](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Microsoft%20Excel.md) document or tables within the same database. We can use a mechanism called cross database joins to combine tables from two different Excel documents or an Excel and a [Google](../../Glossary/Service/Google.md) sheet, or two completely different [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md), maybe a database and an Excel sheet. Wherever your data is, as long as Tableau can connect to it, it can then combine it together to form one single data source. And once that's done, the analysis just happens like every other data source. So let's see this in action. So I have a table here just showing the subcategory and the sales, and I have a text file called Sales Target.csv. And within this I have some categories with a sales target. And I want to know for my current sales, am I hitting the targets? Am I exceeding them? Have I still got some way to go? Now this exists in a completely separate file to the Excel sheet that I've already connected to. So I need to use a cross database join to bring those tables in. The first thing is we right click on our data source name, click edit data source. Now at the moment I have one data source with one data connection. I need to add a second data connection.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-join-tables-in-two-using-a-cross-database-join?u=76281980&t=95)** We're not adding a data source, we are adding a connection within the existing data source. So I'm going to click add on the connection tab. Now this file is a CSV file, which is actually a text file. And then navigate to find it in my data sources folder where I placed it earlier, and I click on open. And Tableau's identified the table structure and I can click on the view data and it's successfully turned the CSV into two columns, one called category name, one called sales target. We can also see that now I have two separate connections within my data source on the top left. I have one to the original Excel file, which is now highlighted blue, and the second one to my new sales target text file, which is colored orange. So now I can simply drag my sales target into the data connection window, the orange noodle appears, and we create the relationship. Ah, now we've got a problem here. We've got a red triangle and an orange dotted line. Well, the reason is, is that Tableau couldn't find two columns that were called the same thing. By default, whenever you do a join or a relationship, Tableau's looking for a matching column name in each of the tables we're trying to relate together. Now in the case of our sales target, we haven't got one. So if I go back and look at it again. Ah, okay, so these are all called category name.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-join-tables-in-two-using-a-cross-database-join?u=76281980&t=189)** Well, in my original data, these values of these [products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md) are actually in a table called subcategory. So that's why Tableau couldn't find the match because we haven't got a common name across the two data sources. So in my data relationship window, the bottom left, I'm going to select the subcategory from my order table and tell Tableau that that's actually the equivalent to the category name column in my sales target. And now I can see that we've correctly identified the column and the join is satisfied, everything's good. Now, when I go back to my sheet one, I can see that my data pen has changed slightly. All of this fields that come from the order table have now been grouped together. I have the dimensions in the top and then the measures at the bottom. And then a new table called sales target with a category name and the sales target. Now, once that join has been created and the relationship has been defined, Tableau treats these are just another set of fields that we can use in our analysis. So what I could do now is I could drag my sales target onto my sales axis. I could put measure names onto my colors and I can compare each one of my subcategories. I can look at the sales in orange and compare it to the target in blue. So I can see appliances, my sales haven't quite reached my sales target, whereas accessories have exceeded it.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-join-tables-in-two-using-a-cross-database-join?u=76281980&t=281)** And it looks like copiers is very, very close, but it looks like it's just exceeded that target. This data is coming from two distinct files in my laptop, but I've defined the relationship between two columns that weren't named the same thing. And once that's happened, Tableau's just handling all the data joins in the background for me, I don't have to think about it anymore. We could go on and add more files or more data connections, making it more and more complex, and Tableau just handles it for us. This is one of the real powers that Tableau has in that it just likes data. It doesn't care where that data is, and it will do its best to put it all together for you.
