#### [How to append one data source to another using unions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-append-one-data-source-to-another-using-unions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-append-one-data-source-to-another-using-unions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] A common way that people like to store data, particularly in something like [Excel](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Microsoft%20Excel.md) or [Google](../../Glossary/Service/Google.md) Sheets, is using tabs to have the same data and the same structure but duplicated over multiple tabs. As an example, look at this data I have here. This is looking at rainfall data for different cities in different states. I have a tab for January, I have one for April, July, and October. Now you see this kind of data structure all the time because it's easy for a human to add another month, add the data to it. It makes it easy for them to kind of look through that data. But in terms of analysis, it's actually quite hard for us to do because now I have multiple datas all over different sheets. I need to be able to combine those together. Now I don't want to join them because that doesn't make sense. And what joining will do will add additional columns. What I want to do is append the April data to the bottom of July data and the April data and the October to make one long tall table. That method is using a union. So let's see that in action in [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md). So first I'm going to connect to my data, which is an Excel sheet. And it's in my data source folder in a file called Average Rainfall. Tableau's going to open that up. It's going to identify the sheets. So it's found my four sheets in here, and I want to union them together. There's a couple of different ways of doing it. First I'll do a very manual way and then I'll show you a bit more of an automated way. So let's start by bringing in January's data.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-append-one-data-source-to-another-using-unions?u=76281980&t=94)** So I can see my three columns from the January tab, my City, my State, my Rainfall. And that's how Tableau's identified that this is a city and put a little map icon here to show that if I wanted to, we could map that data. Well now I want to bring in April's data as well. Now watch as I drag April in, we see the noodle appear to show that Tableau wants to build a relationship between the two. And that's not what I want. I don't want a wider table, I want a taller table. I want to append these. I want to union them together. So what I have to do is move my mouse til its underneath the first table and then the Union icon appears. When I release the mouse, the icon changes to show this kind of brickwork to show that we have unioned the tables together. I could then go on and do the same thing with July, and October, making sure that we don't activate the noodle. And now I have all of my four sheets unioned together. Now if we look in the data pane at the bottom, we can see that I now have my City, my State, and my Rainfall. And I have two new columns at the end, one called Sheet and one called Table Name. This is quite useful because it's telling me that this row of data came from a sheet called April, and if I scroll down I would see July and January and October's data in there as well. So that's one way of creating the union. I'm going to right click and I'm going to remove that and we'll do the second method.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-append-one-data-source-to-another-using-unions?u=76281980&t=190)** The second method is to use the New Union icon. When I drop that in, we get this user interface come up. And again, there's two methods of using this. There's always more than one way of doing it in Tableau. I can either manually drag and drop the tables in. So I could add April's, and then January's, and maybe July's, maybe I only want to use those three. I could then do Apply. And we get the same result we had earlier with my states, cities, my rainfall, and my sheet names. Now if I've only got three or four tabs, that's easy enough to do. But if I had more than that, I could use the New Union and use the Wildcard automatic feature. Now what this will do is it will search through the document and put every single sheet in it can find. This is quite handy because if someone was to add another column, another sheet, the automatic union would automatically bring it in. So if I just do, OK, this is going to include all of the sheets in the average rainfall workbook, and we can see that this time again, I have my three values that have come from the sheets, and I have my sheet name. And this time I have a path. This is because we can also search up and down through our table structure looking for files to bring in. Now you might have noticed every time I did the union, something a bit odd happened.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-append-one-data-source-to-another-using-unions?u=76281980&t=282)** I have these Null values in here. Now why is that? Well, the reason is, is because of a format change in one of my tabs. If I go back in January, my column headings were City, State, and Rainfall. In July, City, State, and Rainfall, changed. Because now it says Rainfall mm. What I think happened in July was that whoever filled this data in, tried to be a little bit more helpful and put the fact that these values are millimeters in here. Now, when Tableau did the union, what happened was it saw that new column and it says, "Okay, well I've got a new column here. I don't what to do. So what I'll do is I'll pad it out with nulls for every place where there wasn't that table so that I can build the ultimate table for you." If I increase my preview, I can scroll down and see that in the case of July we have data in that column, but Null is in that column. Now because I know that actually these two mean the same thing, I can override this and I can say to Tableau, "Combine these two together." If I select one and hold down Control or Command on a Mac, to highlight both those columns, I can then click on the caret and I can select Merge Mismatched Fields. This then combines those two columns into one. So now I won't have any Null values.
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-append-one-data-source-to-another-using-unions?u=76281980&t=376)** I can then go ahead and rename this column and I'll call it Rainfall mm. And now when I go into my sheet, I could go ahead and build my visualization. So maybe I just wanted to look at the total amount of rainfall. I could use the name of the sheet as the color because the name of the sheet denotes the month. I can then see which ones of my cities have the highest rainfall, which have the smallest. I could order them and I can see in which month do they have the most rainfall.

> **[6:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/solution-combining-data-part-2?u=76281980&t=374)** We need more control over what Tableau does. Okay, well there's a make date function. Would that do it? This would return a date value. Well, that's what we want. Constructed from a year, a month, and a day. Aha. Now we've got the year. We haven't got the month and the day, but we could fake that. We could say January 1st because this is all just expenses for the year. So that would work perfectly fine for what we need. Okay, so let's change this to be make date. Now the year we'll use the value of date. Then we'll give it the first of the month. Let's do apply. That looks better. So the date field was 2017 and our new expenses date is now the 1st of January, 2017. That looks better. So let's just check that looks okay on a chart. So we can go ahead. Here's my expenses date. I can put that out in here. And brilliant, Tableau recognizes that those are dates. If I change it to the month, I've now got my monthly dates. Let's put some data on and I can see my expenses for each of those years. Brilliant. We've managed to fix it exactly how we needed it. Looks like this data is ready to go. So let's save that workbook and wait for the next question.

### 4. When and How to Create Data Extracts

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [What are the pros and cons of using a data extract?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-are-the-pros-and-cons-of-using-a-data-extract?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-are-the-pros-and-cons-of-using-a-data-extract?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] This video only applies to [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) desktop. So what exactly is a data extract and why might we want to make use of one? Well, broadly speaking, a data extract is a local copy of a data source. What we do when we create an extract is we copy all of the data from our live connection into a file called a TDX, or TDE, or hyper file onto our local machine. Well, why? Why would might we want to do that? What are the benefits of doing that? Well, the first benefit is speed. Every time you connect to a data source, Tableau's writing commands to that data source to pull information out. Now, if that's a long run query, that can take some time. It might be seconds or even minutes to pull that data out. If you're trying to pull five years worth of data out of a transactional database, that's an awful lot of information that's having to be pulled out. Now, that might be fine if you're doing a one-off query. Maybe the speed is kind of acceptable in that case. However, if you've published that data and you're using it in a dashboard, that's a really poor experience for your end user. Every time they make a change to a dashboard, they've got to wait while this query executes to get all that data in. With the extract, we don't need to do that. That query happens once when we create that extract. When we query the extract itself, we're accessing a very fast file on your local machine. So the speed is really, really good.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-are-the-pros-and-cons-of-using-a-data-extract?u=76281980&t=98)** The second reason why extracts can be useful is portability. If you've got a live data connection to, say, a database, we are running over a network. Now, if you need to go offline for whatever reason, you lose access to that data. And an extract, however, is a local farm on your local machine. So if I create an extract on my laptop, I can unplug my laptop and go anywhere I like and I've still got access to all of that data because I'm accessing it on my laptop. I'm not going back to the primary data source, which could be held on a network server somewhere. Extracts can be shared because they're a file. I can send the file to somebody else. That means that we can all have access to that same copy of the data. We don't have to worry about being able to connect to the network drive or the network share. It's all within our files. Lastly, one of the things you could do with an extract is restrict the amount of information that it has. We can apply filters before we create the extract to only pull out one year's worth of data or certain columns of data. We'd have to pull out the entire data set. This keeps the extract nice and lean because it only has the information we are actually going to make use of, rather than dozens of rows or millions of column that we just aren't interested in. Okay, so extracts sound great. Why don't we use extracts all of the time? Well, there's a couple of good reasons to not use extracts that we need to consider. Firstly, and probably most importantly, it's not a live connection. As soon as you make that extract,
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-are-the-pros-and-cons-of-using-a-data-extract?u=76281980&t=191)** it starts to get stale. Any new data that's added to the primary data source, it's not going to get included in your extract. It's a one-time copy. Now, that might be a good thing because we're not interested in up to the minute data. If you're looking at five years worth of data, does it matter if you haven't got data in there from five minutes ago? Maybe, maybe not. But if we are looking at a data source and we're running an analysis and we need to see up to the minute data, the extract just won't do it. One downside to an extract is the portability and the fact it can be shared. That's some security implications. Because all of the data is now on a file, anybody goes access to that file can see all of the data. Typically, if you are connecting to a network share or connecting to a database, there's a username and password and security measures around that. So as we take an extract and we have it as a local file, we bypass some of those security measures. The third and final thing is the fact it needs to be updated. So it's not a live connection and you've got the portable insecurity issues. And anytime we need to pull in more information, we have to update it because of that staleness. So there are some considerations why we shouldn't use the extract. So if we think about the pros and cons of the extract versus the live, there's kind of three main things. If we need speed and there's no way to speed up the query or make the database work faster, we've hit those limitations.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-are-the-pros-and-cons-of-using-a-data-extract?u=76281980&t=286)** Then the extract gives us that extra boost of speed. If we need to able to access this data offline, maybe we're traveling, when we don't have a network connection, then an extract lets us carry on doing our analysis. The reason why we need to use the live connection is when we need up to the second data. If you've got a dashboard that's looking at current data that's being entered into your system, the extract isn't going to do that. And we can set out extracts to run on a schedule. A server can refresh every 15 minutes or so, but your server administrator might not want you hammering the server every 15 minutes, particularly if the extract itself takes a lot longer to run. So the big question is, should you use a data extract? And the answer is, it depends. What is it you need to be able to do with your data? Is it the speed? Is it the portability? Is it the live nature of the data? Is it extra security? Those are considerations you need to bear in mind before deciding whether or not to actually create an extract in the first place.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) (2)
> **CLI Commands:** make (5)
> **Definitions:** is a  (3), means that (1)
> **Env Vars:** tdx (1), tde (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

Now, for me, I'm fine with that, I'm going to hit Yes. Tableau will then go back to the original source of your data, in my case, my Excel, and pull in all of those changes. It's going to reload that data and then recompute all of the visualizations that use it. Now, I have to do that manually every time I want to pull that in, but if we've published this data source to the Tableau server, when we do that, we can set a refresh period. Now, those periods will be available depending on whether your admin has allowed you to either A, set a refresh period, and they might have said, you can only refresh at a set period intervals. Extracts have good pros and cons. Portability and speed are one of those. So, understand the reasons why to make an extract before you go ahead and do it. To understand how to make extracts more secure and faster, check out the videos on how to limit the data in an extract. Remember, this only applies to Tableau Desktop. Tableau Public cannot create data extracts.

### 5. Comparing Measures

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [What are measure names and measure values?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-are-measure-names-and-measure-values?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-are-measure-names-and-measure-values?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the key things to understand why [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) behaves the way it does is the relationship between green field and blue field, between continuous and discreet fields. To understand them in a bit more detail, check out the chapters where we go into detail on green and blue fields. Now, one of the inbuilt rules that Tableau has is that for every green continuous field you put on a row or a column, it will create an axis. So if I drag out sales and put that onto my column, I get a sales axis with the total sum of all sales plotted in a bar chart. If I pull out profit, I get two green fields, two axes, one next to each other. Now, there's some situations where actually I want to have profit and sales kind of either sharing an axis or all stacked on top of one another so I can compare one against the other. And this makes it difficult to do because we have this inbuilt rule. So Tableau's going to try and help us by using two values called measure names and measure values. Now, these are two fields that exist within your data source, but not necessarily in your underlying data. If we take a look at the Data pane, we can see all of the dimensions in blue, all of the measures in green. But there are a couple of ones that look interesting. There is measure names, measure values, an order count, and a latitude and a longitude. Now you'll notice that all of the names
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-are-measure-names-and-measure-values?u=76281980&t=93)** of these fields are in italics. This denotes that these are not part of the original data. So you won't find these columns in the [Excel](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Microsoft%20Excel.md) sheet that we connected to. These are values that Tableau generates for us automatically when we need them. So let's look at the measure names and measure values. What do they actually do? If I clear out my rows and columns, if I put measure names onto my column, we have one single bar with the value of 2.3 million pounds. Okay, that doesn't really make any sense. But notice what has appeared. We've got a new card, we've got a card called Measure Values. Now what this is, this is a container that has all of our measures inside it. And what happens is that Tableau then takes all of those, combines them together to give us that single bar. But what about this measure names field? What happens if we pull this out? Remember, when we create a visualization using measures and dimensions, typically we are looking at the aggregation of one measure broken down by dimension. We've aggregated the measure value. So let's break it down by measure names. So if I put that on my row, now I have five bars, one for each of our measure names. You have the count of orders, the discount, the profit, the quantity, and the sales. So we can then see the total value of each one
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-are-measure-names-and-measure-values?u=76281980&t=187)** of our individual measures. Now notice they're all plotted on the same axis. This is useful, so I can do a direct comparison between one measure and the other. It might not necessarily make sense. And certainly in this case, why would you plot a currency value of sales and profit against a numeric value of quantity? That doesn't make sense. And certainly when we talk about discount, which is a percentage, and we've chosen to aggregate it as the average doesn't make any sense at all, but Tableau's just treated them as numbers. So in Tableau terms, it's perfectly valid, but our conception of that is it's incorrect. What measure names and measure values allow us to do is to bypass some of these rules that Tableau's built in. So it's quite useful. Most of the time you won't ever use measure values and measure names in this way. You don't tend to add them to a view. They tend to just appear, usually when you've tried to do something that breaks Tableau's rules. Remember, Tableau tries to be really helpful at all times. And rather than just saying, no, you can't do that, it says, okay, I'll let you do it, but let's bend the rules ever so slightly. So just remember this. Measure names and measure values aren't part of your original data. They are two fields that exist as a dimension and a measure in italics that Tableau generates for you as and when you need it. More often than not, they'll suddenly appear in your visualization.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-are-measure-names-and-measure-values?u=76281980&t=280)** You have a filter at the top in the Filter pane. And if we look at this, this would allow us to turn on and turn off which measures we want to visit. So maybe I just want to see the sales and the profit 'cause they're numeric values. That makes sense. Notice as I turn off the filter, whatever was in the measure value container also changes. So maybe if I said, actually, I do want to put quantity in, I could add that to the measure value container, and it has the same effect as changing the filter. We'll see as we go on where measure names and measure values will suddenly appear. But it's good to have an understanding of what they are, what they do, and then how we can exploit them.

#### [Creating a combined axis chart](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-combined-axis-chart?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-combined-axis-chart?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One way to compare measures is to create a combined axis chart. This is where we tell [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) to break its own rules and combined measures into a single axis, so we can compare one measure and one value against the other. So let's see this in action in two different charts. We'll look at a bar chart and then we'll look at a timeline. So for this bar chart here where we have the summer sales broken down by the subcategory, now what I want to be able to do is visualize the profit at the same time. Now obviously there's a couple of ways we could do this. I could put profit onto color so I could then see which of my [products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md) made high sales, low sales, and which ones made a profit. But if I want to see the profit in bars, I have to do it in a particular way. Now, if I added profit to the column, I get a second bar chart. This is due to the inbuilt rule that Tableau has that for every green field on a column or on the rows, it will create an axis. Now, this is a perfectly fine visualization, but it's quite difficult to compare one bar's length against the other bar's length because they're separated. What I want to do is overlay them so they're using the same axis. So to do that, we have to be very careful where we add that profit. So let's remove it from our column and we'll drag it out again, but pay special attention to where we drag and drop it to.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-combined-axis-chart?u=76281980&t=95)** So I'm going to take the profit and instead of dragging and dropping it onto the column, I'm going to drop it onto the existing sales axis. And when I get close, the icon changes to a double axis icon. When I release it, the whole user interface changes and there's a lot of changes that have just happened on here. We've got many more bars. If we look on the column, we now have a green field called measure values. If we look on the rows, we have a new blue field called measure names. There's measure names on the filter. A new cards appear called measure values, which contains the profit and the sales, and my view has changed quite a lot as well. The axis now says value and is just a number, whereas before it was a currency. And for each of our subcategories, we have a bar for profit and a bar for sales, and a header showing the two different values. If I undo that and flick it back and forth, you can see how much changed just by dropping the profit on that existing axis. So what did Tableau do? Well, it wants to do what you asked it to do to plot two fields on the same axis, but it also knows that it has these rules that it can only have an axis per green field. So what it does is it brings in measure values. So what we're doing is we are plotting the value of measure values,
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-combined-axis-chart?u=76281980&t=190)** and Tableau creates a container called measure values that we have dragged and dropped the profit and the sales into. This allows Tableau to satisfy its rule because it only has the one green field on the column. But by having that container, you can put as many of your measures into there as you'd like. Now, this also works for a line chart. So if I go over to my timeline. So here I'm looking at monthly sales. I've got sales on my rows and I've got the month of order date on the column using the continuous value. If I drag out profit and again put it onto the row, I get two timelines. It's not quite what I want. What I want to overlay one on top of the other to use that same axis. So instead, we drag profit out and drop it onto the sales axis. And when we do, we get another combined axis chart. Again, measure values appears on the rows, measure names appears on the filter, and on the marks card we have measure names on the color and we have our measure values container with profit and sales. Now, the reason why measure values appears on the color automatically is 'cause otherwise there's no way to differentiate between those two lines. Whereas with the bar chart, we do have that header with profit and sales. So I could do the same thing actually. So if we took measure names and put that onto the color, now they're colored profit and sales as well. It makes it slightly more easier to see what's going on.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-combined-axis-chart?u=76281980&t=285)** Go back to this timeline. Now, one of the good things about this is that it allows us to see the shape in the data. But the problem is, particularly in an example such as this where the sales and the profit differ by quite a significant amount, we lose a lot of the shape of the data of the profit. The sales seems to have an upward trajectory overall, but also you can see this kind of this annual cycle of peaks and troughs that look to be repeated across the foyers of our data. Is that also present in the profit? And it's a bit hard to see on here actually. So if I was to remove sales, we can see that, well, it doesn't, it appear to have a similar kind of trend, but certainly at that same scale it's very different. The reason for this is that Tableau has to be able to plot all of the values of all of the measures that are in the measure value container. So it has to scale that axis to fit everything. So one of the kind of guidelines for combined axis is although you can mix and match different units, only do it if it makes sense, particularly where you have large differences in the actual amounts. We can see it again with the bar chart that because the bars are scaled for the sales and profit is always going to be less than the sales, we lose a lot of detail. So it's quite hard to see what's happening with some of the least profitable items
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-combined-axis-chart?u=76281980&t=378)** such as envelopes, fasteners, furnishings, and labels. All of their bars are quite small, so it's hard to see a lot of detail of what's going on. Measure names and measure values allow us to make these combined access charts, but we just need to bear in mind a few of those rules. Otherwise it can make things difficult to see.

#### [Creating a dual axis chart](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-dual-axis-chart?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-dual-axis-chart?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Sometimes, we want to compare two measures against each other, but they're either different units or different orders of magnitude. Now, in those cases, the combined axis chart won't quite do it, because when we combine the measures onto that same axis, [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) scales that axis, and if the two measures vary very differently, it's going to have the effect of suppressing one of them down so we don't see as much detail. The way to see all the detail is to use a dual axis chart. So at the moment, I've got the monthly sum of sales. I've got summer sales in my row, and I've got continuous order date at month level on the column. Now I want to see how does the profit change over time. Now, if I create the combined axis chart by just putting profit onto the existing sales axis, I get a very flat profit line, because profit is always a lot less than the amount of sales, which kind of makes sense. But what I want to know is, the cyclical pattern that we see with the sales, is that reflected also in the profit? When sales go up, profit should go up as well, right? I can't really see that in here. So instead what I want to do is create a dual axis chart. I want to have two axes plotted in the same view. So let's go ahead and build that. What we do is we're going to drag profit, and drop it onto our existing view.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-dual-axis-chart?u=76281980&t=93)** But instead of dragging and dropping it onto the existing axis, we're going to go to the far right-hand side of the view, to the opposite side. So as I drag profit out, I pass by the sales axis, I go across the view, and eventually, when I hit the right-hand side, another axis indicator appears. If I release the mouse button then, we have a few things happen. So now we have the summer sales and summer profit on the rows. But notice that the field between the two of them is now a straight line rather than the curved end. I have a key, with profit and sales, and I have two lines, one for profit, one for sales, color coded, and I have two axes. I have my left-hand axis for sales and the right-hand one for the profit. Now in addition, I've also got measure names on my color, and that is what has given us the color legend. You'll also notice that we have got three marks cards, whereas we only had one before. I have one for all of the marks. I have one just for the sales, and one just for the profit. So what Tableau's done is it's created a second axis. It's plotted the profit on that axis, and it's scaled that axis to fill all of the view. So we can see that the sales goes from -20 to 120,000, whereas my profit goes from -4 to 18,000. Now my two lines are overlaid. And I can see that it does appear that, in most cases,
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-dual-axis-chart?u=76281980&t=187)** as sales increased, so did the profit. There's a couple of exceptions, such as around here, where March in 2023, sales went up, but the profit went down. That's interesting, what happened there? I might have to do some further investigation into that. So in cases where we want to look at two measures that vary quite a lot, the dual axis makes sense because we're going to scale to use the full amount of that view. There is one other advantage that we have with the dual axis, and it's these two separate marks cards. The marks card allows us to change the look of the marks. Remember, the mark is either the bar or the line that we see within our view. So in this case, I've got two lines, I have one for sales, one for profit. And I can change each one of these independently of the other. So for example, I could change the size of the profit bar. I could click on Size and I could make it much wider. I could make it much smaller. I could change the type of pattern of that line to make it a dotted line. Maybe I want it to disappear slightly into the background and make the sales that bit more prominent. I can do that, which you can't do with the combined axis 'cause you only have the one marks card. So the two advantages of the dual axis are the axis scaling and the fact you have two separate marks cards. Now we do have the option
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-dual-axis-chart?u=76281980&t=279)** of scaling the axes to be the same. I could right-click on either the sales or the profit axis. I could do right-click and Synchronize Axis, and that would have the effect of scaling both the axes to be the same, which then, in our case, because profit is much smaller, squishes it down towards the bottom of the view. I'm going to untick that to get back to our full view. I'm going to change the path back to the line. Now, one of the other advantages of the dual axis is that we have the ability to color the axes to be different colors. One of the pitfalls of the dual axis chart is, is that you have to make it very, very clear that these are different scales, because you don't want somebody to accidentally think that profit and sales are the same. Conceptually, we kind of know that profit is always going to be less than sales, but with your data, that might not be the case. So it's a good idea to ensure that people understand what the two colored lines mean. I wouldn't just rely on them reading the legend. Ways that we can do this include using profit and sales in the title. So for example, if I double-click on the title, I have the Edit Title box, and I can actually put the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) "profit" and "sales" in the title. I could then change the color of sales to be orange, the profit to be blue.
>
> **[6:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-dual-axis-chart?u=76281980&t=374)** And so now my color legend is encoded in the title for the sheet. In addition, we can also color code the scale of the actual axis itself. If I right-click on the axis, click on Format, I can then click on the font, and I could change that to be orange, to match the sales, and I could click on the profit and do the same thing there and change that to be blue. So now my title says "Profit and Sales" in blue and orange, so does my legend, and my axes. This means we have all the advantages of the dual axis, in that we have the scaling of the axis. We have the ability to change the lines to be different styles. But by color coding the axis, it also means we're less likely to misinterpret that data, which is the most important thing of all.

#### [Creating a bar-in-bar chart](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-bar-in-bar-candlestick-chart?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-bar-in-bar-candlestick-chart?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A slight modification of the combined access chart is a bar-in-bar or candlestick chart. These are really good when you have two measures and you want to compare them, particularly if one of them is set like a target you are aiming towards. You want to see how close we are to reaching that target or how much we've exceeded that target. So you want to plot two measures on the same axis, but change the rearrangement slightly so that instead of having two bars side by side, you have one overlaid over the top of the other. So let's start out by building the combined axis chart and then look how we modify it into a bar-in-bar chart. So our starting point is this simple bar chart of subcategory and sum of sales. Now in my data I have some target sales that we want to compare. I want to see if my sales for each of my sub categories has exceeded the target that's been set for them. And I have that in my data. I have sales target measure. So the first step is to create the combined access chart. So I'm going to take my sales target measure, drop it onto the existing sales access, and I get my combined access chart. So a couple of things happen there. We have measure names and measure values kind of suddenly appear and we have twice as many bars as we had originally. We have one for each sales and one for each sales target for each of my subcategories. There's nothing inherently wrong with this chart.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-bar-in-bar-candlestick-chart?u=76281980&t=95)** It's in exactly what we wanted to do and I can go along each of my subcategories and compare the sales and the sales target. So if we look at appliances, for example, I can see my sales target is 110,000 and my current amount of sales is 108. So we haven't quite reached that target. Whereas something like chairs, we had a target of 160,000, but we're currently at 335,000 in sales. So we've exceeded that target by quite a bit. The only downside to this chart is it's quite messy and quite busy. You know, there's a lot of bars going on. The reason for that is the measure names. We have the measure names on the row, so that gives us our measure names column. And we have a sales and sales target for each one of our subcategory, which means we have twice as many bars. What we really want is to overlay the sales target on top of the sales. That way we can see where we've exceeded the target and what we haven't exceeded the target. So my sales target start at zero
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-bar-in-bar-candlestick-chart?u=76281980&t=190)** and then my sales are stacked on top of the value of that sales target. Now, to make this a bar-in-bar chart, what I need to do is make it so that both the bars are starting at zero. To change that default behavior, we have to go to the analysis menu at the top of the screen. And third option down is stack marks and it's set to automatic, which is on, and I'm going to click off and watch what happens. So the bars now all move to start at zero. Now we can see that the blue bars where they've exceeded the orange bars. So we can see that we've got quite a way to go with fastness before we reach our sales target. Similar with bookcases, we're a little bit short, but appliances we're just about to hit that target. And if you look really carefully, you can see that envelopes have a target of 16,800, and we're current at 16,500. So we're really close there. Now what this shows is cases where we haven't yet reached that target because the sales target is at the back. We want to bring the sales target to the front. We can do that by going to the legend and simply dragging sales underneath the target. This will push the blue bars to the back of the view, and now we see the orange sales target. So now I can see cases where we've exceeded our target so we can see where accessories we're almost double
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-bar-in-bar-candlestick-chart?u=76281980&t=286)** what our target was. Now the disadvantage of this is that now I can't see cases where we haven't yet reached our target. Member appliances and bookcases, so I can't see what the target was. Now it's hidden the actual value of the sales. We either have one view or the other. It's difficult to see both at the same time. To do that, we need to change the size of the bars. If we make one bar smaller, it will sit inside the larger bar. Now, a really quick way of doing this is using measure names again. So we're going to take measure names from the data pane, not the one on color. Leave the one on color, otherwise things will go wrong. Go back to our data pane and we drag measure names and put it onto size. What that does is size one of our measures large, one of our measures small, and in this case the sales target is the smaller of the bars. So at all times we can see what the target was and we can see what the current value of sales is. We can see cases where sales have exceeded our target and we can see cases where we've still got some way to go in order to reach that target. So the combined axis chart has now become a bar-in-bar chart, or it's also known as a candlestick chart because in some cases they look like candlesticks. This is a great way of comparing measures where we have a target to aim for, where we want to see if we've reached the target,
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-bar-in-bar-candlestick-chart?u=76281980&t=378)** whether we've exceeded the target or whether we're really, really close.

#### [Creating a multiple measure crosstab](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-multiple-measure-crosstab?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-multiple-measure-crosstab?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A cross tab is a tabular representation of data. It's still a visualization, even though many people don't consider it one, because we're not plotting a bar or a line. We are using numbers rather than shapes, but it still works in exactly the same way. We're still aggregating the data, and we still break it down by one or more dimensions. And because of that, we can combine measures in a single table. And mechanically, it's very, very similar to combined axis bar charts. So at the start, let's build a simple cross tab, and then we'll turn it into a combined measure cross tab. So the build a cross tab, first of all, we have to insert a measure, so we can look at sum of sales. Now, instead of dropping it on the row or the column, we're going to drop it straight in the middle of the view. This tells [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) that we want to see the value of the sum of sales as text. So the total amount of sales in our data is 2.3 million. Now, much like a bar chart, I'm going to start adding in some dimensions to break this down further. So I'm going to put subcategory into my row, and then maybe I'll put the parent category to the left of subcategory. So now I can see my values for all of my sales broken down by category and then subcategory. So that's a standard cross tab. To turn it into the multiple measure cross tab, we need to add the second measure. And to do that, I'm simply going to take the measure
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-multiple-measure-crosstab?u=76281980&t=92)** and drop it in exactly the same way we do with the combined axis chart. I'm going to drag and drop it over the existing values. So in our case, it's going to be on sales. So I'm going to drag it and drop it over the middle of the view where the numbers are actually being displayed. Now when that happens, it's quite a few things have changed. On the column, we now have measure names. I didn't touch measure names from the data pane, I just moved the profit in. So Tableau's added that for us. By having the measure names in the column means we now have our two column headers, one called profit, one called sales. We are now looking at the value of measure names in the text, not just the profit, not just the sales, but now it's measure values. And we have this measure values container that has appeared, which has got the profit and the sales inside it. So what Tableau's actually doing is it's now displaying the measure values. And what it's displaying is whatever is inside that container. So at the moment we have our two measures, so we have two columns worth of data. But I could add more in there if I wanted to. So we could drag quantity and put that into the measure container, and maybe discount as well. So now I have a table that is showing the aggregate value for profit, sales, the quantity, and the discount for each of my subcategories and my categories. Now, cross tabs are quite useful, because it allows us to see the aggregated values
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-multiple-measure-crosstab?u=76281980&t=185)** without having to have lots of labels or bars everywhere. And it's a familiar-looking table to many people. If people are used to working in [Excel](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Microsoft%20Excel.md) or [Google](../../Glossary/Service/Google.md) Sheets, they're used to a table of data. So the cross tab is quite a familiar-looking thing. But it's important to note we're still aggregating that data. Now, the combined measures lets us see the values of four different measures in one single table, without having to do any major change to the structure of our data. That's what the measure values and measure names allow us to do. So the cross tab is really good when we want to see the values, and the combined measure cross tab is great when we have more than one value that we want to see in a single table.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) (3), [Excel](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Microsoft%20Excel.md) (1), [Google](../../Glossary/Service/Google.md) (1)
> **Versions:** 2.3 (1)
> **UI Navigation:** drag and drop (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Comparing two measures using a scatter plot](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/comparing-two-measures-using-a-scatter-plot?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/comparing-two-measures-using-a-scatter-plot?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Scatterplots are a great way of showing relationships between multiple measures. They're really good at showing things like outliers and correlation between different measures in a very compact and concise visualization. Now, to build a scatterplot, we have two axes, and then we plot the aggregated totals for those two measures somewhere within the axis. Now, because of [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md)'s rules of one green field equals one axis, we know we need two green fields, one on the row, one on the column. So let's put sales onto our column and put profit on our rows, and there's our scatterplot. It doesn't look very interesting, but it is a scatterplot. So what's going on here? What Tableau does is it sums up all of the sales and then sums up all of the profit and then plots where that appears on our axis. And because we didn't tell it to break it down by any of our dimensions, we have one single value. In the same way, when we build a bar chart, we have one single bar before we start adding dimensions. Now to add the dimensions, which is going to drag them onto the detail card on our Marks shelf. So for example, let's look at customer name. If I put customer name onto detail, now each one of my data points is being displayed, is the sum of sales and some profit broken down by customer. And we can see on the status panel
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/comparing-two-measures-using-a-scatter-plot?u=76281980&t=97)** on the bottom left hand corner, I've got 800 marks because I've got 800 customers, the position determined by the amount of sales and amount of profit we get from each customer. Now I'm going to change the mark type from automatic because I'm not too keen on that open circle. So I'm going to go on the mark type, click on circle. Then because we have quite a lot of values that are overlapping, it's kind of one big amorphous blob. So what I'm going to do is put a board around each of my circles to make it slightly clearer to see what's going on. And although the points still overlap, it's much clearer that there are lots of points overlapping rather than several big blobs. So now I can look for correlations and patterns within my data. We can see that we have some outliers. We have some customers where we have large amounts of sales, such as Sean Miller, but although we make a lot of sales with Sean, we don't actually make a profit. His profit is - ,981. Our top profitable customer is Tamara with 8,981. So we can see customers where we have high sales on the right hand side, and we have high profit towards the top of the screen, and we're making losses below the zero axis. I'm still showing 800 individual data points in a very compact view. So that's where scatterplots can be really, really useful. And we can also add more measures or more dimensions to our view, depending on what we want to do.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/comparing-two-measures-using-a-scatter-plot?u=76281980&t=189)** So if we wanted to add color, I could color code them by region, for example. To see if there's any correlation between the sales and profit for customers from a particular region, I can select one of my regions to see if there's anything particularly interesting going on with the data. Now, currently I'm showing two measures, but I could add a third measure by adding one of my measures to color. We could, for example, color code it by sales by putting sales onto the color, but that seems to be a waste of color because although we are now color coding to show the darker color is the higher sales, we've already got that by the position across the x-axis. So maybe using profit might be better. Now if I put it on profit, profitable customers are blue. Once we make losses are orange. That might be a bit more useful to make it really obvious that there are some customers that are really good and other customers that are maybe not so good. The scatterplot allows us to overlay multiple measures in one single view. It allows us to answer questions both individually for a customer, but also look for patterns and outliers. This is a really great way of comparing different measures to see what's happening with our data.

### 6. Transform Your Data with Calculations

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [How do calculations work in Tableau?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-calculations-work-in-tableau?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-calculations-work-in-tableau?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] This video, we're going to talk about calculations, what they are, how you create them, and how they work. So calculations is a huge topic, and it's very difficult to get a real good grasp on them unless you actually start creating them. But it's important to understand what they actually do to your data and how to create them. So that's what we're going to look at now. Now, a calculation within [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) creates a calculated field. So what that means is it's going to be an additional field in our data pane. It's going to be either a dimension or a measure. It's going to be discreet or continuous, depending on the type of calculation that you create. Now, the reason why you might create one is because you need a field that doesn't exist in your original underlying data. You might want to manipulate it in some way. Maybe it's a string and you want to do a string function on it, or it's a number and you want to do a number function. Maybe you want to do some kind of logic calculation. The point is is that you take the input, which could be one of your existing fields, perform a function on it, and you then get an output. Let's have a look at a simple example in action to kind of explain what's going on. So the first thing, to create a calculation, we need to go into the calculation editor. From our data pane, we can click on the caret and select create calculated field. What pops up is this blank window. Now, this can be a little bit scary and intimidating,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-calculations-work-in-tableau?u=76281980&t=93)** because a blank sheet always is. The first thing I'd recommend doing, if it doesn't come up already, is to click on the gray arrow pointing to the right to open up the context menu. This is going to provide some useful help and hints on the functions you can perform, some of the syntax, and then some of the examples. Now, the calculations are grouped together by the type of calculation that they are. So we have number calculations, which are going to be all mathematical functions. There are string calculations, which are going to be ways that we can manipulate an existing string of text. Some of these can be particularly useful if you have useful information embedded within a string, maybe it's a free text field. Maybe you have IDs that are encompassed within another longer string. There are date functions. Dates are one of the trickiest data types we ever have to deal with, and we have functions where we can add datetime to dates. We can look for the differences between two dates, numbers of days elapsed between the orders and the shipping, for example. There are logical functions where we can test to see if certain conditions are true, and then based on that result, give back a different answer. So if this value is greater than X, then do this, else do that. This allows us to build some quite interesting calculations to drive our analysis forward. Now, the basic principle of a calculation is the same regardless of what function you're actually performing.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-calculations-work-in-tableau?u=76281980&t=187)** You have an input, which is typically an existing field, you have the function, and then you have the output. So let's build a calculation to kind of look at how that syntax works. So for the example, I'm going to use one of the string functions. These take a string and produce an output based on that particular function. So there's a function called UPPER. What this will do is it will convert a text string to being all uppercase. Now, I always find it easier to do my calculations when I can see the changes happening in real time. So what I'm going to do is I'm going to create calculations, going to convert the customer name to be all uppercase. Now, because I want to check to make sure that the calculation is working correctly, I'm going to put Customer Name onto my rows. And then going to create my calculation, and then I'm going to put it on the rows next to Customer Name so I can see the result of that calculation. You'll understand what I'm doing in a minute. So first of all, let's create our calculation, so I'm going to give it a name, so let's just call it Upper Customer. And I'm going to use the UPPER function. Now, as I start typing, Tableau will auto-complete for me. So I can select the UPPER function. And it tells me that I need to put the name of the string that I want to manipulate inside the parentheses. So in my case, that's going to be customer name. And again, as I start typing, Tableau auto-completes for me,
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-calculations-work-in-tableau?u=76281980&t=283)** and the calculation is valid. What Tableau does is to test the syntax of the calculation to make sure that we have open and closed parentheses, open and closed quotes, that we've actually referred to a field that exists. If I'd spelled Customer Name with a lowercase n, for example, it says the calculation contains errors. That's because there's no field called Customer name with a lowercase n. Similarly, if I hadn't put the square bracket at the end of customer name, again, the syntax is incorrect. So Tableau will check that we've done it correctly. You know, there's some color coding in there as well. Functions are always colored blue. And the field name is always colored orange. So if we see the correct colors, we also know we've got the correct syntax. Now, Tableau's telling us the calculation is valid. That is not the same as saying the calculation is correct, because Tableau doesn't know what the result of the calculation should be. It only tells you that, if I hit apply or OK, then that calculation will work. So if I hit apply, we can see that the new calculation, Upper Customer, has now appeared in our data pane. And note that the icon has a little equals Abc. That equals tells us it's a calculated field, it doesn't exist in our underlying data, it's been created within the data pane. I can now drag this and put this to the right of my customer name, and I can see the result
>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-calculations-work-in-tableau?u=76281980&t=379)** of that calculation is the uppercase customer name. Now, the reason for doing it like this means that I can check to see that the resulting calculation was correct. If I change the function, instead of UPPER, I change it to lower, the calculation is still valid. Don't worry about the customer, Upper Customer name, that's fine, we're just interested in the function. Now if I hit apply, we see that the result of the calculation instantly is reflected within that simple table. Now, that table isn't the final table that I want, that's not the visualization that I'm after. It's just an easy way to check that the calculation result is what I wanted. There are loads of different calculations, and we're going to look at some of them in different videos, but the syntax is always going to be the same. We have a customer field, which is always going to be colored orange, and we have one or more functions that will wrap around that field. The result of that could be the input to another function, as we'll see in some of the more complex calculations. Calculate fields create an additional table column in our data pane, which we can then use and reference anywhere else we like.

#### [Understanding the order of operations in calculation](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/understanding-the-order-of-operations-in-calculation?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/understanding-the-order-of-operations-in-calculation?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the most important things when it comes to understanding how [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) creates calculated fields is understanding the order of operations, and particularly when does the aggregation occur. Now the citizen action, we're going to look at profitability for some of our subcategories in our superstore data set. So I'm going to start off with a very simple bar chart, which is showing the number of [products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md). So this is the sum of quantity for each one of those subcategories. Now, for every sale that we do, we're either going to make a profit or a loss on each one of those items, and overall, when we add all of our items up together and we divide them by the subcategory, we're going to make either a profit or a loss across the entire subcategory. Now, they seem like very similar questions, but actually they're quite subtly different. And how Tableau handles calculations works in exactly the same way. So first of all, let's talk about profitability overall. So I want to know of all of my sales and my subcategories, which ones were profitable, which ones were losses. The result of this calculation is either going to be true or it's going to be false. There's only two possible answers to this question. > **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/understanding-the-order-of-operations-in-calculation?u=76281980&t=189)** every single bookcase and every single supply that we sell? >
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/understanding-the-order-of-operations-in-calculation?u=76281980&t=284)** our presentation of it has changed, would suggest that well, actually there are profits to be made in tables. Maybe we need to investigate why this section is unprofitable and try and turn that into profit. Now, the reason we get different results is because of where the aggregation occurs. In the case of this calculation, which was the profit per line, we are saying to Tableau, look at each row in our data, look at each transaction, each sale, and say, for this line, and this line only, is a profit greater than zero? If it is, then the result of this calculation is, yes, it's true. Then at the end, we then aggregate those together and then to show the block of the color. So for this calculation, we're looking at each individual row. When we look at our first calculation, which is the profitable overall, we said to Tableau, first of all, sum up all of the profit and then do the test to see if it's greater than zero. So this calculation aggregates the data first and then applies the test to see whether it's greater than zero. The calculation itself, the formula of greater than zero is the same, but it's that aggregation, it's that sum wrapped around the profit that's making the big difference. This is a really important thing to understand with calculations, and it's a good reason
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/understanding-the-order-of-operations-in-calculation?u=76281980&t=378)** why I'd always recommend writing a calculation with a visible table so you can see that results make sense. Both these calculations are completely valid, their syntax is correct, and there is a good use case to use either one of them, but you need to know which one to use and when to use it.

#### [Creating logic calculations using IF functions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-logic-calculations-using-if-functions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-logic-calculations-using-if-functions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Logic functions are a way of testing a scenario, and based on the results of that, we can then return a specific value, which we can then use in our analysis. So to see an example of that, let's look at profitability of the sales of our [products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md). So I'm going to start by looking at the amount of sales for each of our subcategories. So let's put subcategory on our row and I'm going to put sales onto our column. So we now have bars showing summer sales broken down by the category. Now I'm going to know of my sales, which ones were profitable. Now I could do that in a couple of ways. I could put profit onto color. So if I put profit onto color, now all of the bars are color coded by the amount of profit that they made. Then we can see that we've got three potential problems. Bookcases, supplies and tables are all colored orange. This means we make a loss on those three subcategories. Now, the business case might be, well, we should drop selling those three if we're not making profits on them. But actually, is that true for every single transaction? When we put the color with profit, that's looking at the aggregated total, we take all of the products subdivided by the category and then say, "Overall, was this profitable or not?" What we actually want to do is look at the profit for each individual transaction to see whether that was profitable, yes or no.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-logic-calculations-using-if-functions?u=76281980&t=94)** Well, we have to do that with a calculation. That's quite a simple calculation to do that. So let's add a calculation field and we'll call our calculation profitable. Now the calculation is just going to be a very simple, profit >0. This is going to be true or false. There's only two possible values. So we'll create our field called profitable, and now if I put that onto color, we now have all of our fields color coded whether they're profitable, yes, which is true, or false, which would be no. So all my orange bars are where each individual sale of a product was profitable, all ones that are blue, or it made a loss. But again, that doesn't show the whole picture either, because there could be cases where the profit is 0. Now, I'm not sure how these are being represented in here because my current calculation, the result is either true, false, we haven't got that third test in there. To do that, we need to use the if statement. So the if statement will let us have three specific tests. One, if the profit is greater than 0, one if it's less than 0, and one if it is 0, we can then return three different values, which we can then color code. So I'm going to go ahead and I'm going to edit my existing calculation. So right click, edit, and we'll start over. So we'll use the if function. So the if function works by testing the expressions,
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-logic-calculations-using-if-functions?u=76281980&t=191)** and if the expression is true, then it will return the value that's been supplied. So first test, if the profit is greater than 0, then we want to return the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) profitable. The second test is for those where it's less than 0. So ELSEIF the profit is less than 0 then we made a loss.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-logic-calculations-using-if-functions?u=76281980&t=222)** So that covers those two scenarios. The third option we have to take into account is when it is 0. So for that, we'll say ElSEIF profit equals 0, then we've broken even. We then have to tell [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) that this is the end of the IF calculation. So what's going to happen when we apply this is Tableau's going to go down every row of our data. It's going to look at the value of profit, and if it's greater than 0, it's going to put profitable in that column. If it's less, it's going to put loss, and if it's equal, it's going to put break even. So every scenario is covered. I hit okay, and now we see our colors have all changed. I've now got three possible values. I've got cases where we were profitable, which are now colored green. I've got ones that made a loss and I've got some that actually break even. So these weren't visible when we had that simple calculation or when we just used the color. The if function that let us create several different tests. We can make the IF function even more complicated by adding more and more scenarios to be tested. And the results we can then use in any of our visualizations, we can use it on color, we could use it to filter. Once we've created the calculation, it just behaves like any other field. Logic calculations are really, really useful. But I would always recommend building them
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-logic-calculations-using-if-functions?u=76281980&t=315)** in a very simple chart such as we've done here or even in a text table. That way you can see the result of the calculations. Remember, every single transaction, every single row is being tested by this. So we need to catch every single possible scenario. In this situation, it was quite simple because the profit is either profitable or loss or breaks even. There's only three possible values. But for your data, they might be more. If we hadn't have used that break even, we'd have had null values. We can see that, if I go back and change my calculation, if I remove the profit equals 0, and then do okay, we can then see we have null. What this means is that by the time Tableau got to the end of that calculation, it couldn't find any of those expressions that returned true. So when you are doing your calculations, you need to make sure that you catch all of the nulls. So I would always recommend building a simple chart like this because the nulls are always going to be visible. When all your nulls have gone away, you know your calculation is spot on.

#### [How do table calculations work?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-table-calculations-work?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-table-calculations-work?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There are a second set of calculations that [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) can do called table calculations. Now, these differ from calculated fields in one important way. Calculated fields add an extra column to your data. They exist within the data pattern. They can be used anywhere where you use a normal field. A table calculation is a secondary calculation that is applied to the view after the view has been created. Now, to understand that, we're going to look at a couple of examples of table calculations that we're going to apply to an existing view and start with, we'll look at a running total. So here is the monthly sales for our dataset. We have sales on our row, and we have the order date showing the months on the column. Now we can see that we have some peaks and some troughs because this is showing the monthly variation, but maybe we want to see what is the running total. We want to see how the total increases over time. Now, to do that, we can only do that using a table calculation because we can only see how the sum of sales increases every month once we've plotted every month. So to add a table calculation, we have to add it to the existing measure that's been used in our visualization. In this case, it's going to be the sum of sales. So to add the table calculation,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-table-calculations-work?u=76281980&t=93)** what we're going to do is we're going to right click on the green sum sales. That's already part of the view. We're going to go down to quick table calculation. So there are two ways of adding a table calculation. We have the quick view and we have the add table calculation. This one just requires the running total. So let's just select the running total. Now we have one continuous line that increases over time, so we can see how the running sum changes month on month. The other advantage of this is that we don't have those peaks and troughs anymore. So this always makes our data look quite good. We don't see any seasonal variation, so it's quite hard to look for patterns. But if you want to see the trend and how things have grown over time, the running sum will do that for us. Let's look at another one. Let's look at the difference. Again, we'll start with our timeline. Maybe we want to know how did this month compare to the previous month? Maybe we're interested in how the percentage change changes month or month. Again, we can do that with a table calculation. The reason it's a table calculates for the similar one is the one before and that we have to calculate the values for each month first before we can start to compare them to the previous month. That's why it has to be a table calculation. So again, we're going to right click on our summer sales, we'll click on the caret. Going to go to add table calculation. But we can still see the percentage increase or decrease month for month. And in both of these calculations, we have a null value indicator at the bottom right hand corner of the screen. That's because the table calculation is comparing the current month to the previous month. And when you think about it, the very first month in our data hasn't got a previous month to refer back to. So that's what gives us that null value. We can choose to either exclude that data
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-table-calculations-work?u=76281980&t=280)** and not worry about it, or we can add it at the default position. Now, which one you choose will depend on your data and what you're trying to do with it, but that's one way to get rid of that null indicator. The next common use case for a table calculation is the percent of total. That's when we're interested in knowing how much does each of the subparts of the data contribute to the whole. Here's another example where we're looking at monthly sales. This time in a bar chart, and it's broken down by the category. We have three categories, so we have three colors. That's because Tableau's going to update this view as we change the values in our table. That is, we want the percent of total of each of those columns going up and down. So the direction needs to be up and down. > **[7:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-do-table-calculations-work?u=76281980&t=467)** but it's not based on the order of that list. It's based on the underlying data. So I could order them alphabetically because that's easy for me to find a particular customer, but I get the information about their ranking as the column data. So remember, table calculations are applied once the view has been created. It's a secondary calculation to add greater analysis and depth to our visualizations.

#### [Table calc directions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/table-calc-directions?u=76281980)

#### [What are Level of Detail (LOD) calculations?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-are-level-of-detail-lod-calculations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-are-level-of-detail-lod-calculations?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] LOD calculations allow us to control the aggregation of our measures at the level of detail that we want, not the level of detail of the view. Now, what does that actually mean in practice? We have to think about the granularity of our data. So in our Superstore dataset, our data is one row for every product of every order. So in total, we've got just over 10,000 records in this dataset. Now, we always typically want to aggregate those numbers together, but sometimes we want to see the view or a calculation or some values that are a different level of detail. So for example, if we were to look at the category of sales, if I put category and sales of my rows on my column, we get the total amount of sales for each one of our categories. If I turn on the labels, I can see the results of all of the sales for each one of my categories. Now, the calculation for my sum of sales is dependent on what dimensions I have in my view. Now I've got three possible values for category. So I have three bars and that number, the label, is the value of the calculation at that particular level of detail. Now, those values are quite useful
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-are-level-of-detail-lod-calculations?u=76281980&t=93)** 'cause there are the total amount of sales for each one of my categories. But if I break that down further, so if I was to add, say, subcategory in, now the labels have shown me the sum of sales for each one of my categories. What I've lost is the overall value of sales. So if I wanted to compare the sales of my subcategories to the overall sales to maybe see which one has the larger percentage, or if we were doing the average sales per subcategory compared to the average category overall, that's hard to do. What we're trying to do is get an answer at the different level of detail to what had been shown within the view. Now, to get around that we have to use a special type of calculation called an Level of Detail calculation or LOD calc. What that allows us to do is say to [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md), I know you've displayed the data at this level of detail. In this case the level of detail is at subcategory, 'cause that's the smallest level that we can break it down into. But I want you to tell me what the total sum of sales is for the category. So ignore the subcategory and show me at the category level. Now this can be really useful if we want to know what is the average compared to what's been shown in the view. We don't have to worry about creating a view at a certain level just to satisfy the calculation that we need. Now there are three types of LOD calculation, exclude, include, and fixed.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-are-level-of-detail-lod-calculations?u=76281980&t=188)** They all do the similar job in that it allows us to create the calculation at the level of detail that we want. For example, the labeling, or maybe we want to put a reference line on, but they all work in a slightly different way. To understand that, check out one of the three videos about each specific LOD type. Now, LOD calc can be quite confusing and sometimes it's quite hard to think of when might I use an LOD calc. So anytime that you are building a view at a particular level of detail, but you want to see detail in the data at either a higher or lower level of detail, that's when the LOD calc will really come into its own.
