The Include lets us look at a greater level of detail for the calculation,
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/include-lod-calculations?u=76281980&t=191)** but not necessarily have to include that same detail in the view. This is quite useful, because often, we're trying to do aggregations on the data at a much high level of detail. If you think about it, you're going to be accessing data that has thousands, hundred thousands, even millions of rows, but you don't necessarily want to see all of that detail. What you want to be able to do is roll it up to the detail that's important to you, but that level of detail isn't necessarily the same level of detail you want within your view. Even though I've got 2,000 or so products, I've actually got about 10,000 individual transactions in my data. So, the ability to control whereabouts within that level we do our calculation can be really, really powerful.

#### [FIXED LOD calculations](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/fixed-lod-calculations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/fixed-lod-calculations?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The third type of LOD is a fixed LOD function. This allows us to fix a calculation at any level of detail that we want. But the key difference between this function and the other LOD function is that it's not affected by things like dimension filters. This is because of the order of operations that happens with LOD calculations. And to understand that, let's look at the order of operations that typically happens with [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md). Now see where the dimension filters fit within the order of operations. It's between the include and exclude LODs and after the fixed LOD calculation. So if we have an exclude LOD or include LOD calculation and then use a dimension filter with it, that's going to filter the data before we do the calculation. So we're going to get a different result depending on that filter. But if we want to ignore that filtering, then we need to use the fixed LOD calculation. So let's look at an example of this with a simple dashboard. So this is the view using an excluded LOD calculation. All we've got is the amount of sales, the average of those sales for the states and the regions. The second column is an excluded LOD calculation that is ignoring the state breakdown
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/fixed-lod-calculations?u=76281980&t=93)** and giving us the average sales for each region. And the third column shows us the difference between those two. So I can see states that are exceeding or below the regional average. Now look what happens if I add or change a filter. Anytime I add or remove some segments from my data, we can see that the middle column, that is the excluded LOD value, is changing. This is to do with that order of operations. So the filter occurs first and then the LOD calculation happens. Now that might be what we want, but if we don't want to be affected by that filter, then we can use the fixed LOD calculation. So let's create a new calculated field and we'll call this Fixed LOD. So this is a fixed calculation. So we start with the fixed syntax, and I want to fix this at the level of region. And we're going to take the average sales. Now, if I add this to my view, we can see we get exactly the same value as we do for the excluded one. If I go ahead and change it to be a currency, so we use the same number format as our previous one with zero decimal places,
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/fixed-lod-calculations?u=76281980&t=189)** I can go and do the same thing with our new calculation, set it to a currency at zero decimal places so we can see it's exactly the same number. But now look what happens when I change that filter. If I remove the consumer segment, the average on the excluded LOD has increased, whereas the average for the fixed LOD has stayed the same. Now, neither of these is right or wrong. It entirely depends on your use case. But it's important to understand that there is a difference between the fixed LOD and the included and the excluded. The fixed one will ignore the dimension filters. Filters that occur within the data source itself, they'll be applied first, then the LOD count, the fixed one, then the dimension filtering, and then the include or exclude LOD. So it's important to understand the order of those operations and how they occur and how that can affect your LOD calculations. For more information about order of operations, check out the video on Tableau Order of Operations to see a little bit more detail on each of those steps.

#### [Using calculations in a join](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/using-calculations-in-a-join?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/using-calculations-in-a-join?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The most common use case for calculations is when we want to create a calculated field, which will create a new column in our data. But there's another really useful thing we can do in calculations and that's when we're trying to join two tables together, where we need to manipulate the data in one of the columns to match the data in the other column as part of the join or the relationship. > **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/using-calculations-in-a-join?u=76281980&t=285)** we can now see that we have successfully managed to match the data between the two. Now in this particular case, I didn't have to change the case because the match wasn't case sensitive. This depends on the underlying data. But if it was a much more complicated one, we might have to nest several functions together. The real advantage of this is that we didn't have to go back to our primary data source to correct it. I would always recommend that if you can change the primary data source. If it's incorrect in for that visualization, it's probably going to be incorrect for other ones that you might have to do. However, in a lot of cases we don't have the ability to either edit that data or it might be coming from another system we don't have control over, in which case the join calculations mean that we can combine data from different systems with different values and just go ahead and do our analysis and get the results and the answers that we need.

#### [Just because you can, should you create a map?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/just-because-you-can-should-you-create-a-map?u=76281980)

Now, there's always a temptation whenever you have geographic data to instantly want to create a map, and the reason for that is, well, we don't often get to make maps. It's one of those chart types that, when we get the chance to make it, it's really tempting to do that because they're quite nice to make. They look really nice, they're very visually appealing, and they can be really useful in the right context, but there's always a chance that it's not the right chart choice. #### [How to create an area map](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-an-area-map?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-an-area-map?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Okay, you've got some data, you want to create a map, and you're sure that making a map is the right choice. So how do we go about creating a map? And how do we know that [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) even knows how to create a map? Well, if we look in the left hand side of our data pane, we can see the symbols that Tableau's assigned to those fields and tells you what kind of data is there. A, B, C is text. The calendar icon is a date. The hash is a number. But what about these little globes? What are those? Well, that's geographic data. Table's got an inbuilt database where it knows about locations. If it sees a field name with the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) city or country or postcode or state, it would assume that that column of data contains geographic data, and because of that, Tableau will try and create a map for you. We also know that it knows about maps because of two other fields. It has a field called latitude and longitude. Now these are nutanics to show that this is data that's been generated for us. It's not part of our original dataset. We're connected to the Superstore dataset, but there is no column in there called latitude and longitude. What Tableau's got is an inbuilt database that says, "Here's a list of cities, here's list of location, here's the latitude and longitude, so should you want to create a map, I could create the map for you." So how do we do that within Tableau? Well, it's just a case of dragging and dropping the geographic fields into our view, and the map will build it itself.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-an-area-map?u=76281980&t=94)** So let's start at the highest level that we have here. So we have country, city, state, and postcode. So the highest region is going to be country. So let's just simply drag country region right into the middle of our view, not to the row of the column. We want something different to happen, and when we release it, Tableau thinks, okay, this is geographic data. Let's build a map. What it actually creates is a kind of scatterplot. You can see we've got the latitude and longitude on the rows and the column. They're our green field, and the role of green field is an axis. The type of chart that has an x-axis and a y-axis is a scatterplot, and we have one point in the center of one country and one point in the center of another. Now each of my states is colored by the amount of profit.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-an-area-map?u=76281980&t=282)** We can see profitable states and we can see states where we make losses and every gradient in between. The advantage of the field map is that it's a very colorful display. It's very visually quite good. If you have lots of variations within the color shading, it can get a little bit busy. So you have to think about whether it's the good choice or not. Now we don't have to color just by the measures. We could also color it by some of the dimensions. So for example, all of our states are categorized as belonging to a different region, so if I took region out of my data pane and put that onto color, now we have some regional coloring going on. I can see all my states that are part of the central region, east, south, and west. Now if you were to add other dimensions, such as the product name or the customer, that's not going to work because the state is part of one particular region. But we have many customers within the state, so adding those into a field map just doesn't make any sense. If we start dividing that state into smaller and smaller parts, even things such as city, that's not going to work for an area 'cause we can't color that entire area. Area maps are very good for looking at geographic data. They're not a substitute for a good bar chart,
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-an-area-map?u=76281980&t=376)** but they can work really well, particularly in something like navigation. If we want to be able to click on a particular state to see some of its detail and use that as part of a drill down in a dashboard, the field map can actually be quite a good little way of doing that. If you're sure you've got geographic data and you want to see it, the map is a good choice. Area maps rely on the size of the area being colored in. So some of the things you have to consider as well is the actual size of that land mass. Just because it's got a big amount of color doesn't necessarily mean that it's a higher value. These are all things to consider to make sure that your maps are the best they can be.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

#### [How to create a symbol map](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-symbol-map?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-symbol-map?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There are two main ways of mapping data. One is to create a field map where we fill in the outline of a region such as a state or a country. But the other kind of map is where we have a symbol on a latitude and longitude that marks the particular point of data on a map. So let's have a look and see how we would do something like that. Now we know that [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) can create a map because of the way it handles geographic data. When we look in our data pan, we can see there are certain fields that have the globe icon. These are things that Tableau knows about their position of. At the bottom of your data pane, you can see you've got latitude and longitude. Now, that's not part of your original data set. There's no column in our superstore data called latitude and longitude. What Tableau's got is an inbuilt database of hundreds of thousands of locations with a latitude and longitude. And when we drag one of those into the view, Tableau's going to create a plot and position them on that map. Now, what Tableau does is anytime it sees a field called something like city or country, postcode or state, it's going to assume that this is something we want to put on a map. So, how we build that map is actually very straightforward. We simply just drag those fields into our view. The easiest way to do is to start with your top level. So, for our example, I'm going to put country and region onto the very center of our field of view,
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-symbol-map?u=76281980&t=96)** and now we get a map. I mean, it's not an interesting map, but it's a map nonetheless. What this actually is is a map showing the central point of Canada and the United States, 'cause we have only two countries in our data. What we can then do is break this down, much like we've done with every other chart so far, with the bar charts we set with a single bar, we add more dimensions, we get more bars. So at the moment, country's only got two possible values. If I add in state as the next level down, I could add that to my detail to say, "Add this detail into that map." We now get a data point at the center of every one of those states within our data. Now the next level down from that would be city. I could add the city into there, as well. So if I add city to the detail, we now have 616 individual cities that have been plotted on my map. Now this kind of chart might look slightly familiar because actually it's just a scatter block. Remember the in-built rule that Tableau has to work by for every green field on the row or column, it has to create an axis. Now because we've got a greenfield on my column and one on my row, we've actually got an X and a Y. And then the cities are being plotted at the values of those X and Y categories, which is a scatter plot. So actually a map is just a scatter plot with a pretty picture behind it.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-symbol-map?u=76281980&t=190)** So, once we understand that, we can use all the same attributes we can look at with a scatter plot and apply it to a map. So, each of those cities could be colored. Now they could be colored by a dimension. So, for example, the cities are in a certain region. So if I put region onto color, now my cities are colored by the region they're in. If I wanted to see how much of the cities make in terms of profit, I could put profit onto the color instead. So, let's drag profit and put that onto color. And now each of my cities is colored by the amount of profit that we get. Now there's a slight issue with this now in that some of those data points are overlapping. Where we have a big cluster of cities, we can see we have lots of data points overlapping. So let's go to color and I'm going to put a white border around them to make them stand out a little bit more. We could also put a halo around them, as well, if we wanted to to make them stand out just a little bit more from their neighbors. Now we can then see where we have clustering of a large numbers of cities as one single blob. Now, we can see they're individual cities. Part of the issue with using the symbol map is that clustering and congestion of some of those data points. Now, we could [Zoom](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Zoom.md) in on a map in a particular area if we wanted to get around that, but it's something to consider when anytime you build that map, is it actually making the data easier to see or not? In a case like this, maybe it does, maybe it doesn't?
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-symbol-map?u=76281980&t=284)** You have to kind of experiment to see what the data looks like. If I'd of put a different measure, it might have told a different story. If I use this example profit field instead of profit, this one looks slightly different. This one shows that actually there seems to be a more concentration of the less profitable cities in the southern area. What's going on with that? Why is the story there? The symbol maps can also be changed with sizing, much like we saw with a scatterplot where we turn it into a bubble chart. So, for example, if I wanted to see, "Well, which ones of these cities are big sales?" I can see the profits, but how does that relate to the numbers of items? I could put something like quantity onto size, and now each of those city's dots is sized by the amount of sales. If I click on the size slider, I can change the overall size of all of the data points to be larger or smaller. And, again, we see one of the problems that we have with something, a map, kind of like this, is that you have some big, big outlier cities like New York, LA where they are higher concentration of people, the sales are higher, the quantities are higher, which then makes the scaling somewhat harder to see. So, again, is this the best use of data? Luckily, because Tableau makes it so easy to create a map,
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-symbol-map?u=76281980&t=378)** it's actually only a couple of clicks to check it out and see. So, if you have got geographic data, don't assume that the map is the only way to do it, or indeed the best way to do it. But seeing as to create a map is literally a double click, why not give it a go? You might make some interesting insight into your data. Maps are very engaging. They're quite a good choice sometimes to catch people's attention. So, in that case, they can have a good-use case because somebody could see the map, get engaged with the data, and then look at the other charts you've got in there. So maps can work not only as a data visualization in themself, they're also a good way of drawing people in to get excited to see that data.

#### [Create lines to connect locations on a map](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/create-lines-to-connect-locations-on-a-map?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/create-lines-to-connect-locations-on-a-map?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md)'s spatial calculations let us change latitudes and longitudes into spatial objects, join them together with lines, and even calculate distances between them. So let's have a look at this data set that we've got here. So this is showing stores in the LA region. Now each of these data points belongs to a particular store, and I've got the latitude and longitude of the destination, which is the store itself, but I also have this origin latitude in my data pane. Now this origin latitude is the lat and long of the LA store. Now what I want to do is show how far away all of the regional stores are to the main store. Now, to do that, we can use the distance calculation that Tableau has, but also we can join the outer stores to the central store using lines. So to do that, first we're going to open up the calculation editor. We're going to create an origin point, a destination point, then a line, and then the distance. We're going to have four calculations in total. So first of all, we'll create the origin and the destination points. Now these are all spatial calculations, and the ones we're going to use are the MAKEPOINT function. Now what this does is this creates a spatial object when we give it a particular latitude and longitude, which we've got in our data called destination latitude, destination longitude. So let's call this Destination
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/create-lines-to-connect-locations-on-a-map?u=76281980&t=95)** and it's going to be MAKEPOINT, and we give it, first of all, the latitude, so this is going to be our destination latitude, and then the destination longitude. Hit apply, and we've got a new calculation, and we see that this is the globe icon. So this tells us that this is a spatial object. If we drag this into our view, Tableau's going to create a map from it. So let's go ahead and do the same thing with the origin points. We call this one Origin. This is going to be the origin of the main LA store, which in our data is MAKEPOINT Origin latitude, and then Origin longitude. Now we have our two points. So if I go to a new sheet, and now if I drag the destination onto the view, it plots all of those for me. What I can then do is I can change the size of these points. >
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/create-lines-to-connect-locations-on-a-map?u=76281980&t=190)** to each of the regional stores. So that's another calculation. So perhaps the calculation editor. This time we're going to create the line that joins 'em together, and this function is called MAKELINE. Now for this, we need to give it two points to join together, and this is going to be our origin point and our destination point we made a moment ago. So we need the origin and the destination. Now we could, if we wanted to have done was write this all in one single calculation. We could have written MAKELINE, and then inside the brackets, MAKEOBJECT lat long, but it looks a little bit complicated. And actually, I prefer doing it in these single steps because it allows me to make different kinds of maps. Maybe I just want to see the line with some of the points, the points without the lines and so on, so it is a little bit more flexible. We actually have them as separate calculated fields. So I can hit OK, and now we have our line object. Now we can make use of the map layers function. Map layers allow us to overlap one map on top of another. It's similar to, I guess, the combined axis bar charts where you drag and drop a second measure onto an existing axis, and then Tableau plots two measures, one on top of the other. It's a similar kind of thing to that. So what I'm going to do is I'm going to take my line and I'm going to drag that onto the map, and now I have all of my lines, but notice when I hover over them, all of them highlight. That's because Tableau draws 'em as one single line.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/create-lines-to-connect-locations-on-a-map?u=76281980&t=286)** We need to break that down. How do we do that? Well, we add a dimension because that's how Tableau breaks single object down to smaller ones at the lower level of detail. So all I need to do is add store onto the detail, and now each of these is independent. Now I've done that, I could then calculate the distances. So again, we go to our calculation, create calculated field, and let's just call this distance, and for this, we're going to use the distance calculation. Now this is going to give us the distance between two points in whatever unit we give it. So let's calculate the distance between the origin and the destination, and we'll do it in kilometers. Hit OK, and now I have a number which I can then put onto my tool tip for both the line and also my circle destination. So if I hover over, I can see that Santa Barbara is 139 kilometers away from LA, where San Gabriel is just 13.4. If hover over the line, I get the same detail as I do if I hover over the points. That's why I put the distance value on both the destination and the line marks card. So that's how spatial calculations work. We take the existing data where we have the latitude and the longitude, and we turn it into a spatial object.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/create-lines-to-connect-locations-on-a-map?u=76281980&t=380)** Once we've got that, we can then create the calculations, we can draw lines between them. This is kind of the reverse of how we traditionally do the mapping, whereas before, when we've done maps, we drag the city name, and then Tableau looks up the name of the city in its inbuilt database and finds the latitude, longitude, and then plots the point. In this case, we're doing slightly reverse, that we've got the lat and long and we're saying to Tableau create the spatial object and then add that to the map. We can see that because, if you notice on the rows and columns, we're using the generated latitude and longitude, not the one that was in our data. Tableau's spatial calculations are really useful whenever we do anything to do with maps. It allows us to enhance the data and add new things to it to create much more interactive and useful maps.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

So if I was to look at say, 40 miles, I can see that 10 of my stores, which represents 33% of my stores are 40 miles away from LA. This spatial calculation enables to answer questions. It just wouldn't be possible to do just from our raw data. But Tableau understands how to change values into geographic data and spatial objects to answer questions of our data.

### 8 . The Analytics Tab

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [What is the Analytics pane?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-is-the-analytics-tab?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-is-the-analytics-tab?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) not only makes it really easy to visualize our data, it's increasingly making it easier to analyze it as well, giving us drag and drop analytics when we need it. Now, this is very simple to do, because this is how Tableau tries to everything to make it simple as possible. But what is the analytics tab and what do we do with it? Well, if we build a simple chart, we use just drag and drop. So we drag the fields that we are interested in to build a chart. I put the sales on one axis, I put the category on another, and I get a bar chart, because that's the rules that Tableau has. But if I want to go one step further, I might need to do some further analysis on my visualization after I've created it. And this is where the Analytics tab comes in. Now we can find it at the top of the data pane, and in fact, you probably didn't even notice it was there. If we click on Analytics, it changes to the analytics pane on the left hand side. These are secondary analytics that we apply once we've built our view. Now that's really important to understand, and it's actually why it's on a separate tab. Previously, anything that we've done within the data tab, we've added or modified a column in our data. We've either changed the values of it. We've maybe set the default values. We might have created the calculation which creates an extra field, which in a sense, creates an extra column within our data set. Now, all the time we've done that,
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-is-the-analytics-tab?u=76281980&t=92)** we've done that within the data pane. This time we're looking at the analytics pane. So this gives you some understanding of where the analytics happens. The data pane contains all of our raw data, plus all of our modified data that we then drag and drop into our view. Now, the combination of the rows and the columns, the measures, dimensions, discrete, continuous builds the kind of view. We can control what the marks look like with the marks card. Now, once we've got that view built, we can then drag and drop the analytics onto it. So that's why they're separated out, to show that we build the views and the data, then we analyze the view using the analytics. So the analytics part comes at the end of that process. Once we've created our view, we can then start adding the analytics to it. For example, we might want to say, what is the average sales across all of our data? Now, I can't do that for my original data. I could create a calculation, but I want to see it visually. So what Tableau allows us to do is drag and drop things like an average line. I could take the average line out of my analytics pane and I could drop it over the entire table of data. Tableau then draws a line against the sales axis to tell me that the average sales for all of my data is 136,000. Now, that value is dependent on what data I've included as part of my view.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-is-the-analytics-tab?u=76281980&t=185)** If I filtered out some of that data, that average is going to take that into account. Depending on what measures and dimensions I have in the view, this could change the kind of analytical answers that I get. This is because anything that we add from the analytics tab happens after the view has been created. In a way, they are versions of, or very similar to table calculations that we've looked at in the calculations chapter. These happen after the view has been built. So after everything else has been taken into account, once we have an axis, we can then add the reference line. Now, this is because reference lines are attached to the axis. But we can also add things such as totals and subtotals. That could happen with either a bar chart such as this, or maybe with a table. Once we've created our view, we can also do things like modeling and [Forecasting](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Forecasting.md). This allows us to look at our data, but only after we've visualized it, because we need something to look at. We can't create a trend line or a forecast for this particular data type, because it just doesn't make sense. You can't create a forecast based on a bar chart. The forecast and the trend line need a timeline to work with. So we can only add some of these analytics when we have the correct view. This is another indication to show that the analytics happens after we've created our initial visualization. For more detail on how to create each one of these analytics, check out the further videos in this chapter.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/what-is-the-analytics-tab?u=76281980&t=278)** Remember, we have to create a visualization first, then we can drag and drop the analytics on top of it to further understand and see what's happening with our data.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) (5), [Forecasting](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Forecasting.md) (1)
> **UI Navigation:** drag and drop (6), click on (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (2), for example (1), similar to (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2), find (1)

#### [How to create box plots](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-box-plots?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-box-plots?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Box plots or box and whisker plots are a good chart to use to show the distribution of data for a particular measure. They show ranges of that data, they show half of the data in a box, and they show things about the upper lower quartile and the distribution of that data across an axis. Now to create a box plot, we have to overlay it on some data because it's a reference line and reference lines are drawn on a view after that view has been created. So first, let's create a chart and then what overlay a box plot on top of it and see how it allows us to answer some questions on that data. So what I'm going to look at on here is I'm going to look at regional sales broken down by subcategory. So I want to know for which region do certain subcategory perform better than others. So first we'll put region onto our column and we'll look at the amount of sales, and then I'm going to add the subcategory to the level of detail on the marks card. Now what this does is it breaks each of those bars into individual segments, one for each of those subcategories. But what I want to do is actually plot each of these subcategories against that sales axis by themself. And I actually want to create a circle for each one rather than stacking them in a stack bar chart. So to do that, we go to the marks card
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-box-plots?u=76281980&t=97)** and change from automatic to a circle, and I'm just going to put a border around the circle and I'm just going to change the opacity down slightly so we can see that there are actually circles behind the circles. And then we'll change it to fit the entire view. Just make a bit better use of the space. So now I can see for each of my regions how each of those categories and subcategories have performed. But which one is performed the best? What is the average amount of sales for each region across all of those subcategories? Well, this is where the box and whisker plot comes in because we can see there's some kind of different distributions going on. The west, for example, looks like it's all kind of stretched out. There's very few data points that are overlapping, except in this cluster towards the bottom where there's quite a few overlapping. Whereas with the south, the highest value is about half the value of the west. And we can see, because we've turned the opacity down, there's lots of data points overlapping. Now that would suggest that the average sales for south is probably lower than the west, but that's just a guess. I need to actually see that to fully understand it. So let's go to our analytics pane. So we click on the top left on analytics, and we have the box plot.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-box-plots?u=76281980&t=191)** So I'm going to add this to my view by just dragging and dropping it onto the view. And now [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) has drawn the box plot reference on top for me. Now what this represents is the distribution of the data. So in effect, it's pushing all of those individual circles behind and doing some distribution analysis for us and visualizing the results. The boxes represent 50% of the data. So contained within the two gray boxes are 50% of all of the sales for the eastern region. The median, that's the intersection between the light and the dark gray box is the median sales for the eastern region across all of those subcategories. So we can see that the average sales for the east and west are quite close, but we can see that the overall distribution in the 50% in terms of the upper and lower quartiles, is actually higher on the west than it is with the east. And we thought earlier that the south probably on average had lower sales, and we can see that the average for the southern region is actually 17,000, whereas for the west it was 36,000. The box plot gives you an idea of what's happening behind the data. In the case of these points where I've plotted them, it's literally hiding some of the data behind. For example, in all of these cases, whether it's the east,
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-box-plots?u=76281980&t=288)** south, west or central region, there are 17 individual data points. But in the case of something like the south where you have all these data points clustered around the bottom, it's hard to see what's going on. The box and whisker plot pushes that data to the back and shows us just the analysis of those results. Now in this situation, I've only got 17, but we could have 17,000 data points in there. And we just want to look at the top level averages, and that's what the box and whisker plot really lets us do easily.

#### [Adding totals and sub-totals to a view](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/adding-totals-and-sub-totals-to-a-view?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/adding-totals-and-sub-totals-to-a-view?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There's one type of analytics that you've been using without even knowing about it, and that's grand totals and subtotals. Now, why are they part of the analytics tab? Well, all the analytics in the analytics tab, things like reference lines, box plots, and so on, are secondary kind of calculations that have to happen after the view has been built. When we put a reference line into our view, it attaches itself to an axis, and an axis can only exist when we put a green field on the row or the column. So before that point, we can't do that analysis. Totals are very much the same thing. It depends on what the view looks like as to where we can put totals and what we can actually compute those totals by. So let's look at a couple of examples. First, we'll have a look at a bar chart, and we'll add totals and subtotals to that chart. So let's build one using sales. So we'll put that on our column and we'll put profit next to it as well, and let's break this down, first of all, by the category and then the subcategory, and I'm going to change to using time view to fill up all of the available space. Let's put better color on. Let's color it by the category so we can see our three distinct categories. Now, if I want to know what the subtotal is for the furniture [office](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Microsoft%20Office.md) supplies and technologies, I've got two options.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/adding-totals-and-sub-totals-to-a-view?u=76281980&t=91)** I could either hover over and select all of the values for that particular category, and then the hover over it tells me what the total sum of sales is, and I could change that to look at maybe the average or whatever I wanted to do, but actually I want to be able to see the subtotal. So how can we add that from the analytics pane? Well, it's quite simple, really. We go to totals in the summarize section, and watch as I drag it into the view. We have a couple of options pop up. Now, do I want to see the subtotals, the common grand totals? I can't see the row grand totals, because we haven't got a measure on the row, so that's why that's grayed out. So let's pick the subtotals. And there we go. I've now got the subtotals for furniture, office supplies, and technology for both the sales and the profit. Now, if I wanted to add the grand totals, I would do it in exactly the same way. I can go to totals and I can drag on the column grand totals. If course, because the grand total is the final, final big total, we only have one single bar at the bottom of the screen for the grand total. Slight drawback with this is that anytime you use a bar chart and you put grand totals on, inevitably that's going to be much, much bigger than all of your other values. So it is going to have the effect of shrinking all of these down, and if you look in the office supplies in particular where we have the low value items in art, envelopes, fasteners, and labels, they're very, very flat.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/adding-totals-and-sub-totals-to-a-view?u=76281980&t=188)** We lose a lot of that detail because the axis is scaled to fit the grand total. So that's one of the slight disadvantages of those grand totals. If you want to see the grand total, by all means, go and do it, but just be aware that you're going to lose a lot of the detail, so really check that's exactly what you want to do. And the other place you often want to see subtotals is in crosstabs. So let's build a crosstab. So let's start by putting our sales into the middle of our view to get our numeric value. So that's using the text mark, and I'm going to subdivide that down by the category, and then we'll do the subcategory onto the row as well. And let's put, let's say, region onto the column. So now I have a nice simple table. I could color it as well by maybe the sales so that now my text is colored by that sales value. Now I want to put my subtotals on. Again we go to the analytics tab and I can drag totals and I could look at all of the subtotals. So if I put subtotals, I now have the subtotal for furniture, the subtotal for office supplies, and the subtotal for technology. Now notice on here, the subtotals aren't colored, they're grayed out. That's quite useful, because obviously these values are much higher than the individual values for the subcategory in the central region.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/adding-totals-and-sub-totals-to-a-view?u=76281980&t=281)** But by putting 'em in the gray scale, rather than taking any of that blue color, it doesn't change the scaling of that blue. So let's go ahead and add the column grand totals and the row grand totals by dragging the totals and dropping it on the row and the column. Again, we can see that they are colored gray to show that they're not part of that color scheme. We've then got the row totals, the column totals, and the grand, grand total total in the bottom right hand corner. Now for this case, if you did want to color the grand totals as well, the option to do that is if you click on the color on the marks card. Click on edit colors. There's an option to say include totals. Now if I add that and click apply, look what happens. Everything is now scaled between the lowest value of sales, 503, and the highest number in the entire view, which is that grand total of 2.3 million. Now, that's pretty big range, and what's happened is that all of the detail and nuance that we did have for the color has now been lost because of such a high range. So for this particular example in this particular layout, including the color in the total is probably not a particularly good idea. That's why it's an option to turn it on and off.
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/adding-totals-and-sub-totals-to-a-view?u=76281980&t=376)** Grand totals and row totals can be really useful summary information. It's why it's in the summarize section of the analytics tab. By simply dragging and dropping them in, we can perform that secondary calculation to get extra information. Now remember, the grand totals, the subtotals are not part of that data. We can't use it in a calculation or we can't make reference to it. The calculation only occurs after that view has been made, and it's going to depend on the layout and the positioning of our measures and dimensions, and that's going to change those numbers that are going to appear in those totals.

#### [Adding a forecast to a view](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/adding-a-forcast-to-a-view?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/adding-a-forcast-to-a-view?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the types of analysis that we sometimes need to do is predict what might happen in the future. Now to do this, [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md)'s got some inbuilt [Forecasting](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Forecasting.md) tools that we can apply to our view. Now, much like the other analytics in this section, we need to apply that analytics after we've created the view, because Tableau's going to use what is inside the view, the measures, the dimensions, the level of detail, in order to perform the calculations for the forecasting function. So to have a look at that, we're going to build a timeline, and then we're going to use some [Predictive Analytics](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Predictive%20Analytics.md) to see if we can figure out what might happen in the future. So first let's create our timeline. So let's look at sales over time. So I'm going to drag order date using the right mouse click or option click drag, and we will select, let's look at the monthly. And we'll put sales into our rows. Now from here we can see that we looks like we have some kind of cyclical data. We have some sort of seasonal trend going on where we have some peaks in the end of the year, and then we have some dips in the summer months. But overall, it looks like our sales are increasing. The peaks on year on year seem to be getting higher and higher. Now we expect that that trend should probably continue, all things being equal. So let's see what kind of forecasting Tableau can make
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/adding-a-forcast-to-a-view?u=76281980&t=94)** based on that underlying data. So let's go to our analytics tab, and we're looking in the model section at forecast. So let's drag forecast into our view, and there we go. Tableau's extended our line out, showing that the data is an estimate of what Tableau thinks might happen based on the previous. So we can see our data ends on December, 2024, and restarts in January, 2025. So Tableau has carried on that data for us to show that what it thinks the estimate is for December, and how that's going to carry on through 2025. And we can see that it's done a pretty good job of spotting this seasonal trend, and it's carrying it on for us. Now, if we want to go in a bit more detail, we can do. If we right click on the estimated line, go down to forecast, we can click on Describe Forecast. And that gives us some information of how Tableau's calculated that forecast for us, what timelines it's used, what its forecast is based on, and anything it's ignoring. It's chosen the next 13 months, but I could change it if I wanted to. What about if you want to look at the next two years? What do we think that's going to happen?
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/adding-a-forcast-to-a-view?u=76281980&t=186)** Now notice that with the estimate, we've got some error bars in here, that blue shaded part. As we get further and further into the future, those error bars get wider and wider to show that the forecast is getting not quite as accurate as time goes on. That's quite a nice warning for us. We can also change the aggregation, and we can change the forecast model as well. So we could change the forecast to be a custom. Maybe we want the trend to be additive as well as the season. So we can modify the forecast to see what that data's going to look like. And with these two options picked, the additive one for both trend and season, we can see that it looks like a pretty good match to our data. We can still see that seasonal trend going on for the next two years, but that overall growth also looks to be going upwards. All that analytics, all that calculation, all that tricky [Statistics](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Statistics.md) is being done for us. All we had to do was drag and drop. That's the beauty of the analytics pane. I didn't write a single line of code. I didn't have to get involved with any kind of numeric calculations. I simply dropped the forecast onto the view and Tableau did the [rest](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Representational%20State%20Transfer%20(REST).md) for me. We had a little fiddle around with things to make it look slightly closer to what we think it should look like based on the underlying data, but Tableau does all the hard work for us to get the answers that we need with our data.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

#### [Adding a trend line to a view](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/adding-a-trend-line-to-a-view?u=76281980)

We can also use it to make predictions using models. So to do that, we're going to look at creating trend lines. The trend lines need two things. They need an access and they need something to reference it by. So the common use of a trend line is either something like a timeline where we're looking for changes over time, or we're looking for correlation typically within say, a scatterplot. So to build that, first of all, let's create a timeline. So I'm going to take my order date and I'm going to put it onto my row and let's look at the quarter order date. Tableau takes all of the data in this view into account, performs a calculation, then draws that trend line for me. But if I select a subset of those values, it will recalculate that for me. But this can be quite useful. For example, this is showing the overall trend of all of my data, but I want to know, well, what's it been like for the last two years or so? > **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/adding-a-trend-line-to-a-view?u=76281980&t=188)** I can then see that Tableau's recomputed that trend line just for those highlighted data. Again, I'm just going to go for the linear trend line, and I can see that the relationship between the sales and the profit generates this kind of line. Now similarly, if I select a subset of these points, say I want to look at these ones, it recalculates that trend line for me

> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/adding-a-trend-line-to-a-view?u=76281980&t=281)** so I can see the correlation between sales and profit for the selected set. If I have ever select a subset of those values, I can now see all of those four lines have been recomputed to show the correlation just for those selected data points. So trend lines are kind of an amalgamation of the underlying statistics and the dynamic reference line. They recompute depending on what we've selected. They can give an overall picture of what's happening with the data, or in the terms of the timeline, what could happen should that trend continue.

#### [Creating a reference band](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-reference-band?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-reference-band?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Reference lines are secondary calculations that will draw a line on an existing chart. Now, they can be really useful if you are looking to check to see if you've reached or exceeded a target. And a reference band is very similar, but instead of being one single line, it's actually two lines with a colored region in between. This could be really useful if you're tracking changes over time, for example, and you want to see if something has reached or exceeded a particular target range. To do that, we're going to have a look at creating a reference band in a couple of different scenarios. Firstly, we'll create a bar chart and we'll create a reference band to look at some values. So let's build a bar chart. This time we'll look at our sales per category and subcategory, and let's put category onto the color. Now my sales are across 17 different subcategories and I have some high value [products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md) and I have some low value products, and I have some color ones in the middle. Now let's say we have three different sales teams. One team is interested in the lower end of the products, one is interested in the higher end. We have this group in the middle. I just want to focus on my sales that are within 100 to 200,000, okay? That's the area that I'm interested in.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/creating-a-reference-band?u=76281980&t=93)** Now, I could filter those out, but then I lose the context of seeing all the other bars. What I want to be able to do is have an area that is shaded in between the 100,000 and the 200,000. So I can focus in on the categories and subcategories that I'm interested in. ### 9. Using Parameters for Viewer Input

#### [How are parameters different compared to filters?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-are-parameters-different-to-filters?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-are-parameters-different-to-filters?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One thing that seems to confuse a lot of people is how do parameters work, and how are they different to say a filter? Let's look at an example where we are using a parameter in a similar way as a filter to try and understand the subtle differences, and then we'll look at what a parameter actually is and how to create one. Now, in this chart I'm looking at the subcategories and the profit, and we've got color on the profit. And I've got two filters. I have a SUM of Profit filter, and I have this Aggregate Profit filter, which is a calculation. Now, on the right-hand side I have my color legend, but I have two controls here. I have my SUM of Profit slider, and I have an Input slider as well set to 100,000. So, the inputs look quite similar. They're both sided, they're taking some input from the user. Now, when I change the values in the Profit filter the view changes. As I set the upper and lower boundaries for that filter it's excluding some of that data, and then the visualization updates. So, the minimum and the maximum of these values is minus 17,000 and it's 56,000. That was decided on by [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md). Tableau looked at the visualization,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-are-parameters-different-to-filters?u=76281980&t=95)** worked out in that view what is the highest value of sales and set that as the highest value for my parameter, and found the lowest value for the profit and set that as the value for the profit. So, these numbers are based on our sales data. They're looking at the two extremes within the view. Now, if we look at this other input this is saying 100,000, but I have a slider. And as I make changes, nothing happens. So, we keep making the change, and now suddenly something's happened. As I keep making these changes data's been excluded and filtered out. So, this is kind of behaving a bit like a filter, so what's the difference? Well, the difference is is that the parameter is taking the input that I give it, but I'm controlling what that input is. Remember how a filter works? The filter, if we look at the Filter shelf, sits in between the data and our view, and you can literally think of it as that funnel. All of the data exists in our Data pane. We put the filters on the Filter shelf, and any data that is left after it's been through that filter can then be visualized. What the parameter does is slightly different. A parameter doesn't have to be based on any of the existing data. The filter does because the filter can only work with the data it's got. What a parameter is is a way to take input from the user
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-are-parameters-different-to-filters?u=76281980&t=191)** and then do something with it. Now, what we do with it kind of depends on what we want to do. In this case I'm using it as a filter, but there's loads of other applications that we can use parameters for. If I go and edit this existing parameter, which is called Input and lives at the bottom of the Data pane, we can see how this was set up. When we create a parameter we have to tell Tableau the kind of input that we want, and that is from the data type. We can have a floating or an integer, have a string or a Boolean, or a date. Now, the input kind of depends on what we want to do with that input in terms of a calculation or drive the behavior that we expect from Tableau. Now, in this case I wanted to take an integer value. Now, I also then wanted to control those possible values. I could have left all allowable values, but in this case I wanted a range of values. I set a minimum of 5,000, a maximum of 100,000, and then steps of 5,000. And that's why my Input selector is a slider. And if we look it goes from 60 to 65 to 70 to 85 and onwards. So, the input, we can control the type of thing that we have coming into this. Now, that's all well and good, but we have to do something with that input.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-are-parameters-different-to-filters?u=76281980&t=283)** And this is what tricks a lot of people up. They'll create the parameter, they'll add it to their view, they'll start changing the values, and then nothing actually happens. The reason for that is is you have to tell Tableau to do something with that extra input. Remember, filters are already working on the data, so anytime we make a change to the filter it's actually affecting the data there and then because the data has to come from the Data pane, through the filter, into the view. But the parameter we apply to the data. So, in my data I had to create a calculation, and this one was called Profit Filter. And it's a very simple calculation that says is the SUM of Profit less than the value of Input? If it is that's true, and if it's not it's false. Then I add that to my filter, and I'm only interested in times when that is true. So, as I change my Input slider, at 75,000 that is greater than all of the current values so that is true. And as I move it down the calculation then says actually this sum of sales is greater or less than that value, true or false, only show the ones where that calculation is true. So, in this instance my parameter is behaving like a filter, but only because of that calculation. To see how else we can use parameters
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-are-parameters-different-to-filters?u=76281980&t=378)** look at some of the other videos in this chapter. Remember, a parameter is a way of creating an input to the user. The type of input could be a text box where somebody could type something in. It could be a slider where they're allowed to select only a set of values. It might be an input that only allows 'em to select something that already exists within the data, which would be a kind of dynamic parameter, or it could be a date. The way that we set those parameters up depends on what we're going to do with it. We always have to use that parameter somehow in a calculation somehow to get it into that view. If not, you're going to have a parameter control that doesn't do anything. Remember, parameters are a way of getting input from the user. It allows greater interaction and allows manipulation of both the data and the view itself.

#### [How to use parameters in a calculation](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-parameters-in-a-calculations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-parameters-in-a-calculations?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One particular use for a parameter is to use input and use it to drive a calculation to do what if analysis. Now in that case, what we're doing is we're creating an input. We're allowing a widget to appear on the screen that our user can interact with. They could type something in, they could move a slider, they could select a value, and then that value is then passed through into a calculation, which can then be used to drive our visualization. So let's have a look at this in practice. So I've got a simple chart here that's just showing the sum of sales broken down by the region. Now maybe I want to see what would a 10% increase in sales look like? Well, I could do that by creating a calculated field based on the existing value of sum of sales. So let's do that. So if we go to our Data pane and go to Create Calculated Field, and let's call this % Change Sales. Now, my calculation is going to be based on the existing summer sales value, which is what we're seeing in our view. And if we multiply that by 1.1, that's going to be a 10% increase on that sale value. If I want to see that, I could either put it onto the column, so I see it side by side. But you'll notice that because [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md)'s scaling the axis, the actual bars themselves look identical. So I'm actually going to make a bar in bar chart. So let's take the measure off of the column,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-parameters-in-a-calculations?u=76281980&t=93)** drag it onto the existing sales axis, and then we get a combined axis chart, so both of my measures are on the same axis. To understand a bit more about that, check out the comparing measures chapter and the video on combined axis charts. So now I can see the difference between my original sales and my 10% increase. Well, what if somebody wants to look at a 15% increase, or a 17, or a 23% increase? I don't have to go and edit that calculation every single time, and I certainly don't want to have to have a calculation that covers every possible value. Instead, what we want to be able to do is allow our user to, say, type in a particular value, and for that to be passed through to that calculation. So there's a couple of steps we have to do in that case. First we have to create the parameter to accept that input. Now the parameters get created in the Data pane. So right click on the caret and Create Parameter. Now, parameter has got several different data types. We can have floating or integers, both numeric values, we can a string of text, Boolean, true or false, or a date or date time. In my case, I just want to accept a whole number, so I'm going to set that to be an integer. And let's call the name % increase, and we'll start with the current value of 0, so that's going to be a 0% increase.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-parameters-in-a-calculations?u=76281980&t=188)** And we'll allow all values, so this is going to create a text box where somebody can type in whatever they like. So we'll set that, we'll right-click on our new parameter that's appeared at the bottom of the Data pane, click Show Parameter, and now I have my control in here. And if I change that, nothing happens. Okay, so why has nothing happened? Well, this often trips people up when they're working with parameters, they create the parameter and expect it to do something. Well, it won't because we've not told Tableau what to do with that inputted value. All we've done essentially is create this little widget on the right-hand side. The value itself, nothing's doing with it so we need to add that to our calculation. So let's go to our Data pane, I'm going to right-click on our % Change Sales calculation, and I'm going to modify this calculation to accept the value from our percentage increase parameter and do something with it. Now, in order to change that percentage increase into an actual percentage, we need to divide it by 100 and then add it to 1. That will be the equivalent of a percentage increase calculation. So let's get rid of that part of our calculation. Don't worry about the error messages that comes up, that's because the syntax is not correct, but we'll fix that as we fix the calculation. So first of all, let's take our percent increase parameter.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-parameters-in-a-calculations?u=76281980&t=283)** So as I start typing, % increase, you can select it. Now parameters are colored purple so we know we've got the correct one. We're going to divide this value by 100. So then I'll put that in parentheses, and then I'll do 1+, and I'll put that in parentheses as well. So what the calculation is going to do now, it's going to take the value of % increase, which is the input parameter value, it's going to divide it by 100, then it's going to add it to 1, and then this whole number is then going to be multiplied by the summer sales. So if I do OK, we can see it. Now these two bars are identical because % increase is 0. So if we had a 10% increase, we can see that the % Change Sales has increased. If I did 100% increase, we can see that the two values are now twice as big. I could turn on the labels to make it clearer to see the actual values, and I can put any value that I like in here to see what would happen. So that's how we add a parameter into a calculation. You have to first create the parameter with the type of data input that you want, and then you can refer to that in a calculation. So we could create it as a filter.
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-parameters-in-a-calculations?u=76281980&t=376)** We could create it as a calculation here that's actually driving the visualization itself. It could be an input to a calculation, which is then used in another calculation and another one and another one. By allowing Tableau to make use of the values in the parameter, it means that we can just create one single chart. But this chart can be used by lots of people who want to look at varying different increases. That's great because we only have to build one chart, maintain one chart, but it makes it a multipurpose chart that can be used by more than one person.

#### [Create dynamic reference lines using parameters](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/create-dynamic-reference-lines-using-parameters?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/create-dynamic-reference-lines-using-parameters?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Reference lines are secondary calculations and lines that you can draw on a vis after it's been created. Now, one of the things that's quite good about a reference line is that you can set it on an axis as a kind of guide or a measure so you can see how well are we doing? Have we hit a certain value or we hit a certain target? So it's quite good for monitoring sort of progress or targets towards a goal. And we can add a constant reference line from the analytics pane, so if I click on the analytics tab, we have a constant line, which is a constant reference line. If I drag it into my view, I can add it to the table, and it'll ask me for the value that I want to set it to. So say if I set it to 120,000, it draws a reference line for me there, but that reference line is static. It's never going to move. It's never going to change. It's a fixed point. No matter what happens with the data underneath, that reference line isn't going to change, but what if the thing I'm interested in changes? Right now I'm interested in that 120,000 figure, but next week I might be interested in 180. One of my colleagues might want to look at something that's at 200 or 80,000. How do we handle that? Does that mean I have to have multiple charts, each with a line at each of those different values? Do I have to try and preempt what somebody wants to do? Well, no. In that case, what we can make use of are the parameters.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/create-dynamic-reference-lines-using-parameters?u=76281980&t=94)** So parameters are a way to take user inputs and then drive what's happening within our visualization and our dashboard. One of the things we can use that for is dynamic reference lines, and when I say dynamic reference lines, I mean ones that we can control and move ourselves, not dynamic lines that are beholden on the data underneath. They're still going to be fixed, but we're choosing when to fix it. So if I get rid of this reference line, and we'll create a new one, and this one is going to refer to the value within a parameter. So first, let's actually create the parameter to take that input so we can then reference it later on. So we're going to go to our data pane. We're going to click on the top right hand corner and do create parameter. So let's call this sales reference, and we need to decide what kind of input parameter this should be. Should it be a float, integer string, boolean, date or time? Now the one that you pick should relate to what you're going to do with that. So in our scenario, I want to draw a line on the sales axis, 'cause a reference line always has to be attached to an axis, and that means it's going to go on the sales axis, which is a number. So it's either going to a float or an integer. Now see as I'm talking about some big numbers, you know, we're talking in the tens or hundreds of thousands here,
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/create-dynamic-reference-lines-using-parameters?u=76281980&t=187)** I probably don't need the precision of a float, so we'll just use it as an integer. I then got to decide, okay, how fine grained do I let people add that reference line out? Now we could allow them to type in any value that they like, so that's going to live in an empty field that they can type in what they want. We could set a list of allowable values, so we could add in certain values that they could select, or we could have a range. So we could have an upper and lower range, and then have a slider that they could select to move it across. Well, let's go for the range because that'll make quite a nice little widget that they can interact with. So let's set the minimum value of what our minimum value of sales is currently, which is around about 5,000, okay? So let's set the minimum at 5K. We'll set the maximum at say 400K, so that gives a little bit of headroom. So at the moment, our biggest sales is around about 350,000. So that will allow for a little bit more growth, and the step size will allow it to go up in increments of say, 5,000, so change that to 5K.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/create-dynamic-reference-lines-using-parameters?u=76281980&t=264)** Okay, so now we do OK, and I've got my sales reference parameter. So now let's do show parameter, and we have my little widget. There's a little slider where I can move the values up and down. Now at the moment, that parameter isn't doing anything at all, because we're not using that input in any way. All we've done is literally create that widget, so we have to tell [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) to do something with that. Now I want to create a custom reference line that's going to use that value. So what we do is we go to the axis that we want to attach that reference line to, and in our case, we've only got one axis, so it has to be the sales axis, and we click add reference line. That brings up the editor dialogue box, and I'm going to select the entire table because I'm going to create a line across the entire table. Now at the moment, the line is the average sum of sales, and we can see that by the gray line right in the middle of the view. Well, I don't want it to be that. I want it to be based on the sales reference. So what I can do is I can click on the dropdown and we see my sales reference parameter is there. Now, if I didn't want to use the sales reference, I wanted to create a new one, I click on create new parameter and do the process we did a moment ago where we created the parameter, but we did it upfront. Just put these. So now the line is now based on my value. So now we see a reference line down at the 5K. Now by default, the reference line label is the computation.
>
> **[5:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/create-dynamic-reference-lines-using-parameters?u=76281980&t=359)** Well, I don't want to know what the computation is because I know it's the sales reference. It's just calling the name of the parameter. So let's change the label value instead. So now it says 5,000. I can see what my reference line actually refers to. I could make the line a different color or make it bolder, make it bigger, make it more prominent, whatever I want to do to make it stand out, and we can do a fill above and below that as well. So if I put a gray fill above, I could put a darker gray field below it if I wanted to, just to emphasize as well what's going on. And now as I move my slider, my reference line and my shaded areas change. So this means that I can look at everything that's at 80,000. Somebody else would come along and look at everything that's 165,000, and so on. The advantage of having a parameter is that we have one single dashboard that has multiple uses. I can look at it in the way that I want to and somebody else can look at it in the way that they want to, but from our point of view, as authors, we have one dashboard to maintain, one to keep up to date, and only one that we ever have to edit, and that really is the true power of a parameter.
