#### [Use a parameter to select dimensions and measures](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/use-a-parameter-to-select-dimensions-and-measures?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/use-a-parameter-to-select-dimensions-and-measures?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One really good use case for parameters is allowing us to change the measures and the dimensions that are being showed in our visualization. Now, why would we want to do that? What would be the reason for it? Well, in this example, I'm looking at summer sales broken down by category. But what if I want to see the summer sales broken down by the region or by the profit? Well, I can do that because I, as an editor, can go into [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) and I could just swap the profit for the sales. And now I'm looking at the profit broken down by the category. But when we publish this, our users don't have access to the whole data pane. They just have access to the view and the filter controls, and the parameter controls. So instead, what we do is we create two parameters, one to take a measure name, one to take a dimension name, and then we can say to Tableau, display these in the view rather than the set ones that we had. We turn the whole dashboard into a dynamic, selectable dashboard. Now, that's a great thing because it means we have one single dashboard with multiple uses rather than having to have a dashboard for a particular measure or dashboard for sales, a dashboard for profit, and so on. It's just a pain to have to have that many things to edit and maintain, and keep up to date. So how do we go about doing this? Well, there's a couple of steps. First step is we need to get the input from our user. For that, we need to create two parameters, one for the measure, one for the dimension.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/use-a-parameter-to-select-dimensions-and-measures?u=76281980&t=93)** Now, parameters get created within the data pane. So, we'll go to the top of the data pane, click on the caret, and create parameter. So, we get our parameter dialog box come up. Let's call this measure selector. And this is going to be a string. We click a string input. And we're going to select a list of allowable values. So, in our drop-down list, they can either select sales or profit. Click OK. We can add this to our view by doing show parameter on a right click. And I have sales or profit. Then nothing happens because we haven't said to Tableau what to do with that parameter input yet. We'll come to that in a second. Let's create our second parameter first. So, this is going to be our dimension selector.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/use-a-parameter-to-select-dimensions-and-measures?u=76281980&t=158)** And we'll make this a string as well. We'll have the list so they can pick from, say, category and then maybe region, and segment. And we'll give them three options. Again, let's right click show parameter. Now we have our selector. Let's change this to a single value list. We'll do the same with our measure. So now I've got my three dimensions I can select. And I've got my sales profit I can select as well. So far, they're not doing anything. They're just taking input, but we're not doing anything with that input. What we need to do is create two calculations, one for the measure, one for the dimension, to say, if this value is selected, then display that value in the view. So, let's create the first calculation for our measure. So, we'll go to create calculated field. And I'm going to call this one selected measure. Doesn't have to have the same name as the parameter. But it's probably useful to have a similar-ish name so you know they're kind of related. It just helps sometimes when you come to look at an older dashboard to figure out what you did. Now we're going to use a case statement here, which is a logic type statement. To understand more about logic calculations, check out the chapter on logic calculations. So, the case statement is going to check
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/use-a-parameter-to-select-dimensions-and-measures?u=76281980&t=250)** to see what value has been selected by that parameter. So, we tell Tableau to in the case for the measure selector. And the syntax is, when the measure selector is sales, then the value that we want to apply to our selected measure calculation is sales. When the measure selector is profit, then we want to select the profit value. As we've only got those two options, we then put an end. Now it's really important that you make sure that the values that are being passed, so in this case, sales and profit from the measure selector widget, exactly match what you've written in your calculation. Otherwise, the calculation isn't going to work. Sometimes it's easier to use a numeric calculation instead, but in our case, sales and profit is quite simple to type in. So, then we have our measure selector, which is in our data pane now. Now, if I drag and drop this on top of our profit, so now we're looking at the selected measure. And as I change between sales and profit, you can see my bars shrink and grow because I'm now looking at the sales rather than the profit. Okay, next step is to do exactly the same thing
>
> **[5:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/use-a-parameter-to-select-dimensions-and-measures?u=76281980&t=344)** with our dimension selector. So, let's go to calculated field. Again, we'll call this selected dimension. Another case statement that's going to look at the dimension selector parameter. In this case, we've got three possible options. So, when it says category, then pass the category field through the calculation. Alternatively, when it says region, then we'll pass the region value. And finally, if it says segment, then we'll pass through the segment, and finish it with an end. So, the three possible values in our selector widget are now within that selected dimension. I can change this now and replace category with our selected dimension. And I'll put it onto color as well. So now, the dimensions that we've added to our rows, and the measures added to our column are calculated values. And as we change the selectors, both the dimension and the measures change depending on what we're looking at. Now we could add more, we could have a third measure in here for a dual axis, we could have another dimension, say on color. The parameters allow us to customize the view.
>
> **[7:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/use-a-parameter-to-select-dimensions-and-measures?u=76281980&t=439)** At the point of viewing, we don't have to preempt what somebody's going to want to look at. This means we can make incredibly flexible [Dashboards](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Dashboards.md) to show what's been selected. We can go one step further to make it slightly easier to see what has been selected by double clicking on the title and using the insert option to insert the actual value of the two parameters. So, if I put the measure and then type four, and then the dimension selector, hit OK, we can now see that whatever value we select in our profit or sales is now reflected in our title. So not only can we now select what we want and see what we want, the title is telling us what we've selected.

#### [Use a parameter to search free text fields](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/use-a-parameter-to-search-free-text-fields?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/use-a-parameter-to-search-free-text-fields?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A really useful use case for parameters is the ability to search through text files. Now, one of the, not necessarily the worst data types, but certainly one of the trickiest data types you have to deal with is free text fields. This is when you are allowing somebody to type into a field in your data. Now, it can be really hard to work with because of typos, uppercase, lowercase, punctuation, that sort of thing. But there's an awful lot of good, useful information that can be found in free text that might be useful for your analysis. How can we access and get into that and actually make use of it? Well, one way we can do that is by using pattern matching and searching for free text. Similarly to what you would do in any kind of [Word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) document or Notepad where you can search through looking for particular words or phrase or anything that you can type in, we can build that same kind of functionality within a visualization. So for example, if we were looking at something to do with our customers, and we wanted to find all of the customers that started with a particular name or had a particular phrase within their name, how could we go ahead and do that? Well, we could write a calculation to do that. So we could do something like use the Contains Function. So let's just use Text Filter
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/use-a-parameter-to-search-free-text-fields?u=76281980&t=96)** as our calculation name. And if we did something like contains, so does the customer name contain the letter A? That's going to give us a true or false. And we could put that onto our filter. We could select True, and now I've got only the customers that have the letter A in their name. There's quite a lot of them. Let's change it to something a bit more unique, maybe the letter Q. Okay, one person has the letter Q in their name, and that's fine. But what I want to be able to do is allow our user of our dashboard to type in anything that they like, and that's where the parameter comes in. The parameter is going to give us a text box that somebody can type something in, and we can then pass that value into this calculation, for example. So let's create a parameter to do just that. It's the top of the Data pane. We're going to go to Create Parameter, and let's just call this Text To Find. The data type is going to be a string, but obviously, we could type numbers in if we needed to. And we'll set the Allowable values to All because we want to enable somebody to type in whatever they like. Parameter's created at the bottom of the Data pane. We can right click and then do Show Parameter. And now I have some text in here that I can type anything that I like in here, and that value isn't available for us to use.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/use-a-parameter-to-search-free-text-fields?u=76281980&t=189)** Now at the moment, it doesn't do anything because at no point are we referencing this new parameter, we've only just created it. So if I go back to that Text Filter calculation that we made a moment ago, and I can make a slight modification to it. So the contains function needs two inputs. It needs the string to search through. In our case, it's customer name. And it needs a thing to search for. At the moment, we're saying, "Search for the letter Q." If I get rid of that, and instead use the parameter name itself, what we're saying to [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) is, "Use the contain function, search through customer name using whatever value has been entered into that parameter box to see if you can find it. And if you do, that's true. And if you can't, that's false." So now I can see all customers that have the letter H in their name. If I change it, I can see all the customers that've got a Z in their name. I could put a number of letters in. So this person, Benjamin, has got the word hat in their name, for example. And there's no limitation on how big that text is that we're searching for or how big we're searching through. It's only limited by the actual size of the data itself. This could be really, really useful. If you had, for example, a system where somebody's doing some QC on some [Data Quality](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Data%20Quality.md) and they are typing in some visualization, you know, feelings of what they see on screen
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/use-a-parameter-to-search-free-text-fields?u=76281980&t=283)** when they're doing their QC, and they might be typing in sort of, you know, whatever they think is the problem. It could be that weeks or even months later on, you realize that actually there was a problem with your process. You don't want to go back looking through all of those QC statements and comments that somebody added in to look to see, well, when could we have picked that up? What you could do is you could create a dashboard such as this. You could input all of your data in. You could create that parameter to then search to look for certain phrases. Maybe the problem manifests itself by cloudiness in an image. You could then search through all QC comments where the word cloudy appeared. The input parameter, when it's passed through to a calculation, is really, really useful. To see other ways of doing it, look how we can create dynamic reference lines, how we can create dimensions and measures, and also how we can do top-end analysis. But I really like this one. The ability to actually make use of a really tricky data type, such as free text, is a real bonus when we come to working with parameters in Tableau.

> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/top-n-analysis?u=76281980&t=94)** So that's good. It's always going to be nicely up to date. #### [Dynamic parameters](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/dynamic-parameters?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/dynamic-parameters?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] By far, the most requested feature for [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) for a long, long time was dynamic parameters. Well, what is a dynamic parameter and why are they so useful? Well, let's look at an example of using a parameter to highlight data. And in this example, I'm looking at my [products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md). I've got 1800 products, and we're looking at them across the sales and the profit with the size based on the quantity of each product sold. Now, what I want to know is what relationship the subcategories have with each other. I want to be able to highlight a particular subcategory within all of those data points. Now, I could filter on subcategory, but that's going to remove data. So I definitely don't want to do that. I could put subcategory onto the color, but we've got a lot of subcategories, which means a lot of colors. And even though I could click on the legend on the right-hand side and highlight some of them, it's still far too colorful for what I want. What I want to be able to do is select from a dropdown a name of one of the subcategories, and for just those ones to be highlighted. Well, we can do that with the parameter. So let's create the parameter, then we write the calculation to use that parameter, and then we'll see how dynamic parameters can take it to the next level. So first I'm going to take the color away 'cause we don't need that. And I'm going to go ahead and create a parameter. So we do that from the top of the data pane,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/dynamic-parameters?u=76281980&t=95)** and we'll call this Selected Subcategory. We have our new parameter. I can right click show the parameter control, and now I have a dropdown where I can select any of the subcategories that I'm interested in. And at the moment, that's all it does. It's just a selector. What I need to do is create a calculation to compare the selected value with the subcategory of each of my products. So the top of the data pane, Create Calculated field, and we'll change this to be Highlighted Subcategory.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/dynamic-parameters?u=76281980&t=177)** My calculations can be very simple. It's going to be the value of the parameter equals the subcategory of the product. Answer to that is either yes or no, true or false. Then I can add that to the color, and then I can add the color legend. So let's go to Legends, put the color legend on. >
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/dynamic-parameters?u=76281980&t=274)** with my updated Book value. So what we've done is, effectively we've created a new subcategory within our data. So is Tableau going to notice that? And is it going to pull it into the parameter? Well, let's see what happens. So we've saved it, we've done the export. Now let's refresh my data. Tableau warns me that you're using this data, so if you refresh it, things might change. But that's okay, we want that to happen. Okay, nothing's changed in the view, and okay, there's no Books in my subcategory selector, so let's just check to see if it's actually worked. Sometimes we want to just import the data as of right now when we actually click on it. But there are other times we want it to update dynamically. Now, if we imagine that our sales is going to just carry on building, and building, and building over time, we don't want to have to keep coming back to this dashboard and updating it every time there's a new category. >
> **[6:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/dynamic-parameters?u=76281980&t=369)** to be a dynamic parameter. So instead of being fixed, if we click When the workbook opens, and then select the Sub-Category, notice that Book has suddenly appeared in my list. What's going to happen is every time this workbook is opened, or if it's a published dashboard, every time the dashboard is published, Tableau is going to reevaluate the content of that parameter. It's going to look at the subcategory field, find the up-to-date subcategories that are in there and import them in. Now, if I click on OK and go to our selector, we can see that Book is in there, and if I select it, we find the one solitary Book. It's right there in the bottom. So the dynamic parameter is now looking at the underlying data. This means it's feature-proofed. We get all the advantages for the parameter in which we can drive the behavior, in this case a highlighting calculation. But because it's dynamic, it will update and pull in new values without us having to do a thing.

### 10. Dashboard Actions and Design Tips

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Introducing the dashboard UI](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/introducing-the-dashboard-ui?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/introducing-the-dashboard-ui?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] [Dashboards](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Dashboards.md) are a way to combine one or more sheets into a dashboard where you have full control over the layout and the styling of that visualization. Now, you can create loads of different charts within [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md). In this workbook, I've got dozens of different charts I've created. But in order to display them, I want to put them inside a dashboard. The reason for that is we have better control over what the final product looks like. If we were to publish any of these views just as views, we have no control over how Tableau sizes them, where it places things like legends and filters. It's going to put them where it thinks it's correct. But in the dashboard, we have control. We can put all the elements exactly where we want it so that we know when somebody else views that dashboard what they're going to get. We can make sure that the dashboard is consistent across all different types of devices and different screen sizes, because we have that control. So how do we create a dashboard? Well, first, we click on the new dashboard button. But where is that? Well, there's a couple of places we can find it. It's in the bottom right-hand corner with a little grid with a plus for a new dashboard. Or we can go to the top menu and click on Dashboard, and New Dashboard. Now, this creates a brand new user interface. What this shows is the area in which the dashboard is going to be created. That's the white area in the middle of my view. Now, by default, Tableau picks the best screen size that it thinks it should use.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/introducing-the-dashboard-ui?u=76281980&t=93)** But we have full control over that. On the left-hand side, we have the dashboard pane. This controls things such as the overall dashboard size; it lists the sheets and objects, the elements that we can add into our dashboard. So the first thing we always need to do with a dashboard is to set the sizing, and we can do that from the size part in the menu. Now, you have a whole load of options on what you select for your sizing. Picking the right one is crucial, because you want to make sure that the dashboard is going to look correct. Do you want a long, tall dashboard that somebody's going to scroll through? Do you want it to fit on a single page? Do you want it to be a very wide dashboard? Should it fit on just a laptop size? We can fix the size of the dashboard so that we can ensure that we know exactly how much real estate we've got to play with when we're positioning our elements, but also to make sure that it fits on your viewer's screen. If you don't know how somebody's going to view it, always err on the side of caution. You don't want to make a really wide dashboard, and then people have to view it on a smaller screen and they have to scroll past, 'cause that's just not a nice experience for anybody. Similarly, don't make it so small that it's in really cramped. Remember, visibility is everything. Why spend all your time finding the data, connecting the data, and then cramping it in a small dashboard? Once we set the size, we need to add things to our dashboard. Now, there's two types of things that we can add to our dashboard. One are the visualizations that we've already created. And in the user interface on the left-hand side,
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/introducing-the-dashboard-ui?u=76281980&t=188)** we have a list of all of the sheets in our dashboard. Now, I've got a big long list of sheets. And as I hover over, it gives me a preview of each one. So I can look at the sheet and think, "Yep, that's the one that I want." When I drag that into my view, the gray border appears to show that when I release the mouse button, this is where that sheet is going to go. The sheet's there, and because there was a legend present on the sheet when I created it, I have the legend as well at the top right-hand corner. I can go ahead and add more and more of my sheets, the layout of which is determined whether it's floating or tiled. To understand whether to use floating or tiled and how to create them, check out the chapters where we talk about containers, and then creating the tiled and the floating dashboard. There are other objects that we can also add into a dashboard, such as the containers we've talked about. We can add text boxes, we can add images, we can add blanks, and so on. These should only ever enhance your dashboard. We always make the dashboard easy to understand, because we don't want it to be a barrier to people interacting and seeing their data. So only add exactly what you need to add in order to make the dashboard more user friendly. There's a lot of design elements we can do with a dashboard as well, and some of that comes under the layout tab. Clicking on the layout tab lets us change the position and sizing of some of those elements.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/introducing-the-dashboard-ui?u=76281980&t=281)** Depending on whether you're using tiled or floating, some of these options are going to be grayed out. We can do things like adding borders. If we want to have a particular item to stand out, we could put a border around it. So I could put a border around this scatter plot. Maybe I want to put a red border around it to make sure it's important. We can also change the backgrounds. At the moment, this background for the pie chart is none, but I could give it any background color that I like. Again, only add background colors and borders where it makes sense. Having everything colored or having a border around everything just makes it too complicated. Use them sparingly. And if in doubt, don't use it at all. We can also add padding around our objects. This can be a really powerful tool. A bit of white space gives you room to breathe. Rather than having all of your sheets clustered in together, you can put some gaps around them. If I select the scatter plot, for example, I can put a bigger outer padding. Currently, it's set to 4. But if I change that to 20, we can see there's a white padding that's been put around my chart. Have a play around with the padding and the orientation of your objects. You want to make a good looking dashboard, because that catches the eye and people want to interact with your data. Lastly, we have the item hierarchy. When we drill down, we can see the various containers and objects that are situated in each one.
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/introducing-the-dashboard-ui?u=76281980&t=376)** This can help or sometimes hinder the layer of your dashboard. Experiment and play around. The dashboard is what your end user is going to see. So take the time to make sure that everything in the dashboard is adding to the understanding of your data to make a really good, effective dashboard.

#### [How to create a tiled dashboard](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-tiled-dashboard?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-tiled-dashboard?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Containers help us make nice ordered tiled layouts. If you want precise alignment for both your vises and any other chart elements, the tiled container layout really does help because we can automatically align and distribute objects evenly so that the alignment is pixel perfect. So let's have a look at building a very simple four-sheet dashboard. So I've got four charts. I've got a sales bar chart, a sales line chart, a profit map, and a profit heat map. #### [How to create a floating dashboard](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-floating-dashboard?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-floating-dashboard?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One option we have for creating [Dashboards](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Dashboards.md) are creating floating dashboards. If we were to use a tiled layout, we're constrained with aligning everything to the grid. And in some cases that works really well, but in other cases, it's not necessarily a good use of space. Let's look at an example. So if I switch this dashboard into tiled mode, so I've got a bar chart and a map that I would like to have in that same view. So I'll drag my bar chart in first, and then I need to add my map. Well, if I put my map underneath my bar chart, then I lose a lot of information from my bars. I'm only showing this at the top eight or so states. Okay, well, what happens if I move the map to the right of the bar chart? How does that look? Right, well, I can see more of my states, which is good, but there's an awful lot of white space at the top and bottom of that map. It doesn't size to fit that space. So the grid layout is actually hurting rather than helping in this particular situation. What we want to be able to do instead is position both those charts where we want them to go, not have them constrained by the grid. So for that, we're going to create a floating layout. And my profit legend I'm going to place just below the map. So what I'm able to do with the floating layer is control exactly where everything goes. I'm not constrained by having it on a grid, or even having any of the things the same size. Now, what's good about that is that control. What's not good about it is that control. I've had to size everything independently. Now, which you use kind of depends on the use case. In general, if you're doing a more artistic and creative dashboard, floating is probably what you're going to be doing. If you've got background images that you're trying to align with your charts, then the floating allows you to position them all over the place, whereas the tiled layout is much more useful when you want that solid grid. Which one you choose is entirely up to you, but I would always advise pick one or the other. In general, mixing and matching floating and tiled layouts doesn't always work. There's a risk that sometimes when you publish
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-floating-dashboard?u=76281980&t=287)** and people view it on different devices, it can look different.

#### [How to use filter actions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-filter-actions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-filter-actions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Filter actions are a great way to add interactivity into our dashboard and allow us to filter data, but without taking the performance hit that we normally get from filtering. And because filtering works kind of in between the data and the visualization, it can include a bit of a performance hit. If we were to add filters to every sheet in our dashboard, every time we review the data or update the dashboard, [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md)'s got to check all of those filters, to see what data it should get through to be visualized and that's going to take a bit of a performance hit. Each filter might not take a lot, but if you have more than 1, 2, 3, 4, it kind of all adds up. Filter actions instead are filters that apply when an action is performed. This means that if you are not performing that action, the dashboard loads up really quickly. So for an example, this dashboard here is looking at our superstore dataset and I've got my four charts, my bar chart, my profit map, heat map, and a sales line chart. Now at the moment, there's no filtering in place. If I hover over any of my charts, I can see the detail that is held within each of those bars or areas or lines. But at the moment, these four charts don't know about each other. They don't know that they are sitting next to each other. What I can do with actions is get them to interact with them. So for example, if I were to select a state on the map, I'd really like to be able to see
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-filter-actions?u=76281980&t=96)** that state's data reflected in the other three charts. Now, to do that, we can use dashboard actions. The quick way of doing it is to simply turn on filter actions for the selected sheet. So if I select the map so it goes gray, I can click on the little filter icon to say use as filter. Now, if I interact with this chart, it's going to filter. So for example, if I select Texas, I'm now looking at the Texas timeline in the bottom left. I'm looking at all the sales for the state of Texas, which is one of the central states. And in my heat map, I'm looking at detail for the category and the segment for just Texas. Any of the states that I select, I can see that same level of detail. And if I was to select a number of states by left clicking and dragging, I'm now looking at the sales for the combined states. They're broken down by whether they're the central, eastern, or southern region. And I can see some detail in my profit heat map. The actions are performed as and when I clicked. So the filtering didn't happen until that same point, which means performance wise is nice and quick. Now, I can do the same thing with say, the heat map. I could turn on the user's filter, and now if I click on Furniture, I'm just looking at the sales for each furniture. So my timeline is just looking at furniture as are my bars. And I can see the states where I made good profits
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-filter-actions?u=76281980&t=190)** and losses for furniture. And any missing states will be places where we didn't actually sell any furniture. Similarly, I could look at the central region. Now, by highlighting that column, I'm just looking at the central region bar chart for sales, the central region timeline, and just those states that are classified as being part of the central region in my data. Dashboard actions are really good, but that's just using the quick action, but we can get more fine detail should we want to. So to do that, I'm going to turn off all of the filters that I've just put on and instead we'll create them on the dashboard itself. So go to the dashboard menu option at the top and click on Actions. Now, we can click on Add Action, add Filter. And now this is where we're going to create our filter and we decide what sheets to act on and what sheets we were going to click on to perform that action. So let's say that I want to be able to click on the bars and for it just to update the line and nothing else. I can untick the ones I don't want to involve as part of the action. So I just want to be able to select the bar chart and the action is going to be a select. In general, I would recommend always using the select as the actions. These one are actually going to click on a bar or a line or part of the map. If you do hover, anytime you move your mouse over one of those elements and then is a dashboard,
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-filter-actions?u=76281980&t=286)** it's going to update everything else and that's going to look not as good as you think it's going to look. So always select, select. Then we select the target sheet, the action is going to be performed on. Well, I just want it to affect the line. So I'm going to untick the other three options. And then when we remove the action, I want to show the value. So I want everything to be restored as it was. Let's click OK, and it's now added that as a possible filter. So let's go back. Now, if I select any of my states, nothing happens. If I select any of my values in my heat map, nothing happens. But what about if we select something in our bar chart? Let's select the eastern region and we can see the only thing that's changing is that line chart. So we have complete control over not only the source of the action, we control the type of action, and we control where the action is performed. This gives great granularity to decide how and where to perform those actions. Remember, dashboard actions are a really good way of adding interactivity to our dashboard. It makes them more engaging and ask people to answer many more questions without having to go through lots and lots of different filters. Notice we haven't got any filters on this view at all. So even though I'm now looking at just the central region, I haven't not selected from a dropdown. When I'm finished, I can just click away and everything comes back, that's the true power of filter actions.

#### [How to use highlight actions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-highlight-actions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-highlight-actions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Highlight actions take a user selection on one sheet and highlight the corresponding data on another. This could be really useful because filtering data sometimes loses the context. What do I mean by that? Well, let's look at an example. If I was to change my profit map in this dashboard where I have four sheets to be a filter by using the quick filter icon, and then I select one of those states, that's going to filter the [rest](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Representational%20State%20Transfer%20(REST).md) of the dashboard. If you want to understand more how that worked, check out the video on Filter Actions. But now that's set, if I select Texas, I'm now looking at all of my Texas sales broken down by the category and the segment in my profit heat map. broken down by region and category in my bar chart. And also, I'm only looking at my Texan customers. If I select any of the other states, I can look at each of those individual customers. But the problem is, how do I know how good those customers are? I've lost all the other customers out of that data, out of that scatterplot. Now, I can see the actual values. So I can see, for example, with my Colorado customers, I have this one very bad customer. But overall, are my customers good or bad? How do they relate to my entire customer base? I don't know that because I've removed everything. We filtered all of that data out and all that useful information.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-highlight-actions?u=76281980&t=93)** What we need to do instead is highlight. When I click on a state, I want it to highlight those customers so I can see, in context, whether they are good or bad. Well, to do that, we need to create a highlight action. We do that by going to the dashboard menu item at the top of the screen and click on Actions, and then going to remove the filter action that I created a moment ago. Click on Add Action and click on Highlight. Now, what I need to do is tell [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) what is going to be the source of the action, the action I'm actually going to do, and the sheet it's going to apply to. Now, in my case, we're going to click on the Profit Map. So I'm going to untick all the other sheets to just leave the profit map highlighted, and my action is going to be select. In general, as a rule, I would say the select action is probably the one you want to use. If you're using the hover action what that means is anytime you move the mouse over, it's going to adapt and fire off the action and the dashboard's going to change, and that's probably not the behavior that you want. So, generally, have it select so you have to click and select an object. So when we select a state on our map, we want it to apply to our customer sales scatterplot. So I'm going to turn off everything else and then we'll hit OK so now my highlight action has been created and now, when we click on a state, nothing happens.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-highlight-actions?u=76281980&t=188)** Okay, so why is that? Let's have a look. The state map has got states in it, but my customer sales doesn't. So what I need to do is say to Tableau, just select the action based on the state and province. Okay, that's cool, we can do that. So now, when we click on the state, it should be highlighting the state. And it's still not doing it. Okay, let's have a little look. Now, my profit map has been created using the inbuilt latitude and longitude that Tableau has for the states. We've got our profit and we've got our state and our country region. Let's have a look at our scatterplot. So I've got my profit and my sales. Profit is on the color, and customer name is on the details so that I have one data point for each customer. Ah, there's the problem, there's no state information in that view so Tableau can't highlight the state. If we were filtering the state, that would be okay because the filtering happens before the view is created. Whereas, the highlighting happens after the view has been created. And because state isn't used to create this view, we can't highlight it. So if we add state and province to our detail, now each of our customers has got its state information attached to it. Let's go back to my dashboard.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-highlight-actions?u=76281980&t=281)** And now if I select a state, I can now see that my customers for that individual state are now highlighted. So now what I can see, if I select Colorado, I can see my poor customer but, in context, they're not the worst, we have some that are much better. And I can see that my other customers are quite typical around that space. I can select any of my other states to look for interesting outliers. Is that state typical or is there something different going on? Remember, in order to highlight, you have to have the same field on both the source and the target sheet. Otherwise, Tableau doesn't know what field it's supposed to use for the highlighting. We can combine both the filter action and the highlight action. So if I wanted to, I could have one single action that performed both on the map, the customer sales, the heat map, and the sales bar by mix and matching the type of actions that we're doing. So for an example, if I go to dashboard and click on Actions, I'll leave my highlight action alone, but I could add a filter action. This time, again, I want it to work on the map, but I only want it to work on the heat map and the bar chart. I could use selectors before and show all values when I clear it. Now, when I select the map, it's highlighting the customers, but filtering the charts. This means that I can look at my sales,
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-highlight-actions?u=76281980&t=378)** I can look at my profit, and I can find that poor customer is probably the consumer segment in the home [office](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Microsoft%20Office.md) because they are some pretty low sales there. Mix and matching these actions allow us to do some detailed analysis, but still have a nice, clean, precise dashboard.

#### [How to use URL actions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-url-actions?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-url-actions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] URL actions are a way that we can link out to web pages that might have extra information. The first thing we're going to do is give it a name. Now, in general, the best way to handle URL actions is with the menu. What this will mean is when we select the state, this option is going to appear in the tooltip as a hyperlink. So we can actually use that to make the name a little more descriptive. If you have hover on the Go to URL anytime you move your mouse over one of those states, the webpage is just going to pop up, and we definitely don't want that. The menu is a much nicer way of doing it. The URL action will open up in a webpage if it doesn't really exist. And finally, we need to give it the URL we're going to put in. When I click on that, it opens up the Wikipedia page for that given state. This means that I can have all the additional information about California come up in a webpage rather than try to fit it into the View. This is also particularly useful if you're using a dashboard to link out to an internal website where you've got some tracking information about your data. The ability to pass out to the URL means we can bring more information in without having it part of our original data.

#### [How to use set actions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-set-actions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-set-actions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this video, we're going to look at an example of set actions to do what's called proportional brushing. Now in this very simple dashboard, I've got two charts. I've got my sales on the left showing the sales per region. On the right hand side I've got those same sales, but this time broken down by the subcategory. Now what I'd like to know is, of those subcategories, what sales were attributed to each one of my regions. Now, I could put region onto color, but what I want to be able to do is to see how much is down to one particular region and then what percentage is left for the other three regions. Now, filtering the data isn't going to do that either. If I turn on the simple filter action and then select say the central, it's only shown me the central states, I'd lost the context. What I want to know is what percentage of the central states are then involved in the sales across each of my categories. So what proportion is due to that central state? Now to do this, we need to use a set action. So the first thing I have to do is create the set for the regions, then apply that to the subcategories, and then create the action to trigger between the two. So first step, let's create the set based on the region. So let's go to the region chart and I'm going to select region from the data pane. I'm going to right click and I'm going to go to create a set. Now the easiest way to do this is just to pick one value
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-set-actions?u=76281980&t=96)** so that we have a set that is both the in and the out values. This is how we can set the colors in the next step. So let's just pick the central region. So nothing changes for my data in this view because I've just created the set, but I'm not doing anything with it yet. What we're going to do is we're going to go to the bar chart, and if I put the region set onto color, what that does is color code each of those bars. If it's in the set, it's blue, if it's out of the set, it's gray. Now that means because we set central as the only region that was in the set, those blue segments are actually the central sales. Now I'm going to rearrange the in and out because it makes it easier to see the ones I'm interested in if they're aligned to that zero axis. Now the slight problem I've got is I've got quite a variation in the size of those bars. Some of 'em are very big, and then envelopes, fasteners, and labels are very small so it's very hard to see that detail. So I think what I'd actually rather see is a hundred percent stacked bar chart. To do that we need to use a secondary table calculation. So I'm going to go to my summer sales in my column, click add table calculation, and we're going to select the percent of total, and table along. So then now each of my bars is a hundred percent stacked bar and I can see the percentage
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-set-actions?u=76281980&t=189)** due to whatever has been selected in that set. Now, I'm not necessarily interested in this number over here, but we'll keep it in for the moment because this is the number that's telling me what is out of the set. So that might be useful. So let's just change the formatting slightly 'cause I don't need that much precision. So I'm going to click on format, change the number, percentage, and let's just do that to zero decimal places. And then we can do that again for the pane, set the number there to a percentage and zero decimal places. That's better. Okay, so now I've got my labels and I'm just going to change the color of the font as well. So let's change that to white so it stands out a little bit better. Now we can apply our action. If we go back to our dashboard, we can see that we've now got the sets in and out, but I haven't got any interaction. If I click over here on the left hand side, nothing changes. We need to add the action to add those values to that set that we created a moment ago. So for that, we go up to the dashboard, I select actions, and we're going to add a set action. So change set values is what we're going to add. Now the action is going to apply to the sales map chart. When I select something, I want it to add that region to the set.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-set-actions?u=76281980&t=284)** So the target set that the action is going to work on is this region set. It's the only one in here so that's one I'm going to select. Now, running the action, what do I want it to do? Well I want it to assign those values to the set. When I clear the selection, what do I want it to do? Do I want it to keep those values? Do I want to add all the values? Or remove all the values? Well, what I'll do is I'll get it to keep those values in there until I click on a different region. Let's do okay. So now let's see what happens. If I select the eastern region, now the blue bars are showing me the percentage sales due to the east. I can click on the southern and then I can click on the west and I can see how those percentage change. Now what I've got is not only can I see what that state has done, I can then see what the other three regions have also done as well. Now that's really useful. Those western states make up 41% of sales in labels, but 59% of all the other states across all those other regions don't make up the sales. This is really useful 'cause I can look at the proportion of that data. Sometimes filtering removes too much information, but the set action allows us to do this proportional brushing so we can better understand the proportion of those sales.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

#### [A deeper look at containers](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/a-deeper-look-at-containers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/a-deeper-look-at-containers?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Containers are a design element that we can use within a dashboard to help with alignment of different objects. So what are containers? Well, there are a way of allowing us to group dashboard elements together. If we have certain things that belong to together in terms of grouping, then this is a good way of doing it. Once they're grouped together, we can put things like borders or shading behind those selected elements. It allows us to size them all independently so they fit all the space that's available to them. If we don't do that, we have to kind of manually resize objects or type the width or height of those in. When an object gets put inside a vertical or horizontal container, we have the option to distribute those objects evenly. What that means is that they will take up the same amount of space as each other within that container, which can help to make a nice-looking grid layout. And because containers are always aligned with one another, it makes it very easy to create tiled layouts. And the downside of that is they're not necessarily as useful for creating more artistic [Dashboards](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Dashboards.md). If you need complete control and pixel perfect alignment of different objects, maybe you want to add images or other kind of artistic flare to your dashboard, then the floating layout might be slightly better because it gives you more control over where you put everything. Now, which one you choose is kind of up to you,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/a-deeper-look-at-containers?u=76281980&t=95)** but to understand how containers work, let's kind of look at an example. So say I have four charts that I want to put inside a dashboard. I want to arrange them in a grid, but what I want to be able to do is group them together so that I can put a colored border around the two on the left-hand side and the two on the right-hand side. Maybe because those two charts on the left and on the right are kind of related to each other in some way, and I want some visual indication to show that these charts are showing the same sort of data that, and then what I want to do is put maybe a colored border or a colored background across both of those charts. Now, the way that we would approach that in using layout containers is with vertical and horizontal containers. Now, in this example, the two red boxes are vertical containers. Anything that we put inside those vertical containers are going to be stacked vertically. So in the case of these four charts, the two in each of the red boxes can be scaled to fit that space. What that means is that both of the charts will be exactly the same size, which will make for a nice layout. Then if I want the border around all of the charts, the blue space, I need to have a horizontal container. That's because I'm going to nest
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/a-deeper-look-at-containers?u=76281980&t=189)** those two red vertical containers inside the blue container, and I want them to space each other horizontally to make most space usage. So the way that we would construct this is kind of working backwards. What we would do is start with our blank dashboard and we would add the horizontal container. We then add one of the vertical containers inside it, and then place another container to the left of it. Once those are in place, I can start adding my individual charts into those containers that are waiting for me. This will allow me to create that grid-type layout. Layout containers can be quite tricky to use, and in fact, color them is quite a good way to see them. So put a border around them when you're creating them, 'cause you can always get rid of it later. If you want a more free-flowing dashboard, then using the floating one might work for you, but you're going to have to manually move and fix everything in space. So which one you decide is kind of up to you in your use case. To understand how to make dashboards using each one of these methods, check out the two videos where we go through the details of creating one using containers for a tiled layout, and one using the floating. Which one you pick depends upon what you want to do.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Dashboards](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Dashboards.md) (2)
> **CLI Commands:** make (4)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

### 11. Analysis Tips and Tricks

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [How to create bar charts](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-bar-charts?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-bar-charts?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Here's how we create bar charts. Bar charts have two features. They have an axis where we plot the value, and in general, we have a dimension. That breaks the bar into individual bars based on the number of independent values of that dimension. So, to build a bar chart, first we put a measure on the row or the column. So, in this case, we'll put Sales onto our column and we have a single bar chart. Now, we add more dimensions to break it into its components. So, for example, let's break it down by Sub-Category. We can add further dimensions, which will create more bars. If I add them to the rows, such as Category onto the row, we then group first the bars by the Category, and then break them down by the Sub-Category. I can also add dimensions to the columns. If I put Region onto the columns, now I have multiple bars for each of the Regions, each of the Sub-Categories, and then grouped by the Category.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Analogies:** for example (1), such as (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [How to use colors to highlight data](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-colours-to-highlight-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-colours-to-highlight-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Lecturer] Color is by far the most powerful analysis tool that we have. It allows us to focus on what's important and allows us to discover the interesting things within our data. Then there's multiple ways we can add color to a visualization, but the two most common ways is by using the marks card. The marks card has a field for color. If we click on it, when we have a view such as this where we have single bars, any change to the color is going to change all of the bars. Now, I could change the bars so it matched maybe a corporate color scheme, but we can do so much more. We can color based on the underlying data itself. We can color based on a dimension. So I have three different dimensions in my view. I have region, category and subcategory. Let's see what happens when I put one of those colors onto the color shelf. So if I put region onto color, my bars are color coded by the region they belong in. What this now does is focus to look vertically across each of our subcategories and categories in terms of the region. By coloring it in the vertical, which is the region, we are now focusing on one particular region. Now I'm looking to see what is the highest selling category and subcategory across one particular region. Let's see what happens if I put category onto color instead? Drag and drop category onto color, and now we're looking across the table. Now our focus is on each individual category.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-colours-to-highlight-data?u=76281980&t=94)** That's the most important thing to us. The bars haven't changed. The orientation of the bars haven't changed. The length of the bars haven't changed. It's just the color, but it completely changes the analysis of that data. If I put subcategory onto the color, how does that look? Well, let's be honest. It looks a mess. There is so much color on here, it's hard to determine anything. In actual fact, it makes it worse. So color is a really important tool, but we have to use it carefully. Now, instead of dimensions, we could use a measure instead. The bar is sized by the sum of sales. So if I put sales onto color, what do we get? We get a range of values because the measure is a continuous range, so we get a continuous range of colors. Now, what this is good for is to highlight those larger and those smaller values. Now, in the case of a bar where the bar is also showing the length of the underlying value, this could be seen as a bit of a waste. But color is such a good thing that maybe it helps to double in code sometimes. What about if we want to show a different measure at the same time? Well, let's put profit onto color. What does that look like? Well, now we see something different. The bar is the length of the sales, but the color is the value of the profit. So we can see that something like tables in the East stands out. That bright orange color tells us there's something bad happening with tables in that eastern region. Experiment with the colors. It's easy to do.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-use-colours-to-highlight-data?u=76281980&t=188)** You just have to throw something onto the color shelf. Try a measure, try a dimension. Try all the different measures and all the different dimensions. Try ones that aren't part of the view, such as the profit on here. If I was to add segment to the color, which is a discrete value that we're not using as part of the view, we get a segmented bar. It's easy to add the color, so why not give it a go? You might find something that you never knew you had in that data.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

#### [How to create timelines](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-time-lines?u=76281980)

A timeline is when you plot one or more measures against a continuous date. To understand more about discreet continuous dates, check out the date chapter where we go into this in more detail. Now, in order to create a timeline, we need two things. We need a measure. This is the thing we're going to track the change over time. This needs to be a numeric value, and we then need the continuous version of a date. In our dataset, we have Order Date and Shipping Date, so we can look at the Order Date. Now, the quick way to get a continuous value is to use the right click drag or Option drag method for a Mac user. When we add the date to our row or column, we get this dialogue box come up here where we can select the type of date that we want. Discrete Dates are the top in blue, Continuous Dates are at the bottom in green. So let's select the month of order date. When we click OK, [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) puts a mark on each of the individual months that we have data for. This is just a placeholder to say, when you add the measure, this is where they are going to go. So we can go ahead and now add the measure to our view. So let's look at Profit. Let's put Profit onto the row. So now we have one continuous timeline. It's a continuous line because the date is also continuous. We are comparing one month to the previous month and the month afterwards. This is good for checking changes over time. We can add colors to this.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-time-lines?u=76281980&t=94)** If we wanted to add a measure, we can drag and drop a measure such as Profit onto the color. Then we can see cases where we have negative profit or losses go to orange. Positive ones going to blue. Maybe that's the most interesting thing of our timeline. Or we could compare different dimensions. If I put something like segment onto color, what we're tracking now is the individual performance of each individual segment, month on month over time. Timelines are good for tracking changes over time. We use them when the chronological passage of time is what we are interested in.

What happens here now is that all of the axes scale to fit the minimum and maximum for all of our values. Remember the one we looked at a moment ago, the office supplies in the southern region? Now it's stretched out even more. We can see more detail in those smaller months. Small multiples let you look at a single timeline spread across a number of different dimensions. #### [How to create a combined axis timeline](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-combined-axis-timeline?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-combined-axis-timeline?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A combined axis timeline is when we want to compare two measures and how they change over time. To start, let's build a simple timeline. I'm going to take order date and use the right-click or option drag to put it onto the column, and they're going to select a continuous date. So, let's use QUARTER. I'm now going to add Sales to my rows to have a quarterly timeline. I now want to see, how does profit change over time? If I add Profit onto the rows, I'm going to get a separate timeline, which isn't what I want. In order to create the combined axis, I need to drag the second measure, in this case Profit, onto the existing axis. Now, I have two axes, because I have two green fields. I have a quarter of order date, and I have the sales, so make sure you drop it on the correct axis. In this case, I want to have Profit and Sales on the same. So, now I have two timelines. I have my Profit in blue and my Sales in orange, and I can see how the two are related. The one thing to remember about a combined axis chart is that we are using multiple measures on the same axis with the same scaling. So, Profit is always less than the Sales, so we lose a lot of the detail. If I add something like Quantity, we can see the line is almost flat. That's because the Quantity is greatly outweighed by the amount of Sales.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-combined-axis-timeline?u=76281980&t=92)** They're in always that same scaling axis. So, actually, we should only ever combine measures that have similar orders of magnitude, otherwise, we're going to lose an awful lot of detail, and you can't make a decent comparison.

#### [How to create a dual axis timeline](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-dual-axis-timeline?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-dual-axis-timeline?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Unlike the combined axis timeline, when we have two measures occupying the same scaling, the dual axis allows us to show two independent measures at independent axes with independent scaling. To do that, let's first of all build a simple sales timeline. I'm going to put Order Date onto my column, and then I'm going to select the continuous monthly. I'm going to add Sales onto the row. Now, in order to create a dual axis chart, we need to drag and drop the second measure, but we need to drag it to the opposite side of the view. We don't want to drop it onto the existing axis, 'cause that will create the combined axis. So let's look at Profit. I'm going to take the Profit, and I'm going to drag it to the right-hand side. And as we get to the edge of the screen, we see the axis icon appear. And when I release my mouse, we have a dual axis chart. So notice the things that have changed. We have two continuous measures on our row, but I have a straight line in between the two. This shows that they've been combined, but we have this dual axis. I have one axis for Sales on the left, and one access for Profit on the right. My Profit is colored blue, my Sales is colored orange. What this allows me to do is to look at the individual data for each individual month and compare the shape of the data. Notice that the profit scales are completely different.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-dual-axis-timeline?u=76281980&t=95)** There's about an order of magnitude difference between the two. This tells us that the sales and the profit are very different. The point of the dual axis is to look for the shape. We're looking at the changes over time to see if the peaks and troughs of each independent measure kind of marries up. And in this case, they pretty much do. As sales go up, profit goes up, with a couple of exceptions, which are interesting. Why has that happened? We wouldn't see that if we weren't scaling the axes independently. One of the downsides of this is it's not entirely clear which axis belongs to which color. So one thing we can do is format the axes. To format them, we're going to right-click on the Axis, select Format, and then we can change the fonts in the format pane to match the color. So Sales is going to be orange. I can select the profit axis and change that to blue. This now helps to identify what each of the axes belong to, so we can check that the scaling is the same, to make sure that people don't misinterpret our data.

#### [Two ways to create a crosstab](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/two-ways-to-create-a-crosstab?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/two-ways-to-create-a-crosstab?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Under the right circumstances, A cross tab can be a really good choice of data visualization. Broadly speaking, there's two ways of creating a cross tab. We can either start with the initial measure and then break it down into individual components, or we can be able to scaffold first and add the measures separately. So first, let's look at the measure first approach. I want to look at the sales broken down by subcategory, region, and segment. Now to do that, I'm going to start off with the measure. If I take sales and drag it onto the column or the row, we're going to get a bar chart. The third place we can drop it is right in the middle of the view. So if I take sales, drag and drop it into the middle, we get a label that tells us we have 2.3 million in sales. Next, I'm going to take that single mark and break it down into each of its subcategories, regions, and segments. So let's put subcategory onto the row, add region to the column, and now I'm going to add segment after the region. So now I have a cross tab showing 203 individual marks. I can look down to find a particular subcategory, I can read across to find the particular region and segment that I'm interested in. That's the first method, starting with a measure and then breaking it down to its individual components. The second method is to build the components first and then add the measure. So to recreate that table, let's pull those out.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/two-ways-to-create-a-crosstab?u=76281980&t=96)** I want to look at subcategories, and then I want to look subcategories across region and then across different segments. When we put our measure values in, they're going to fill in where it says the A, B, C. [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) always needs to aggregate and show the data. So the A, B, C is a placeholder which says, when you're ready, this is where I'm going to visualize the data. Now we've got our scaffold in place. It's simply a case of taking the sales, dragging and dropping it into our view.

#### [Enhance a crosstab using colors to create a highlight table](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/enhance-a-crosstab-using-colours-to-create-a-highlight-table?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/enhance-a-crosstab-using-colours-to-create-a-highlight-table?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] So the highlight table is a simple enhancement of a cross tab. The good thing about a cross tab is if you know where to look, they're easy to find the data. If I know the particular subcategory, region, and segment I'm interested in, I can hone in on that answer. But if I want to know what is the highest value, what is the lowest value, what is the best segment, what is the worst subcategory? That's really hard to do. I have to compare many, many values, in this case, 203, to make sure I've got the understanding of that data. Just by adding a better color, which is by far the most important tool we have in our toolbox, this can make the job much, much easier. There's two steps to enhance a cross tab to turn it into a highlight table. The first is to add some color. To do this, I'm going to duplicate the profit that I'm showing as part of the label. So I'm going to take the profit and drag it onto color. Now, each of my labels is colored, depending on the value of the data it's showing. If it's profitable, it's blue, if it's a loss, it's orange. Now, although that's helping, it's still a bit hard to see because some of those grays kind of fade into the background. That might be what we want. But to go one step further, to turn it into a highlight table, we have to change the mark type. At the moment, the mark type is a text. Instead, we're going to change it to a square, and when we do that, we get a highlight table. Now the background is being colored based on the value it's displaying.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/enhance-a-crosstab-using-colours-to-create-a-highlight-table?u=76281980&t=94)** This makes it really easy now to see the highest values such as copiers in the corporate segment. And it also lets us see the worst performing categories. So we can see that tables are not doing too good in most places except for the corporate segment in the Western region. The highlight table uses the power of color to hone in on the interesting things in our data. We can turn a plain cross tab into something much more appealing and much more useful.

#### [How to turn a crosstab into a heatmap](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-turn-a-crosstab-into-a-heatmap?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-turn-a-crosstab-into-a-heatmap?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Cross tabs and highlight tables typically only let you see one measure at a time. In this example, we are looking at the profit, but I want to know what the relationship between the profit and the actual amount of sales that we're making is. If I add sales to my view, by dragging and dropping into the center of the cross tab, we get a view like this where I'm showing one row for the profit, one row for the sales, but that's just too complicated. It doesn't help with my understanding. Instead, what we can use is sizing. If we add a second dimension to the size, remember, I'm looking at squares here. We can size the square by the value of that measure. So let's go ahead and do that. If I take sales and put that onto size, each one of my squares is now sized based on the value of the sales. Change the fit to be entire view, and because it's not particularly helpful, I'm going to untick show mark labels. I can alter the relative size of all of the squares to make them easier to see. Now, we can see some interesting things within our data. The color is determined by the amount of profit. The darker the blue, the higher profit, the darker the orange, the bigger the loss. We can also see the size of the square shows us about the quantities of the sale, how big the sale was. So if we were to look at chairs, for example,
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-turn-a-crosstab-into-a-heatmap?u=76281980&t=96)** we can see that in the consumer segment, we have quite a large amount of sales. That also equates to a higher amount of profit. But we can see other places where we are making significant sizes of sale, say in tables, but overall, we're making losses everywhere. In the Western regions where the highlight table showed that we were making profits for tables, we can see that actually we sell quite a decent amount because the size of the square is quite large, and yet we still make profit. These aren't small one-offs. This seems to be a significant amount that's going in there. We can now compare the sizing, the relative sizing for each segment, region, or subcategory, along with the profitability. The heat map gets rid of the labels, it gets rid of the values and starts letting us look at the data. We can see what's going on behind the numbers to really understand what's happening with our data.

#### [How to create an area chart](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-an-area-chart?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-an-area-chart?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] An area chart is a type of timeline, but where we color in the area underneath the line. To build that, we need two things. We need at least one measure and we need at least one continuous date. So let's look at the changes to sales over time. We'll put sales onto our row, right click drag order date, and change this to month. I'm now looking at my monthly sales. This is automatically a line chart 'cause [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) knows that is the best way to view this data. But if I want to turn it into an area chart, I simply have to change the mark type. We can go to the mark card and change from automatic to area. This is now an area chart where we've colored in the region underneath. What's good about an area chart is it allows us to see what dimensions make up the hole. What I mean by this is if we add a dimension, we're going to get the shaded area colored by each individual member of that dimension. So for example, if I put segment onto color, we have three possible segments, consumer, corporate, and home [office](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Microsoft%20Office.md). And now I can see how much each one of those segments contributes to the monthly total in sales. The area chart is a good way to look at not only changes over time, but also the part to whole relationship for a given dimension.
