#### [What's the difference between line charts vs. area charts?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/whats-the-difference-between-line-charts-vs-area-charts?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/whats-the-difference-between-line-charts-vs-area-charts?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Tutor] Line charts and area chart, both plot changes over time. So what is the difference? Doesn't they do exactly the same thing? Yes, in a way, there's very little difference between a line chart and an area chart, if we have no other dimensions in the view. If I flick between this line chart and an area chart, we can see that not a lot changes, other than, underneath the line it gets shaded in. But look what happens when we start to introduce different dimensions into our view. I'm going to change from looking at Monthly to Quarterly, to make the line a little bit simpler. If we were to look at changes for sales over time, broken down by category in a line chart, what will happen? If I put Category onto Color, I now get one line for each individual category. What this lets me do is to compare each month or each quarter, and the sales attributed to those three categories. So what I can see is, in 2023 Q4, the sales of my three categories were pretty close. In fact, it looks like they're probably the closest they've ever been. Ordinarily, we can see that [office](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Microsoft%20Office.md) supplies lags behind furniture and technology, and we can see cases where technology has outperformed all of them. The line chart plots three lines, one on top of the other. This lets us do the comparison
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/whats-the-difference-between-line-charts-vs-area-charts?u=76281980&t=95)** between those individual categories. But what we've lost in doing that is the overall total. If I take the Category off, notice what happens to the axis. The total quarter sales for that Q4 in 2023 was 236,000, but when I have them broken down by the category, I lose that information because now, I'm looking at each individual's category. Now I could select all three of them, and [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) would tell me in the Tall Tip what the total amount was. But if I'm interested in what the total amount was all the time, the timeline makes it harder to do. So what happens if we switch between the area and the timeline? So if I change the line to Area, look what happens. The axis has changed to show the full range like we had before. Instead of three lines, we have three areas, and what we see is that the Technology is at the bottom, Office Supplies is stacked on top, and then Furniture is stacked on top of that. We can then see how each of those has contributed to those quarterly totals. So I can see that for that Q4, furniture contributed 80,000, office supplies 75, and technology 81. The area takes everything into account, still lets us see the totals, and how much each of those individual values in the category
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/whats-the-difference-between-line-charts-vs-area-charts?u=76281980&t=188)** contributed to the whole. What it's much harder to do ever, is to look to see which category did best in any grid quarter. That looks easier with the line chart. So, which is the right one to use? The line chart or the area chart? Well, the answer is, it depends, what's most important to you? What is it you're trying to do? Are you interested in knowing the overall total? Do you want to know how each value of category has contributed to the whole? Or do you want to be able to compare one category directly against the other? That is the only choice between the line and the area that you have to make.

#### [How and why to add a running total to a line chart](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-and-why-to-add-a-running-total-to-a-line-chart?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-and-why-to-add-a-running-total-to-a-line-chart?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The Running Total is a good way to show how data accumulates over time. Typically, we use it on a time series line chart to see how day on day, month on month, year on year, our overall values are increasing, but there's another good use case for the Running Total. To explain it, first, we'll create a continuous timeline chart looking at Sales. So first, I'm going to put Sales onto my row, and we'll right-click Order Date to put it onto our column, and we'll select the month of sales. Now, what this shows is the sales that are happening each month within our business. Now, we know we have seasonal variation. We know that towards the end of the year, our sales increase, over the summer, they decrease, and then pick up again in the autumn. Now, we understand that business. We understand that that's perfectly acceptable and we've mitigated that within our business structure. But to somebody else, what they are seeing is peaks and troughs. They're seeing good sales and bad sales. Now, we don't necessarily want to expose that kind of information, because they might get the wrong impression, so the Running Total helps to eliminate that miscomprehension. To create the Running Sum is a secondary table calculation. To add that, we go to the Sales on our row, we go to Quick Table Calculation and Running Total. [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md)'s now performing a cumulative total month on month.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-and-why-to-add-a-running-total-to-a-line-chart?u=76281980&t=95)** It starts at zero, adds the first month of data, and then adds the sum total for each month going across, so we can then see that the total amount of sales is 2.3 million. So, not only can we see how the sales have increased over time and what our Running Total is for any given month, it also eliminates any negativity in that chart. Because it's a Running Total, it's only ever going to go up. The sales are only ever increasing. We miss out all of those cases where we have those lean summer months. If you look closely in the chart, you can see cases where that is shown, because the line is a lot flatter, but we don't have any obvious dips. If you want to show people what's happening over time, but you don't want to show dips where things go down, because although it looks bad, you've mitigated those as part of your business, the Running Total can be a really good use case.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) (1)
> **UI Navigation:** go to (2), right-click (1), select the (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (3)
> **Versions:** 2.3 (1)

#### [How to show the difference between values in a line chart](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-show-the-difference-between-values-in-a-line-chart?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-show-the-difference-between-values-in-a-line-chart?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Instead of needing to see the actual values on a timeline, it's often useful to be able to tell the differences between the former and the latter of any particular data point. This needs a secondary calculation or a table calculation added to an existing field. So first, we'll create a timeline. We'll right click Order Date, drag and drop it onto the columns, and select one of the continuous date fields towards the bottom. I'm going to select the month. We then add our measure to rows. So let's put Sales onto the rows. So this is a timeline that is looking month on month at the value of the sales. So I can see for an individual month how many sales we had, but what I want to be able to do is compare one month to the previous month to see was that a larger or smaller amount? To do that, we add the secondary calculation via a table count. Right click on the sum of sales on the row, go to Quick Table Calculation, and select Difference. Now, instead of plotting the actual value of the sales, it's plotting the difference between the current and the previous data point. So for example, in September, 2021, this shows us that we have a higher sales by 53,000 than we had do previously. Notice on the bottom right hand corner, we have one null indicator value. This is because what we are showing is the difference between the current and the preceding data point. For the first record in our data,
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-show-the-difference-between-values-in-a-line-chart?u=76281980&t=92)** we don't have a preceding data point, therefore, [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) cannot complete that calculation. To overcome this, we can click on the null indicator and say, show data at default position. This will put a data point in at zero at our starting point.

#### [How to create a scatter plot and a bubble chart](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-scatter-plot-and-a-bubble-chart?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-scatter-plot-and-a-bubble-chart?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A scatterplot is a way to show the relation between two different measures using two different axes. They're very simple to create, they don't take up a lot of room, and they can have a huge amount of information. So, to create a scatterplot, we need to have two measures. We put one on the row and one on the column. So, let's look at profit on our row, sales on our column, and we get a scatter plot. Now, we only have one single point because [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) is showing us the total sum of sales and the total sum of profit across all of our dimensions. In order to break this down into finer detail, we need to add one or more dimensions to our marks. So, for example, if we wanted to look at the relationship between [products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md), we could put the product name onto the detail. Now, we have one single data point for each one of our products. We can change the shapes and the colors of all of those points. Click on the automatic marks card and select circle. We can then go to color and change the border to have a border around it. So, we can easily see the individual data points in the mass towards the lower values. We can then add color. We could color by category. We could color by region by putting any dimension onto the color. This may increase the number of data points depending on the granularity of that data. We can also add a measure. We could color by one of the values that is on the row or column, such as the sales.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-scatter-plot-and-a-bubble-chart?u=76281980&t=95)** Or we could color it by the profit. We could go ahead and color it by the value that isn't even in the view. Now, we can also change the size of these data points. I can change the size of all of them by just clicking on the slider. And now all the data points are going to be larger, but we can size them depending on the underlying data. So, instead of putting quantity onto the color, we could put quantity onto the size. Now I have different size data points. Ones that have higher values of quantity are going to be bigger compared to the other ones. This is an example of a bubble chart. So, the scatterplot to bubble chart is just putting a value, such in our case, quantity, onto the size.

#### [How to create a bar-in-bar chart and a candlestick chart](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-bar-in-bar-candlestick-chart?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-bar-in-bar-candlestick-chart?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A bar in bar or candlestick chart allows us to compare two measures using the same axis, and we use the measure name to measure values trick in order to achieve this. This is a great use for checking to see if we have exceeded or reached a target. To do that, we need to build a simple bar chart to begin with. So let's look at Sub-Category sales. I've got Sub-Category onto my row. Sales onto my column. Change the fit to entire view to make best use of the space. Now, I have Categories and Sales, but I also have a Sales Target that I want to see if I've exceeded or not. This exists at the bottom of my data pane and it's called Sales Target. If I add this to my view on the column, I get a second set of bars. Now, although that's useful, because I can then see which is the highest or the lowest target, it's very difficult to compare the two bars, because they're not next to each other. Instead, what I need to do is add both of those values onto the same axis. So when we drag Sales Target from the data pane on the left, we need to drag and drop it onto the existing sales axis. This invokes the measure names and measure values to allow [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) to plot both those bars on that same axis. However, it doesn't look very good. It's quite messy, there's lots of bars, and it's difficult to see what's going on. To turn this into a bar in bar chart, there's a couple of steps we need to do. First, we need to get rid of the measure name's header.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-bar-in-bar-candlestick-chart?u=76281980&t=94)** This is the reason why we have two bars for each of our subcategories. It would also be useful to be able to color them differently so I can tell one from the other. So we can do both of those with one step. Take Measure Names from the row and put it onto Color. We now have one bar for our Sales Target and one bar for our actual value of sales. Now, Tableau's default behavior is to stack bars. In this context, this makes no sense, because the sum total of sales plus sales target conceptually doesn't make sense to us. Instead, we want the sales and the sales target to both start at zero. To do that, we have to change the default behavior of Tableau. This is achieved by going to the Analysis menu at the very top of the screen, selecting Stack Marks and turning them off. This now means that both of the bars for each of the subcategories will start at zero. However, they're both overlapping, so I can't see them. What I need to do is change the size of just either the sales or the sales target to be bigger or smaller so I can see the one behind it. The quick way of doing this is to drag a second copy of Measure names from our Data pane and put it onto Size. So I take Measure Names from the bottom of my data pane, put it onto Size, and now I have two very distinct bars. One is large, one is small, one is Sales, one is Sales Target. This is an example of a bar in bar chart, but it's also known as a candlestick chart.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-bar-in-bar-candlestick-chart?u=76281980&t=187)** I can see all of my targets, and I can see all of my sales. I can see which ones have reached the targets, and which ones have exceeded it.

#### [How to create a symbol map](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-symbol-map-23118520?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-symbol-map-23118520?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] A symbol map is where we plot geographic data using individual data points. This is very similar to how a scatterplot works in that we plot the latitude and longitude as our X and Y and have an image of a map behind it. To create a map within [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md), it's simply a case of dragging and dropping geographic data into our view. The easiest way is to start with the highest level of detail that you have. So, in our example, we have country. So, I'm going to drag country into the middle of my view, and now, I'll get a map showing the center point of both the United States and Canada. As I add more detail, we're going to get more data points in exactly the same way as we do with a scatter plot. So, if I add state to detail, we now have a data point at the center of each state. If I go ahead and add city, we now have all of the cities plotted on my map. So, currently, I've got 616 individual cities plotted on a map. Each of these data points can be formatted. We can go to the Marks card and change the color, the size, and the labeling. The color can be controlled by either selecting an individual color for all of the data points, or I can color it by a measure or a dimension. For example, if I put region onto color, now all of my data points are colored depending on the geographic region in which they exist. I could put profit onto color instead. Then I can see which of my cities are most and least profitable.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-symbol-map-23118520?u=76281980&t=93)** We can also change the symbol itself. We can choose to have a square, a circle, or pick any particular shape that fits our data. Symbol maps will always plot the center point of our locations. If we don't have that data already in our individual latitudes and longitudes, Tableau can use its inbuilt database to do that work for us.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

#### [How to create a filled area map](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-filled-area-map?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-filled-area-map?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A filled area map is a type of map where we color in an area that we're interested in. Symbol maps create a data point, whereas a filled map creates an area. To create a filled map, we need geographic data. [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) understands that cities, countries, post codes, and states are all kinds of geographic data with latitudes and longitudes. If we drag these into the view, Tableau's going to create a map for us. To create a filled map, there's a couple of steps. First, add in some geographic data. I'm going to start with my top level, which is country. Now, by default in my setup, Tableau's put a single data point for Canada in the United States. If I want to change this to be a filled map, I go to the marks card and change the dropdown from automatic to map. I now have a filled map showing the complete area of those two countries. I can break this down further by adding more detail. If I add state or province to detail, Tableau will now draw individual areas for each one of the states in my data. We can change the color of those states. I can go to the color on the marks card and change them all to be a different color. We can also apply data to that color. I could color them by a geographic region. I can take region, which is dimension, drop it onto color, and at each of my segments, a color according to whether it's central, east, south, or west. We can also apply measures to our color. If I was interested in the sales per state,
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-a-filled-area-map?u=76281980&t=96)** I could put sales onto color. Each one of my states is now color from light blue to dark blue, depending on the value of the data behind it. If I add profit, which can have both a negative and a positive, we have a range of values from dark orange to dark blue. The filled map is a great way to show data when the geographic data is what's important. Don't just craft a map for the sake of it. Only do it where the where is important.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

#### [How to create pie charts](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-pie-charts?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-pie-charts?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Although much belied pie charts are a very simple visualization, they're understood by pretty much everybody. They're also very simple to create. A pie chart works in two ways. It has a number of segments and each segment is sized by an angle that's dependent on the percentage of the data that's being shown in the view. To create a pie chart, there's a couple of simple steps. First, we need to tell [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) we want to create a pie. To do that, we go to the marks card, click on the dropdown, and select pie. Notice we have a new field appear in our marks card, angle. This is going to be used to put a measure to say that, calculate the measure into the angle. So the next step is decide how we're going to segment our data, so for example, maybe I want to know what the regional breakdown is for all of our data. I'm going to put region onto color, and now I have my segments. There are four regions, I have four segments. They're all equally sized because we not have any measure on the angle. So if I want to know what the sales are for each of my regions, I simply take sales and put it onto angle. Now my segments are sized by the angle. I can change the overall size of the pie. I can change the fit to fit entire view, and now I have a much bigger pie to play with. I can turn on the labels by clicking on label and show mark labels, so I can see the values for each one of those segments.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-pie-charts?u=76281980&t=93)** Ordinarily you want to see the percentage when we talk about pie charts. To do that, we need to drag the value onto the label. So let's take sales and put that onto the label. The view doesn't change 'cause I was already showing the labels, but we need to change the computation. I want to see what the total percentage is. To do that, we need to use a secondary calculation or table calc. So right click on the summer sales that's being used for the label. Go to quick table calculation at the bottom, select percent of total, and we have our percentages.

#### [How to create Gantt charts](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-gantt-charts?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-gantt-charts?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Gantt charts are a proper way of tracking progress over time. They're particularly useful for project work where you want to see the start and the end of a piece of work and calculate the durations. Gantt charts need a couple of bits of information that aren't necessarily part of our data. For example, we need the number of days elapsed between two times. So I have order date and I have shipping date. So I'm going to create a calculation, which is going to be the duration between the order date and the shipping date. So we go to the top of our Data pane, click on the caret, and Correct Calculated Field. Let's call this Duration. And the calculation is going to be DATEDIFF. DATEDIFF calculates the number of date units between a start and end date. In order to create a Gantt chart, we need the number of days. So to do that, I'm going to do, calculate the day between the order date and the shipping date. This is going to return a number that I can use for my durations. We click OK, and it's been added to my measures. So now I want to create my Gantt chart. First, we need is a continuous order date where we're going to put the start of each of our orders. So let's drag Order Date with a right click + drag or option + drag onto the column, and we'll select the Order Date Continuous. This is because I want to see the exact time when that order was placed.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-gantt-charts?u=76281980&t=95)** There's a lot of information here, so I'm just going to create a subset of that data. I'm going to go to Order Date, I'm going to select Filter, and I'm going to select the Year. I'm just going to pick 2024. So now I can see all of my data points for 2024. Every order in my data has one start and one finish. So every order is going to have a duration. So the next thing for the Gantt chart is to add the order ID. I drag Order ID and add that to the row. It's going to warn me that there's a lot of data here, but that's fine. Now I can see one point for all of my orders in my data. For example, I can see that this order was placed on the 17th of the fourth, 2024, but I want to know how long did that order take to do? At the moment, this bar is of zero size. All I need to do is take the Duration that we calculated at a moment ago, put that onto Size, and now each of my bar is sized by the duration. So the time between the order being placed and the order being shipped in this case was 16. However, if we look at the underlying data, we can see that this is not exactly true. [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) calculates the duration for each row, and then because we tell Tableau to aggregate data, it then sums those four rows, which gives us the total duration of 16, which is clearly not correct.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-gantt-charts?u=76281980&t=188)** So rather than the sum of duration, we need to do the average. What that will do will suppress the multiple lines and just give us the duration of four. So I'm going to change the Sum of Duration, change the measure type to be Average. So now we have the correct data. I can then look down and see where I had quick orders and slow orders. Now our data has a shipping mode, so people pay for shipping when they place their order. I want to know if people are actually getting the service that they've paid for. So if I put Ship Mode onto Color, I can then see all of those that wanted First Class, Same Day, Second Class, and Standard Class. I can then scroll down and visually see ones that went wrong.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-to-create-gantt-charts?u=76281980&t=243)** This one, for example, was same day shipping. The duration was one day. So I can see that that was working well. The Gantt chart is very useful because it lets us plot changes over time. If I had a whole cluster that were getting late, I know there was something wrong with the business.

#### [How can I grow my data science and Tableau skills?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-can-i-grow-my-data-science-and-tableau-skills?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-can-i-grow-my-data-science-and-tableau-skills?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Congratulations on completing the course, but what are you going to do now? What's the next steps in your learning? Well, it's always good to learn more. There's a couple of courses that I would really recommend that you look at. First is the Data Visualization Tips and Tricks. This goes into a lot more detail about why we choose certain chart types for our data. We'll go into more detail about the choices that we have to make depending on whether we are investigating our data or showing our data off. There's lots of tips and tricks there that anybody at any level will be able to pick up. Now, although it uses [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) for all of its visualizations, it's actually tool agnostic. The other course you should really look at is the [Creating Interactive Tableau Dashboards](Creating%20Interactive%20Tableau%20Dashboards.md). This goes into a lot more detail about the final part of our Tableau journey, which is creating the [Dashboards](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Dashboards.md). In that course, we talk about the types of dashboard that we create. We talk about how to ensure that the who, what, where, and why of creating dashboards is considered. This goes into detail about layout, about design, so I'd really recommend this. Think about it. If you spent all that time looking for the data, curating the data, analyzing the data, and visualizing the data, why let the very last bit go to chance? Ensure you know exactly how to organize your dashboard for the very best results. I would just recommend practice. Practice as much as you can. That's how the really good people get good at Tableau. So, find some data that you are interested in.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-can-i-grow-my-data-science-and-tableau-skills?u=76281980&t=96)** It doesn't matter what it is, any data will do, but if it's a topic that you find interesting, that'll be more fun for you. And just viz. Build something. It doesn't matter if it looks good or it looks bad, it's all good practice. Eventually you'll get that muscle memory. Join some of the community events. Tableau has a huge thriving online community, and there are some great events that happen every single week. Some of these include Makeover Monday, Workout Wednesday, Sports Viz Sunday, Data+Movies or Data+TV events, Back 2 Viz Basics, and Iron Quest. All of these are opportunities to show and grow your Tableau talents. Topics like Makeover Monday every week will give you a dataset and an original visualization, and your task is to make it better. This is a great way to flex your creative muscles, because you're using data that you don't see on your day-to-day jobs, for example. Workout Wednesday is a more tricky thing where you are given a dashboard you have to recreate, and that might need some advanced techniques. Sports Viz Sunday is all about sports. If you love sports, get involved in that one. Back 2 Viz Basics, instead of looking at the advanced chart types, looks at dashboards that are actually practical that you can use in your day-to-day job. The important thing to note is that all of these are public. They all happen on the social media, and people publicize all of their work. Post it to Tableau Public,
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-tableau-for-data-scientists/how-can-i-grow-my-data-science-and-tableau-skills?u=76281980&t=192)** get people to look at your work, critique it, and learn. Tableau Public is one of the best learning platforms out there, because you can see what other people have created and published. In 99% of cases, you can download their visualization and reverse engineer it to find out how they built it. And if you want to know more information about how somebody built their visualization, just ask them. Everybody wants to help everybody else. Everybody started as a beginner one day. Join the Tableau DataFam, the Tableau community. There's a number of different ways you can do that. There is the Tableau Community Forum, which you can get to through the Tableau webpage. This is a great place to ask and answer questions. There is a huge, thriving community. If you get stuck with a technical question or creative question, simply post it on here, and within minutes you'll get a response from other users just trying to help. The Tableau Data community is very active on all social media, so look out for the hashtag Tableau and DataFam. Remember, this course is just the first step on a journey. It's not the end point, but rather the start of it. Grow, viz, and get better. But most of all, enjoy your time with Tableau.
